Divine Decree
intermediate
7 min
Refuting the Coercion Argument
Nasafi refutes Abu Hashim's coercion argument and exposes its Jabriyya consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi position refutes Abu Hashim's claim that human actions are coerced, and how that leads to the Jabriyya doctrine of punishment without act.

Core Idea
If human actions are coerced, punishment for sin is meaningless — that is the true jabr.

This passage tackles a key objection from Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, who argued that since the servant cannot escape God's creation of his actions, all human acts are coerced. Nasafi exposes the logical consequences of this view, showing that it leads to punishment without any act, which is precisely the doctrine of the Jabriyya. He then critiques Abu Hashim's interpretation of divine compulsion, revealing an inconsistency: if God can compel faith by threat, He would also be compelled to be just — which undermines His absolute freedom.

Guided Path
1
Understand Abu Hashim's argument
Read how Abu Hashim claims the servant is compelled because he cannot exit from creation. Notice his reasoning about movement and its opposite.
This is the foundation of his coercion view.
2
See the refutation using the prayer example
Nasafi objects: if the servant can do neither action nor abandonment, then passing prayer time without acting would mean no punishment. But Abu Hashim still claims punishment, which is impossible without an act.
This exposes the flaw in assuming no act can be done.
3
Recognize the identification with Jabriyya
Nasafi explicitly states that punishing without an act is exactly the doctrine of the Jabriyya. This refutes the claim that the Maturidi view is jabr.
The true jabr is this very position.
4
Examine the critique of divine compulsion
Nasafi shows that Abu Hashim's interpretation of God compelling faith by threat leads to God being compelled to be just and truthful, which contradicts divine freedom and lordship.
This highlights the internal contradiction in the opponent's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعورض أبو هاشم في قوله : إن العبد لما لم يمكنه الخروج عن مخلوق الله تعالى كان مضطرًا إذ العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن الحركة وضدها، فكان مكرها على أحدهما، فزعم أنه يمكنه الخروج عنهما، وزعم أن القادر السليم الآلة التام القدرة يجوز خلوه عن الفعل وتركه فكان متمكنا من الخروج عنهما، فلم يكن مكرهًا على أحدهما فارتكب المحال بذلك، ولزمه أن وقت صلاة لو انقضى وهو لم يفعل شيئًا لا فعلها ولا تركها أن لا يعاقب، لأنه لم يفعل فعلاً قبيحا ومن المحال أن المأمور بالصلاة يمضي على وقت الصلاة وهو لم يصلها ولا يستحق العقاب فزعم أنه يعاقب، قيل: على ماذا يعاقب ولم يفعل فعلاً؟ فقال: يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا عين مذهب الجبرية: أن العبد يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا أربى عليهم وقال : أن هذا يجب على الله تعالى تعذيبه وجوبًا لو امتنع عن ذلك إذا خرج العبد من الدنيا قبل التوبة لصار سفيها وزالت ربوبيته، وقد رددنا هذا عليه في مسألة الاستطاعة، وأعدنا ذكر هذه المسألة هاهنا ليعرف العاقل مذهبهم الذي عليه حذاقهم في زماننا هذا، ويعلم أن نسبة هؤلاء خصومهم إلى الجبر أنهم يثبتون للعبد الفعل ويعرفون الاختيار ونفي الاضطرار بالضرورة مع أن مع هذه مقالتهم وقاحة عظيمة وظلم ظاهر، ثم من مذهبه في تفسير مشيئة الجبر وأن الله تعالى قادر أن يجبر العبد على الإيمان بأن يخلق له علمًا يعلم بأنه لو لم يؤمن يعذب عذابًا أليما فيصير بذلك العلم مجبوراً على الإيمان، ثم هو يزعم أن الله تعالى قادر على الظلم والكذب ولو ظلم أو كذب لاستحق الذنب وزالت ربوبيته، فصار على تقدير قوله مجبوراً على العدل والصدق؛ إذ لو لم يفعله للحقه ضرر عظيم إذ لا ضرر أعظم من زوال الألوهية وبطلان الربوبية، فكان هذا الرجل قائلاً
And Abu Hashim was objected to in his statement: Since the servant cannot exit from the creation of God Almighty, he is compelled, because the servant cannot exit from movement and its opposite, so he is coerced into one of them. He claimed that he can exit from both, and he claimed that the capable, sound of limb, perfect in power, it is permissible for him to be devoid of action and its abandonment, so he is able to exit from both, so he is not coerced into either. Thus, he committed the impossible by that. And it follows from him that if the time of prayer passed while he did nothing, neither performing nor abandoning it, that he should not be punished, because he did not perform an evil act. And it is impossible that the one commanded to pray passes the prayer time without praying and is not deserving of punishment. So he claimed that he is punished. It was said: For what is he punished if he did no act? He said: He is punished not for an act that occurred from him. This is precisely the doctrine of the Jabriyya: that the servant is punished not for an act that occurred from him. And this is even more excessive than them. And he said: This obligates God to punish him necessarily; if He refrained from that when the servant left the world without repentance, He would become foolish and His lordship would cease. And we have already refuted this against him in the issue of capacity. And we have repeated this issue here so that the intelligent person may know their doctrine which their experts hold in our time, and know that the attribution of these opponents to jabr is that they affirm the act for the servant and acknowledge choice and negation of compulsion by necessity, while with their statement there is great insolence and manifest injustice. Then from his doctrine in the interpretation of the will of jabr: that God Almighty is able to compel the servant to faith by creating for him knowledge that he knows that if he does not believe he will be punished with a painful punishment, so by that knowledge he becomes compelled to faith. Then he claims that God is able to do injustice and lying, and if He did injustice or lie, He would deserve sin and His lordship would cease. So according to his estimation, He becomes compelled to justice and truth; for if He did not do them, great harm would befall Him, since there is no greater harm than the removal of divinity and the nullification of lordship. So this man was saying.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage refutes Abu Hashim's argument that human actions are coerced. The objection is: since the servant cannot avoid God's creation of his actions, he is compelled. The response uses the example of movement and its opposite: if the servant could not exit from both, he would be forced to one, but Abu Hashim claimed he could exit from both by being able to do neither (i.e., be free of both action and abandonment), which is impossible. The author then draws a consequence: if a person neither prays nor abandons prayer during the prayer time, Abu Hashim would have no basis to punish him (since no act occurred). But Abu Hashim claims he is punished, which is exactly the doctrine of the Jabriyya (punishment without an act). The author notes that this view obligates God to punish necessarily, or else He would become foolish—an absurd implication. The author then critiques Abu Hashim's interpretation of jabr: God can compel faith by creating knowledge of punishment, yet He also claims God can do injustice. But if God were to do injustice, He would lose lordship, so by this reasoning God is compelled to be just and truthful, which contradicts His omnipotence and divine freedom. The passage exposes the inconsistencies of the opponent's position.

Key Terms
مضطر (compelled)
جبر (compulsion)
اختيار (choice)
قدرة (power)
مشيئة (will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Maturidi/Ash'ari view on human action is equivalent to Jabr (compulsion) as held by the Jahmiyya.
Correction: Nasafi shows that true Jabr is the doctrine that punishes without an act; Maturidis affirm choice and accountability.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the consequence of Abu Hashim's view on punishment without an act?
A) It leads to the denial of divine attributes.
B) It is exactly the doctrine of the Jabriyya (punishment without an act).
C) It affirms human freedom.
D) It necessitates that God is unjust.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the true Jabr is not affirming choice but punishing without an act, refuting Abu Hashim's coercion argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action you take is directly compelled by God, would you feel personally responsible for your sins? Or would you blame God? Read how Nasafi exposes the absurdity of that position. Now ask yourself: Have you ever excused a sin by thinking 'God made me do it'? How does this lesson hold you accountable for that excuse?
?
Abu Hashim argued that God can compel faith by creating fear of punishment. But Nasafi shows that this leads to God being compelled to be just. Consider: Is your own faith motivated by fear of punishment or by love? If fear were removed, would your faith remain? Be honest—what does your answer reveal about your heart?
?
Nasafi says the intelligent person should know the true doctrine of the opponents. Are you willing to be that intelligent person who seeks truth no matter how uncomfortable? Will you commit to studying these issues until you can defend the correct understanding of divine decree with clarity and conviction—not just in your mind, but in your actions?

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
The Qadari's Absurd Consequence: Punishment Without an Act
The author refutes a Qadari claim about coercion and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qadari argument about avoiding both acting and leaving leads logically to punishing without an act, which contradicts the Qadari's own position and aligns with Jabrī determinism.

Core Idea
Punishing without an act reveals the Qadari's own Jabriyya.

The author refutes a Qadari claim by exposing its inner contradiction. The opponent argues that a capable person can refrain from both doing and leaving an act, thus escaping coercion. However, the author shows that this leads to a scenario where a person who does nothing is punished for no act—a hallmark of Jabrī doctrine. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of logical deduction.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that a capable person can avoid both acting and leaving. Find this claim and understand its basis.
3
Expose the absurd consequence
The author draws the consequence: if prayer time passes and the person does nothing, no ugly act occurs, yet the opponent says he is punished. Ask yourself: what is the punishment for?
This leads to the Jabriyya position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكرها على أحدهما، فزعم أنه يمكنه الخروج عنهما، وزعم أن القادر السليم الآلة التام القدرة يجوز خلوه عن الفعل وتركه فكان متمكنا من الخروج عنهما، فلم يكن مكرهًا على أحدهما فارتكب المحال بذلك، ولزمه أن وقت صلاة لو انقضى وهو لم يفعل شيئًا لا فعلها ولا تركها أن لا يعاقب، لأنه لم يفعل فعلاً قبيحا ومن المحال أن المأمور بالصلاة يمضي على وقت الصلاة وهو لم يصلها ولا يستحق العقاب فزعم أنه يعاقب، قيل: على ماذا يعاقب ولم يفعل فعلاً؟ فقال: يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا عين مذهب الجبرية: أن العبد يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا أربى عليهم وقال: أن هذا يجب على الله تعالى تعذيبه وجوبًا لو امتنع عن ذلك إذا خرج العبد من الدنيا قبل التوبة لصار سفيها وزالت ربوبيته، وقد رددنا هذا عليه في مسألة الاستطاعة، وأعدنا ذكر هذه المسألة هاهنا ليعرف العاقل مذهبهم الذي عليه حذاقهم في زماننا هذا، ويعلم أن نسبة هؤلاء خصومهم إلى الجبر أنهم يثبتون للعبد الفعل ويعرفون الاختيار ونفي الاضطرار بالضرورة مع أن هذه مقالتهم وقاحة عظيمة وظلم ظاهر، ثم من مذهبه في تفسير مشيئة الجبر وأن الله تعالى قادر أن يجبر العبد على الإيمان بأن يخلق له علمًا يعلم بأنه لو لم يؤمن يعذب عذابًا أليما فيصير بذلك العلم مجبوراً على الإيمان، ثم هو يزعم أن الله تعالى قادر على الظلم والكذب ولو ظلم أو كذب لاستحق الذنب وزالت ربوبيته، فصار على تقدير قوله مجبوراً على العدل والصدق؛ إذ لو لم يفعله للحقه ضرر عظيم إذ لا ضرر أعظم من زوال الألوهية وبطلان الربوبية، فكان هذا الرجل قائلاً بأنه تعالى مجبور، وقال في العباد بما هو خير محض، فلا أدرى لم ينسب غيره إلى القول بالجبر؟! والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن مثل هذه المناقضات، والتحكم على الله تعالى وعلى خلقه بالباطل والاحتجاج على الخصوم بما لا حجة فيه، والله الموفق.
Coerced into one of them. He claimed that he can avoid both. He claimed that the capable person, sound of limbs, with complete power, can be free from the act and its abandonment, so he is able to avoid both; thus he is not coerced into one of them. He thereby committed impossibility. And it necessarily follows that if the time of prayer passed while he did nothing—neither performing it nor leaving it—he should not be punished, because he did no ugly act. And it is impossible that the one commanded to pray passes the time of prayer without praying and does not deserve punishment. But he claimed that he is punished. It was said: 'For what is he punished, since he did no act?' He said: 'He is punished not for an act that came from him.' This is exactly the doctrine of the Jabriyya: that the slave is punished not for an act that came from him. And this is worse than them. And he said: 'It is necessarily incumbent upon Allah to torment him; if He refrained from that when the slave leaves the world before repentance, He would become foolish and His lordship would vanish.' And we have refuted this against him in the question of capacity (al-istiṭāʿa). We have mentioned this question again here so that the intelligent person may know their doctrine, which their shrewd ones follow in our time, and so that he may know that the attribution of these opponents of theirs to jabr is that they affirm the act for the slave and know by necessity choice and denial of compulsion, even though this statement of theirs is great insolence and manifest injustice. Then, from his doctrine in interpreting the will of jabr: that Allah is able to compel the slave to faith by creating for him knowledge that he knows that if he does not believe, he will be tortured with a painful torment, so by that knowledge he becomes compelled to faith. Then he claims that Allah is able to do injustice and lying; and if He did injustice or lied, He would deserve sin and His lordship would vanish. Thus, according to his estimation, He becomes compelled to justice and truthfulness, because if He did not do them, a great harm would befall Him—and there is no harm greater than the loss of divinity and the nullification of lordship. So this man was saying that He is compelled, and he said concerning the servants that it is pure good. So I do not know why he attributes others to the saying of jabr! Praise be to Allah, who preserved us from such contradictions, from judging Allah and His creation falsely, and from arguing against opponents with no proof. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Qadari position regarding coercion and punishment. The author begins by summarizing the opponent's claim: a person is coerced into one of two alternatives, but the opponent argues that a capable person can avoid both by refraining from both acting and leaving, so he is not coerced. The author then exposes the absurd consequence: if the time for prayer expires and the person does nothing—neither prays nor intentionally leaves—then according to the opponent's logic, he should not be punished for any ugly act. Yet the opponent claims he is punished. When asked for what, the opponent says he is punished not for any act. The author declares this to be the very doctrine of the Jabriyya (determinists), who punish without an act. The author then presents another aspect: the opponent claims that Allah must necessarily punish the slave if he dies without repentance, otherwise Allah would become foolish (safīh) and His lordship would vanish. The author notes that this was already refuted in the discussion on capacity (al-istiṭāʿa). The purpose of revisiting it is to expose the opponent's true doctrine and show that while they accuse others of jabr (compulsion), they themselves affirm that the slave has action and choice, yet their own statements lead to absurdities. Next, the author examines the opponent's view on the divine will: Allah can compel faith by creating knowledge of painful punishment, making the person compelled. Yet the opponent also claims Allah can do injustice and lie, which would cause loss of divinity. Thus, to preserve His divinity, Allah is compelled to be just and truthful. The opponent therefore ends up saying that Allah is compelled and that human beings are pure good. The author expresses astonishment that such a person attributes jabr to others. The passage concludes with praise to Allah for preserving the author from such contradictions and from arguing without proof.

Key Terms
جبرية
قادر سليم الآلة
فعل
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qadari position preserves human responsibility and avoids determinism.
Correction: The author shows that the Qadari's own argument leads to punishing without an act, which is a form of Jabrī determinism.

Checkpoint
According to the refuted Qadari argument, if a person does not pray during the prayer time, what is the basis for punishment?
A) The act of not praying
B) The act of leaving prayer
C) Not for any act from him
D) The intention to leave

Key Takeaway
The Qadari's attempt to avoid coercion leads to the absurdity of punishing without an act, revealing that their own logic implies Jabrī determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you excused your failure to pray by saying you 'had no choice' or that it 'wasn't the right time'? How does this passage expose such reasoning as an attempt to avoid responsibility—and actually align you with the Jabriyya you may reject?
?
The Qadari claimed that doing nothing makes one free from both act and abandonment. Think of a duty you know you must fulfill but keep postponing by doing 'nothing.' Are you, in that inaction, actually pretending to be neutral? What would it mean to truly own your inaction as a sin of abandonment?
?
This lesson shows that punishment without an act is absurd and Jabrite. But do you sometimes live as if your sins don't 'count' because you didn't actively choose them? Examine your heart: where have you relied on the excuse that 'it just happened'—and how can you repent by affirming your act and seeking forgiveness?

Divine Decree

Contradictions in the Qadari View: Allah Compelled to Justice
The author refutes a Qadari claim about Allah's compulsion and exposes the opponent's own Jabri position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qadari opponent's views on Allah's power and justice lead to the conclusion that Allah is compelled, and that he accuses others of Jabr while holding a contradictory position.

Core Idea
The Qadari ends up saying Allah is compelled, not humans.

The author continues the refutation by examining the opponent's statements about divine punishment and compulsion. The opponent claims Allah must punish or become foolish, and that Allah can compel faith by creating knowledge. However, the opponent also says Allah can do injustice, which would destroy His lordship. This forces the opponent to admit that Allah is compelled to be just, contradicting his own rejection of Jabr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the different claims the opponent makes.
This passage is longer; read slowly.
2
Analyze the opponent's claims about Allah
Identify the three key claims: (1) Allah must punish or become foolish, (2) Allah can compel by knowledge, (3) Allah can do injustice. How do they relate?
3
See the contradiction
The author points out that if Allah can do injustice, that would nullify His lordship. Therefore, to preserve His lordship, Allah is compelled to be just. So the opponent's view implies Allah is compelled.
This is ironic because the opponent accuses others of Jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكرها على أحدهما، فزعم أنه يمكنه الخروج عنهما، وزعم أن القادر السليم الآلة التام القدرة يجوز خلوه عن الفعل وتركه فكان متمكنا من الخروج عنهما، فلم يكن مكرهًا على أحدهما فارتكب المحال بذلك، ولزمه أن وقت صلاة لو انقضى وهو لم يفعل شيئًا لا فعلها ولا تركها أن لا يعاقب، لأنه لم يفعل فعلاً قبيحا ومن المحال أن المأمور بالصلاة يمضي على وقت الصلاة وهو لم يصلها ولا يستحق العقاب فزعم أنه يعاقب، قيل: على ماذا يعاقب ولم يفعل فعلاً؟ فقال: يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا عين مذهب الجبرية: أن العبد يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا أربى عليهم وقال: أن هذا يجب على الله تعالى تعذيبه وجوبًا لو امتنع عن ذلك إذا خرج العبد من الدنيا قبل التوبة لصار سفيها وزالت ربوبيته، وقد رددنا هذا عليه في مسألة الاستطاعة، وأعدنا ذكر هذه المسألة هاهنا ليعرف العاقل مذهبهم الذي عليه حذاقهم في زماننا هذا، ويعلم أن نسبة هؤلاء خصومهم إلى الجبر أنهم يثبتون للعبد الفعل ويعرفون الاختيار ونفي الاضطرار بالضرورة مع أن هذه مقالتهم وقاحة عظيمة وظلم ظاهر، ثم من مذهبه في تفسير مشيئة الجبر وأن الله تعالى قادر أن يجبر العبد على الإيمان بأن يخلق له علمًا يعلم بأنه لو لم يؤمن يعذب عذابًا أليما فيصير بذلك العلم مجبوراً على الإيمان، ثم هو يزعم أن الله تعالى قادر على الظلم والكذب ولو ظلم أو كذب لاستحق الذنب وزالت ربوبيته، فصار على تقدير قوله مجبوراً على العدل والصدق؛ إذ لو لم يفعله للحقه ضرر عظيم إذ لا ضرر أعظم من زوال الألوهية وبطلان الربوبية، فكان هذا الرجل قائلاً بأنه تعالى مجبور، وقال في العباد بما هو خير محض، فلا أدرى لم ينسب غيره إلى القول بالجبر؟! والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن مثل هذه المناقضات، والتحكم على الله تعالى وعلى خلقه بالباطل والاحتجاج على الخصوم بما لا حجة فيه، والله الموفق.

He thereby committed impossibility. Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Qadari position regarding coercion and punishment. The author begins by summarizing the opponent's claim: a person is coerced into one of two alternatives, but the opponent argues that a capable person can avoid both by refraining from both acting and leaving, so he is not coerced. The author then exposes the absurd consequence: if the time for prayer expires and the person does nothing—neither prays nor intentionally leaves—then according to the opponent's logic, he should not be punished for any ugly act. Yet the opponent claims he is punished. When asked for what, the opponent says he is punished not for any act. The author declares this to be the very doctrine of the Jabriyya (determinists), who punish without an act. The author then presents another aspect: the opponent claims that Allah must necessarily punish the slave if he dies without repentance, otherwise Allah would become foolish (safīh) and His lordship would vanish. The author notes that this was already refuted in the discussion on capacity (al-istiṭāʿa). The purpose of revisiting it is to expose the opponent's true doctrine and show that while they accuse others of jabr (compulsion), they themselves affirm that the slave has action and choice, yet their own statements lead to absurdities. Next, the author examines the opponent's view on the divine will: Allah can compel faith by creating knowledge of painful punishment, making the person compelled. Yet the opponent also claims Allah can do injustice and lie, which would cause loss of divinity. Thus, to preserve His divinity, Allah is compelled to be just and truthful. The opponent therefore ends up saying that Allah is compelled and that human beings are pure good. The author expresses astonishment that such a person attributes jabr to others. The passage concludes with praise to Allah for preserving the author from such contradictions and from arguing without proof.

Key Terms
جبر
مشيئة
عدل
صدق
ربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume the Qadari consistently defend human free will without contradictions.
Correction: The author shows that the Qadari's arguments lead to attributing compulsion to Allah and logical inconsistencies.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's view, what would happen if Allah refrained from punishing an unrepentant slave?
A) Allah would be merciful
B) Allah would become foolish and lose lordship
C) The slave would be forgiven
D) Justice would be served

Key Takeaway
The Qadari opposing Jabr ends up asserting that Allah is compelled, revealing the internal inconsistency of their position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accused someone of a fault that you yourself unknowingly commit? The Qadari accused others of Jabr while their own logic forced Allah into compulsion. Where in your life might you be quick to judge others without examining your own inconsistencies? What will you do today to change that?
?
This lesson shows that even a wrong belief about Allah can lead to self-contradiction and arrogance. Do you hold any beliefs about Allah that you have not fully examined from the revelation? Take a moment to ask Allah to purify your understanding and protect you from contradicting His attributes.
?
The opponent argued that Allah would become 'foolish' if He didn't punish a certain way. Have you ever spoken about Allah in a way that assumes you know what He must do? Repent right now for any such presumption and ask Allah to grant you humility before His infinite wisdom.

The Slain Person's Term: Mu'tazilite Views and Maturidi Proof
Nasafi presents Mu'tazilite views and provides his proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing Mu'tazilite positions on the slain person's appointed term and the Maturidi stance supported by the Qur'an and hadith.

Core Idea
The slain person's term is fixed; no alternative term exists.

This passage explores a key qadar (divine decree) issue: does a slain person have a different term than his time of death? The Mu'tazilites disagreed among themselves, and the author weighs in with a definitive proof from the Qur'an and hadith. Let's examine these views and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the different positions mentioned.
Note that the author first mentions the Mu'tazilite distinction between death and killing.
2
Identify the three Mu'tazilite views
Look for the views of Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Jubba'i, and the majority (al-baqun). Note how they differ on whether the slain person has a single term or a different one.
Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Jubba'i held that the term is the same; the majority said it is cut off.
3
Analyze the author's proof
The author cites a Qur'anic verse (7:34) and a hadith about the angel writing the term. Understand how these support his position that the term is fixed and cannot be changed.
The verse emphasizes that when the term comes, no delay or advance is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيره من المعتزلة في المقتول معنيان: أحدهما وهو الموت، والآخر من العبد وهو القتل، وما ذكرنا من دلائل إبطال القول بالتولد يوجب بطلان هذا كله، ثم يتصل بهذا أن المقتول ميت بأجله وهذا هو أجله لا أجل له سواه، وكذا قال أبو الهذيل من جملة المعتزلة حتى قال: لو لم يقتل لمات بأجله في وقت قتله قال: والمدة التي لم يعش إليها لم تكن أجلاً ولا من عمره، وعندنا ليس الأمر كذلك بل يقتل لا محالة، وكذا قال الجبائي أن لا أجل له إلا هذا وقال الباقون المقتول مقطوع عليه أجله كذا ذكروا، ولنا قوله: ﴿ فَإِذَا جَاءَ أَجَلُهُمْ لَا يَسْتَأْخِرُونَ سَاعَةً وَلَا يَسْتَقْدِمُونَ ﴾ والحديث المعروف أن عند تصوير العبد في بطن أمه يأمر الله ملكا فيكتب على جبهته رزقه وأجله وسعادته وشقاوته.
Others among the Mu'tazilites [say] concerning the slain person there are two meanings: one of them is death, and the other from the servant is killing. And what we have mentioned of the proofs for nullifying the saying of tawallud (generation) necessitates the invalidity of all of this. Then connected to this is that the slain person dies by his appointed term (ajal) and this is his appointed term; he has no appointed term other than it. Likewise said Abu al-Hudhayl among the group of the Mu'tazilites, such that he said: If he were not killed, he would have died by his appointed term at the time of his killing. He said: And the period which he did not live to reach was not an appointed term nor from his lifespan. And with us it is not like that; rather, he is killed inevitably. Likewise said al-Jubba'i: He has no appointed term except this. And the rest said: The slain person is cut off from his appointed term. Thus they mentioned. And for us is His saying: {And when their appointed term comes, they will not delay an hour nor will they advance [it]} and the well-known hadith that when the servant is formed in his mother's belly, Allah commands an angel and he writes on his forehead his provision, his appointed term, his felicity, and his misery.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the different Mu'tazilite views on the term of the slain person and then provides the author's own position supported by Qur'anic verse and hadith. First, the author notes that the Mu'tazilites differentiate two aspects in the slain person: death (from God) and killing (from the servant). He then asserts that his earlier proofs against the theory of tawallud invalidate this distinction. The core issue is whether the slain person dies by a single appointed term. Abu al-Hudhayl held that if the person were not killed, he would have died at the same moment by his appointed term; the time he did not live was never part of his lifespan. Al-Jubba'i likewise said there is only that term, no other. The majority said the slain person's term is cut off (i.e., he would have lived longer if not killed). The author's position is that the slain person is inevitably killed by his appointed term; there is no other. His proof is the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-A'raf (7:34) stating that when their appointed term comes, no delay or advance is possible, implying it is fixed. Additionally, the well-known hadith describes that before birth, an angel writes the person's provision, term, felicity, and misery on his forehead, confirming that the term is predetermined.

الأجل
القتل

أبو الهذيل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites agree that the slain person has a different appointed term than the time of death.
Correction: The passage shows internal disagreement: Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Jubba'i held that the term is the same, while the majority said it is cut off.

Checkpoint

B) The verse from Surah al-Baqarah about death.
C) The verse about the angel writing the term.
D) The verse on tawallud.

Key Takeaway
The slain person's term is predetermined and cannot be changed; this is supported by the Qur'an and hadith.

?
If your appointed term is set and cannot be delayed or advanced even by an hour, how should that change the way you spend this very day? Are you living with urgency for what matters most, or do you assume you have more time?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Interpretation of the Lifespan Verse
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite proof from the verse 'وما يعمر من معمر' with a linguistic reinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's linguistic refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that the Qur'anic verse about lifespan proves the possibility of shortening a person's term.

Core Idea
The verse compares two similar persons, not a single lifespan being changed.

This lesson examines Nasafi's argument against the Mu'tazilite interpretation of a key Qur'anic verse on lifespan. By reanalyzing the pronoun and using a linguistic analogy, Nasafi shows that the verse does not support the idea of a shortened term. Let's see how he does it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the content.
Notice the verse and Nasafi's reinterpretation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite proof
Understand what the Mu'tazilites used this verse to argue: that a lifespan can be decreased, implying the slain person's term is cut short.
This is the view Nasafi refutes.
3
Understand Nasafi's reinterpretation
Nasafi reinterprets the pronoun 'his' in 'عمره' as referring to a similar person, not the same individual. He explains that the verse compares two different persons' lifespans.
The key is the linguistic principle of 'من يماثله في الاسم'.
4
Study the analogy
Nasafi gives the example of 'a dirham and half of it' meaning half of another similar dirham. This clarifies that the decrease is relative to another similar being.
This analogy anchors the linguistic point.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this interpretation preserves the doctrine of fixed terms and refutes the Mu'tazilite argument about the slain person's term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا يُعَمَّرُ مِن مُعَمَّرٍ وَلَا يُنقَصُ مِنْ عُمُرِهِ إِلَّا فِي كِتَابٍ ﴾ وتقدير الآية عندنا - والله أعلم - وما يعمر من معمر، ولا ينقص من عمر معمر آخر سواه أي يعطي لهذا الثاني من العمر ما يكون ناقصا بمقابلة عمر الأول والهاء راجع إلى من يماثله في الاسم لا إلى عين المذكور كما يقال: هذا درهم ونصفه أي نصف درهم آخر مماثل للأول في الاسم كذا ذكر كبار أهل اللغة منهم الفراء.
And their clinging to His saying, the Exalted: {And no one is given long life, nor is his lifespan diminished, except in a book.} And the estimation of the verse with us - and God knows best - is: 'And no one is given long life, nor is diminished from the lifespan of another similar to him' - i.e., He gives to this second from the lifespan what is decreased in comparison to the lifespan of the first, and the pronoun refers to one similar to him in name, not to the same mentioned person, just as it is said: 'This is a dirham and half of it' meaning half of another dirham similar to the first in name. Thus mentioned the major scholars of language, among them al-Farra'.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite use of the Qur'anic verse 'وما يعمر من معمر ولا ينقص من عمره إلا في كتاب' (Surah Fatir 35:11). The Mu'tazilites argued that this verse supports the possibility of a lifespan being shortened, which they used to claim that the slain person's term could be cut short. The author provides a reinterpretation: the verse does not refer to one person's lifespan being increased or decreased, but rather compares two persons. The meaning is that no person is given a long life, and likewise no other similar person's lifespan is decreased; the decrease refers to the second person's lifespan relative to the first. The pronoun 'his' (الهاء) in 'عمره' refers to a similar person, not the same individual. He gives an analogy: saying 'this is a dirham and half of it' means half of another dirham similar to the first. This linguistic analysis is supported by major linguists like al-Farra'. Thus, the verse does not prove any change in a single person's term, but rather affirms that each person's term is fixed in the Book.

Key Terms
معمر
ينقص من عمره
الفراء
تفسير الآية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse speaks of changing one person's lifespan, meaning a single individual's term can be increased or decreased.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the verse compares two similar persons; the pronoun 'his' refers to a different person, not the same individual. The decrease is relative to another person's lifespan.

Checkpoint
How does the author reinterpret the pronoun 'his' in 'ولا ينقص من عمره'?
A) It refers to the same person whose life is shortened.
B) It refers to a similar person, not the same individual.
C) It refers to the angel writing the term.
D) It refers to the killer.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's reinterpretation of the pronoun preserves the doctrine of fixed terms and refutes the Mu'tazilite argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Qisas Objection and the Role of Allah's Custom
Nasafi answers the Mu'tazilite qisas objection using Allah's custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that qisas is obligatory due to the violation of a prohibition and Allah's custom of creating death after such acts, not because the victim's term was shortened.

Core Idea
Qisas is about violating a prohibition, not altering the appointed term.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if everyone dies by their appointed term, then qisas and compensation become meaningless. Nasafi refutes this by distinguishing between the cause of death and the basis for legal obligation. This lesson unpacks his response, focusing on the concept of Allah's custom ('adah) and the role of prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite objection
Start by reading the objection carefully. The Mu'tazilites claim that if death is always by a fixed term, then killing someone should not incur qisas or compensation, since the term was already set.
Notice the example of slaughtering a sheep: if it was destined to die, it would be a good act.
2
Identify Nasafi's key premise
Nasafi answers that the obligation arises from violating a prohibition (not killing) combined with Allah's custom of creating death following such prohibited acts. This is the core of his argument.
He references the earlier discussion on tawallud (generated acts).
3
Understand the verification argument
Nasafi then argues that even according to the Mu'tazilites, the appointed term is possible but not certain. Legal obligations like qisas cannot be based on doubt; since qisas is obligatory, it confirms that the death is indeed the appointed term, but the obligation is due to the prohibition and custom.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters for the qadar debate: it maintains that Allah's decree is fixed while also upholding moral accountability for human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقولون: لو كان مات بأجله لم يجب القصاص ولا وجب الضمان إذا كان شاه فذبحها لأنه أحسن إليه، والجواب: أن وجوب القصاص والضمان لارتكابه النهي باكتسابه الفعل الذي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بتخليقه الموت عقبه لا على ما وجد في المحل من المعنى المنافي للحياة على ما مر في مسألة التولد. يحققه أن عندهم كان من الجائز أن يكون هذا أجله إن لم يكن واجبًا، والضمان لا يجب مع الشك ولا القصاص، ومع وجب هاهنا دل أنه إنما وجب ذلك لما ذكرنا ثم الحقيقة أنه لا خلاف في هذه المسألة لأنهم يقولون: إن معلوم الله تعالى في انتهاء عمره غير هذا إذ لا يظن هذا بمن خالف هشام بن عمرو من تجويز كون المعدوم معلومًا، وإنما يقولون: إن الله تعالى يعلم أنه يقتل لا محالة ويعلم أنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى وقت كذا على الأصل أنه تعالى يعلم ما يكون، ويعلم أن ما لا يكون لو كان كيف كان يكون على ما قال في حق الكفار: ﴿ وَلَوْ رُدُّوا لَعَادُوا لِمَا نُهُوا عَنْهُ ﴾ وإن كان يعلم أنهم لا يردون ونحن ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر كون ذلك مشكوكاً مجهولاً عنده كما يكون ممن يجهل العواقب وهم أيضا يأبون هذا والله الموفق.
And they say: If he had died by his appointed term, then qisas (retaliation) would not be obligatory, nor would compensation be obligatory if he had a sheep and slaughtered it because he did good to it. The answer: The obligation of qisas and compensation is due to his committing the prohibition by acquiring the act that Allah, the Exalted, has customarily caused death subsequently, not due to what was found in the locus of a meaning contrary to life, as passed in the issue of tawallud. It is verified that with them, it was possible that this could be his appointed term if it were not obligatory, and compensation is not obligatory with doubt, nor qisas. And since it is obligatory here, it indicates that it is obligatory only due to what we mentioned. Then the reality is that there is no disagreement in this issue because they say: Allah's knowledge concerning the end of his life is other than this, since this cannot be supposed of those who opposed Hisham ibn 'Amr by allowing the non-existent to be known. Rather, they say: Allah, the Exalted, knows that he will be killed inevitably, and He knows that if he were not killed he would have lived until such a time, on the principle that He, the Exalted, knows what will be, and He knows that what will not be, if it were, how it would be, as He said regarding the disbelievers: {And if they were returned, they would return to what they were forbidden from} even though He knows they will not be returned. And we deny this; we only deny that it is doubtful or unknown to Him, like one who is ignorant of outcomes. And they also reject this. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazilite objection and the author's detailed response, concluding with a clarification that there is no real disagreement on the core issue. The objection: If the slain person died by his appointed term, then qisas (retaliation) and compensation (daman) would not be obligatory; for example, if someone slaughtered a sheep, it would be a good act if the sheep was destined to die at that time. The author answers that qisas and compensation are obligatory because the act violates a prohibition (the command not to kill), and Allah's custom ('adah) is to create death following such prohibited acts, not because the act itself contains a life-denying quality. He explains that the Mu'tazilites themselves consider it possible that this is the appointed term, but since it is not certain (not obligatory), compensation and qisas would not be obligatory if there were doubt; the fact that they are obligatory shows that the term is indeed this one. Then the author clarifies that there is no real disagreement (la khilaf) in this issue because the Mu'tazilites actually agree that Allah knows the future: He knows that the person will be killed, and He also knows what would have happened if he were not killed (i.e., he would have lived longer). This is like the verse about the disbelievers: if they were returned, they would disbelieve again, even though Allah knows they will not be returned. The author insists that the point of contention is not Allah's knowledge, but the claim that the alternative is unknown or doubtful to Allah. Both sides reject that Allah is ignorant. Thus, the real disagreement is not about predestination of the term, but about whether the term is uniquely fixed or if alternatives are unknown. The author's view is that the term is singular and fixed, while the Mu'tazilites' view leads to positing a potential alternative that Allah knows, but that alternative is not His decree for that person.

Key Terms
القصاص
الضمان
العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qisas is about restoring the victim's original term or compensating for a shortened life.
Correction: Nasafi argues qisas is about the violation of prohibition; the term is fixed and unchanged, but the act triggers legal consequences due to Allah's custom.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's answer, why is qisas obligatory?
A) Because the killer shortened the victim's life.
B) Because the killer violated a prohibition and Allah customarily causes death afterward.
C) Because the victim had a right to live longer.
D) Because the killer intended evil.

Key Takeaway
Qisas is not about altering the term but about moral accountability for prohibited acts within Allah's fixed decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever use the belief in divine decree (qadar) to justify your sins, thinking 'it was meant to be'? How does this lesson's focus on the prohibition and Allah's custom demand you take full responsibility for every forbidden act you commit?
?
When someone wrongs you, do you secretly feel that their punishment (qisas) is pointless if it was already decreed? How does Nasafi's argument that punishment is for violating the prohibition—not for altering the decree—shift your perspective on seeking justice or forgiveness?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's custom (adah) is the only reason your actions have consequences, would you live more carefully? What one prohibited action do you need to stop rationalizing, and will you commit today to treat it as a violation of a divine command, not just as a natural mistake?

No Real Disagreement on Allah's Knowledge
Nasafi clarifies the real point of agreement on Allah's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi claims there is no real disagreement with the Mu'tazilites regarding Allah's knowledge of future events, including hypotheticals.

Core Idea
Both sides agree Allah knows all possibilities; the dispute is over whether the alternative is known or fixed.

After answering the qisas objection, Nasafi clarifies that the Mu'tazilites actually agree with him on a key point: Allah knows what will happen and what would have happened if things were different. The real issue is not about Allah's knowledge but about whether the alternative term is unknown or doubted. This lesson examines that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on no disagreement
Read Nasafi's claim that there is no real disagreement. Identify the two groups he contrasts: the Mu'tazilites who oppose Hisham ibn Amr and his own position.
Hisham ibn Amr reportedly claimed that non-existent things can be known, which is relevant to knowing alternatives.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position on Allah's knowledge
The Mu'tazilites affirm that Allah knows the person will be killed inevitably, and also knows that if he were not killed, he would have lived longer. This is analogous to the verse about disbelievers: if returned, they would disbelieve.
3
Identify Nasafi's point of denial
Nasafi says he denies only that the alternative is doubtful or unknown to Allah, not that Allah knows it. Both sides reject ignorance in Allah.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Nasafi concludes that the disagreement is not about Allah's knowledge but about whether the alternative term is part of Allah's decree or merely possible knowledge.
This resolves a seeming theological conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقولون: لو كان مات بأجله لم يجب القصاص ولا وجب الضمان إذا كان شاه فذبحها لأنه أحسن إليه، والجواب: أن وجوب القصاص والضمان لارتكابه النهي باكتسابه الفعل الذي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بتخليقه الموت عقبه لا على ما وجد في المحل من المعنى المنافي للحياة على ما مر في مسألة التولد. يحققه أن عندهم كان من الجائز أن يكون هذا أجله إن لم يكن واجبًا، والضمان لا يجب مع الشك ولا القصاص، ومع وجب هاهنا دل أنه إنما وجب ذلك لما ذكرنا ثم الحقيقة أنه لا خلاف في هذه المسألة لأنهم يقولون: إن معلوم الله تعالى في انتهاء عمره غير هذا إذ لا يظن هذا بمن خالف هشام بن عمرو من تجويز كون المعدوم معلومًا، وإنما يقولون: إن الله تعالى يعلم أنه يقتل لا محالة ويعلم أنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى وقت كذا على الأصل أنه تعالى يعلم ما يكون، ويعلم أن ما لا يكون لو كان كيف كان يكون على ما قال في حق الكفار: ﴿ وَلَوْ رُدُّوا لَعَادُوا لِمَا نُهُوا عَنْهُ ﴾ وإن كان يعلم أنهم لا يردون ونحن ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر كون ذلك مشكوكاً مجهولاً عنده كما يكون ممن يجهل العواقب وهم أيضا يأبون هذا والله الموفق.
And they say: If he had died by his appointed term, then qisas (retaliation) would not be obligatory, nor would compensation be obligatory if he had a sheep and slaughtered it because he did good to it. The answer: The obligation of qisas and compensation is due to his committing the prohibition by acquiring the act that Allah, the Exalted, has customarily caused death subsequently, not due to what was found in the locus of a meaning contrary to life, as passed in the issue of tawallud. It is verified that with them, it was possible that this could be his appointed term if it were not obligatory, and compensation is not obligatory with doubt, nor qisas. And since it is obligatory here, it indicates that it is obligatory only due to what we mentioned. Then the reality is that there is no disagreement in this issue because they say: Allah's knowledge concerning the end of his life is other than this, since this cannot be supposed of those who opposed Hisham ibn 'Amr by allowing the non-existent to be known. Rather, they say: Allah, the Exalted, knows that he will be killed inevitably, and He knows that if he were not killed he would have lived until such a time, on the principle that He, the Exalted, knows what will be, and He knows that what will not be, if it were, how it would be, as He said regarding the disbelievers: {And if they were returned, they would return to what they were forbidden from} even though He knows they will not be returned. And we deny this; we only deny that it is doubtful or unknown to Him, like one who is ignorant of outcomes. And they also reject this. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazilite objection and the author's detailed response, concluding with a clarification that there is no real disagreement on the core issue. The objection: If the slain person died by his appointed term, then qisas (retaliation) and compensation (daman) would not be obligatory; for example, if someone slaughtered a sheep, it would be a good act if the sheep was destined to die at that time. The author answers that qisas and compensation are obligatory because the act violates a prohibition (the command not to kill), and Allah's custom ('adah) is to create death following such prohibited acts, not because the act itself contains a life-denying quality. He explains that the Mu'tazilites themselves consider it possible that this is the appointed term, but since it is not certain (not obligatory), compensation and qisas would not be obligatory if there were doubt; the fact that they are obligatory shows that the term is indeed this one. Then the author clarifies that there is no real disagreement (la khilaf) in this issue because the Mu'tazilites actually agree that Allah knows the future: He knows that the person will be killed, and He also knows what would have happened if he were not killed (i.e., he would have lived longer). This is like the verse about the disbelievers: if they were returned, they would disbelieve again, even though Allah knows they will not be returned. The author insists that the point of contention is not Allah's knowledge, but the claim that the alternative is unknown or doubtful to Allah. Both sides reject that Allah is ignorant. Thus, the real disagreement is not about predestination of the term, but about whether the term is uniquely fixed or if alternatives are unknown. The author's view is that the term is singular and fixed, while the Mu'tazilites' view leads to positing a potential alternative that Allah knows, but that alternative is not His decree for that person.

Key Terms
معلوم الله
المعدوم
الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites deny that Allah knows future events or that He knows hypotheticals.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that the Mu'tazilites affirm Allah's knowledge of both what will happen and what would have happened; the disagreement is about whether the alternative is known or fixed as part of His decree.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say he denies regarding the alternative term?
A) He denies that Allah knows it.
B) He denies that it is doubtful or unknown to Allah.
C) He denies that the Mu'tazilites accept Allah's knowledge.
D) He denies the verse about the disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The real point of agreement is that Allah knows all; the disagreement is only about whether alternatives are deemed unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the fact that Allah knows every possible alternative to your life—including what would have happened if you had not sinned—does that make you feel more accountable or more hopeful? Why? And what does that reaction reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
Nasafi states that both sides agree Allah knows all, but the dispute is over whether the alternative is known or fixed. How does your heart respond to the idea that Allah not only knows what you will do but also what you would have done in any other scenario? Does this increase your awe of Him or create unease? Sit with that feeling and ask yourself what it stems from.
?
If you truly believed that Allah knows every 'what if' of your life, how would that change the way you think about past regrets? Would you still dwell on 'if only I had done that'? Or would you submit more completely to His decree? What is holding you back from that submission?

The Maturidi Definition of Provision (Rizq)
Nasafi defines rizq and refutes Mu'tazila view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of rizq as encompassing both halal and haram sustenance, and how it refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Rizq includes all sustenance, even the forbidden; it is not limited to lawful ownership.

This passage tackles a classic theological debate over the nature of provision. Abu al-Muin al-Nasafi begins by stating the Maturidi position, then defines rizq linguistically, and finally uses a Quranic verse to argue that rizq must include nourishment, not just ownership. This lesson will guide you through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Don’t worry about details yet.
Note the contrast between 'with us' (the Maturidis) and the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where the author states the Maturidi view and where he quotes the Mu'tazila. What are the key differences?
The Maturidis deny that a person can fail to eat his own provision.
3
Examine the linguistic definition
Look at how the author defines 'rizq' in language: measured sustenance (qut muqaddar). Then note the two broader uses: ownership and nourishment.
The example of the 'murtaziq' (stipend receiver) shows the core meaning of measured provision.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Read the verse (Quran 11:6) and the author's interpretation. Why does he prefer the nourishment interpretation for animals?
Animals have no legal ownership, so 'rizq' in that verse must mean what nourishes them.
5
Understand the resolution
The author concludes that the disagreement with the Mu'tazila is merely verbal. How does the two-fold meaning of rizq allow this?
If rizq means ownership, haram is not rizq; if it means nourishment, haram is included. Both sides can agree on facts but label differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يماثل هذه المسألة مسألة الأرزاق ثم عندنا كل يستوفى رزق نفسه ويأكله حلالاً كان أو حرامًا ولا يتصور أن لا يأكل إنسان ما جعل رزقا له ولا أن يأكل غيره رزقه أو يأكل هو رزق غيره، وقالت المعتزلة: من الجائز أن لا يأكل رزقه ويأكل رزق غيره ويأكل رزقه غيره، والرزق في اللغة اسم للقوت المقدر، ولهذا يسمى من يجري عليه السلطان من المرابطين في كل شهر مقدار مرتزقة، وقد يستعمل ويراد به الملك المطلق، وقد يستعمل ويراد به الغذاء. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا ﴾ والدواب لا ملك لها بالأسباب المشروعة، فكان المراد منه ما يحصل لها به الاغتذاء فإن حمل على الملك لم يكن الحرام رزقا والإنسان قد يأكل رزق غيره أي ملكه ويأكل غيره رزقه أي ملكه، وإن حمل على الغذاء كان الحرام رزقًا لأن الله تعالى يغذينا أي يخلق التغذي والنمو في أبداننا، وهو تعالى يخلقه متفرد لا صنع للعبد فيه، ومن المحال إطلاق اسم الرزق على الملك خاصة دون الغذاء بل هو يقع عليها جميعًا ثم يقبح أن يقال فلان عاش مائة سنة لم يأكل رزق الله تعالى، والشيخ أبو الحسن الرستغفني وأبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني مما حققا الخلاف في مسألة الآجال والأرزاق، وقالا : فيهما خلاف من حيث العبارة لا غير، وهو الصواب.
And similar to this issue is the issue of provisions. Then with us, every person consumes his own provision and eats it, whether it is lawful or forbidden, and it is not conceivable that a person does not eat what has been made his provision, nor that another eats his provision, nor that he eats the provision of another. The Mu'tazila said: It is possible that he does not eat his provision and eats the provision of another, and that another eats his provision. And 'rizq' in the language is a name for measured sustenance; for this reason, the person upon whom the ruler runs from the frontier guards each month is called a 'murtaziq' (one who receives a stipend). And it may be used with the intended meaning of absolute ownership, and it may be used with the intended meaning of nourishment. Allah the Exalted said: 'And there is no creature on earth except that upon Allah is its provision.' And creatures have no ownership by legal means, so the intended meaning was that which produces nourishment for them. For if it is interpreted as ownership, then the forbidden is not provision, and a person may eat the provision of another, i.e., his ownership, and another may eat his provision, i.e., his ownership. And if it is interpreted as nourishment, then the forbidden is provision, because Allah the Exalted nourishes us, i.e., He creates the process of feeding and growth in our bodies, and He the Exalted creates it uniquely with no act of the servant in it. And it is impossible to apply the name of 'rizq' to ownership exclusively without nourishment; rather, it applies to both. Then it is repugnant to say: So-and-so lived a hundred years without eating the provision of Allah the Exalted. And Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini both verified the disagreement on the issue of terms and provisions, and said: The difference in both is merely verbal, and this is correct.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the nature of provision (rizq), defending the Maturidi view against the Mu'tazila. The author begins by linking this issue to a previous discussion. He then states the Maturidi position: every creature consumes its own provision, whether halal or haram; it is inconceivable that a person fails to eat his provision or that another eats it. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila view, which allows that a person might not eat his provision and instead eat another's. Next, he provides a linguistic definition: rizq in the language means 'measured sustenance' (al-qut al-muqaddar), giving the example of soldiers receiving a monthly stipend (murtaziq). He notes that rizq is also used for absolute ownership (milk) and for nourishment (ghidha'). He then cites Quran 11:6, which states that Allah guarantees provision for every creature. Since animals do not possess ownership by legal means, the verse must refer to nourishment, not ownership. He then argues: if rizq is interpreted as ownership, then haram is not provision, and a person can eat another's property. But if interpreted as nourishment, then haram is included because Allah creates the feeding process in bodies without the servant's act. He concludes that it is impossible to confine rizq to ownership alone; it applies to both meanings. Finally, he mentions that two scholars—Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini—verified that the disagreement on matters of terms and provisions is merely verbal, and that this is the correct view.

Key Terms

قوت
غذاء
ملك
مرتزقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rizq refers only to lawful food (halal).
Correction: The author argues that rizq includes both halal and haram sustenance because the linguistic and Quranic evidence points to nourishment, not just ownership.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the literal (lughawi) definition of rizq?
A) Absolute ownership (milk)
B) Nourishment (ghidha')
C) Measured sustenance (al-qut al-muqaddar)
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Rizq in Islamic theology includes all sustenance, legal or illegal, because it is defined by nourishment not ownership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever consumed something prohibited—like food from a haram income—do you truly believe it was still your rizq from Allah? How does that realization confront your feelings about repentance and change?
?
When you worry about not having enough provision, are you implicitly denying that every creature eats its own allocated rizq? What would it take for you to genuinely trust that your rizq cannot be eaten by anyone else?
?
Consider a time you felt another person's gain was at your expense. Does the Maturidi view of rizq challenge that sense of injustice? How can you align your heart with the belief that Allah provides each person exactly what is theirs, no more and no less?

Ahl al-Haqq's Proof from Q3:178 and Q7:179
The author presents the Ahl al-Haqq's proof from two Qur'anic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ahl al-Haqq use Qur'anic verses to demonstrate that divine will encompasses both good and evil, and that God's will is not limited to what pleases Him.

Core Idea
God's will includes evil for some, as shown by Q3:178 and Q7:179.

The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot will evil or command what He does not will. The Ahl al-Haqq respond by citing two clear Qur'anic verses that show God intentionally wills the increase of sin for some and creates others for Hell. This lesson examines that proof and its logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from the initial argument to the two verses.
Pay attention to the logical structure: a problem then a scriptural proof.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
The passage begins by stating the Mu'tazila claim: if God's will is ironclad, then disbelievers are either excused (contradicting the Book) or punished beyond capacity. Both are unacceptable to them.
This sets up the need for a different understanding.
3
Examine Q3:178
The verse says God gives respite to disbelievers 'so that they may increase in sin.' The lam (li) indicates purpose, meaning God intends their increase of sin. This directly shows God wills evil for some.
Contrast with the view that respite is always for repentance.
4
Examine Q7:179
The verse states God created many for Hell. The author argues: if God created someone for Hell while intending paradise for them, that would be unjust. Therefore, God must intend from them what leads to Hell.
This shows divine will encompasses the means and ends of damnation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخروج عما أراده الله تعالى على زعمكم فيصير الكافر مجبورا على كفره، ممنوعًا عن الإيمان بالإرادة التي هي أبرم وأحكم من الحديد في المنع، فإما أن يعذر على ذلك والقول به تكذيب للكتاب وخروج عن الإجماع، وإما أن يعاقب عليه ولا يعذر وهو القول بتكليف ما ليس في الوسع، ولأن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه والأمر بما لا يريده سفه في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب إلى مثل هذه الشبهات يذهبون. وأهل الحق يحتجون بقوله تعالى: { وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا أَنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ خَيْرٌ لِّأَنفُسِهِمْ ۚ إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِثْمًا } دلت الآية أنه أراد منهم ازدياد الإثم حيث أملى لهم ليزدادوا إثما، وقال تعالى : { وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنسِ } ومن ذرأه لجهنم أراد منه ما به يصير لجهنم إذ لو ذرأه لجهنم مع إرادة ما به يصير للجنة فقد أراد فيه ما يصير بإدخاله ما ذرأه له ظالمًا، وهذا محال.
Exiting from what Allah the Exalted willed, according to your claim, would make the disbeliever compelled in his disbelief, prevented from faith by an will that is more binding and firmer than iron in prevention. So either he is excused for that—and saying that is a denial of the Book and a departure from consensus—or he is punished for it and not excused, which is the saying of obligating what is beyond capacity. Because willing what one is not pleased with is foolishness, and commanding what one does not will is foolishness in the witnessed world, so likewise in the Unseen. They go to such specious arguments. And the People of Truth adduce His saying the Exalted: {And let not those who disbelieve think that what We grant them is good for themselves; We only grant them so that they may increase in sin.} The verse indicates that He intended from them the increase of sin, since He granted them respite so that they would increase in sin. And He said the Exalted: {And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind.} And whomever He created for Hell, He intended from him that by which he becomes for Hell, because if He created him for Hell while intending that by which he becomes for Paradise, then He intended for him that by which he enters what He created him for unjustly, and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Qur'an presented by the Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth) against the Mu'tazila who argued that divine will cannot compel evil. The author first states the problem: if God's will is as strong as iron in preventing faith, then the disbeliever is either excused (which contradicts the Book and consensus) or punished without excuse (which implies obligation beyond capacity). Both are unacceptable. The Ahl al-Haqq then cite two verses. First, Q3:178: God says He grants respite to disbelievers so that they increase in sin. The lam (li) here indicates purpose (lam al-ta'lil), meaning God intends their increase of sin. Second, Q7:179: God created many for Hell, so He intended from them what leads to Hell. If He created them for Hell while intending paradise for them, that would be unjust and impossible. Thus, divine will encompasses both good and evil.

Key Terms

ازدياد الإثم
ذرأ
لجهنم
تكليف ما لا يطاق
أملى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will only pertains to good and that He cannot will evil for anyone.
Correction: The Qur'an explicitly states that God wills the increase of sin for some (Q3:178) and creates others for Hell (Q7:179), showing that divine will includes evil as part of a just decree.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Haqq's reading, what is the purpose of God giving respite to disbelievers as stated in Q3:178?
A) To give them a chance to repent
B) So that they increase in sin
C) To test their patience
D) To demonstrate His mercy

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an directly states that God wills the increase of sin for some and creates others for Hell, proving that divine will includes both good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever resented God's decree for someone's suffering? How does this lesson's proof that God's will includes evil change your understanding of His justice?
?
If God specifically wills the increase of sin for some, how does that make you examine your own sins? Are you more fearful of His judgment, or more reliant on His mercy?
?
When you encounter a person who persists in disbelief, do you see God's will at work? How does that recognition affect your attitude toward them—do you feel compassion, humility, or something else? What action should you take?

Refutation of al-Ka'bi's Interpretation of Divine Guidance and Misguidance
Refutation of al-Ka'bi's reward/punishment interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ka'bi's interpretation that guidance and misguidance are reward and punishment is a distortion of the Quranic text.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi reversed cause and effect, making God's action a reward rather than the cause of belief or disbelief.

The Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi argued that when God guides or misguides, it is merely a reward or punishment for prior actions. Imam Nasafi refutes this by showing that the Quran clearly makes God's expansion or tightening of the chest the cause of belief or disbelief, not the consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice al-Ka'bi's claim and the author's immediate response.
Focus on the reversal of causality.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Al-Ka'bi says the expansion or tightness of the chest is a reward or punishment for prior submission or disbelief. Find this in the text.
3
Understand the reversal of cause and ruling
Nasafi points out that the verse makes expansion the cause of Islam, not the result. Al-Ka'bi swapped cause and effect. Reflect on why this is a distortion.
This is the key logical error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحرجه سببًا يحصل به الضلال وأخبر أنه متى أراد أن يهدي إنسانا يشرح صدره ليهتدي، ومتى أراد ضلاله يجعل صدره ضيقًا حرجًا لئلا يؤمن فيبقى على الكفر

والكعبي لتحيره اعترض بما كانت المداواة أولى به لو كان ظن هذا الاعتراض صحيحًا فقال : معنى الآية أن من أسلم آتاه الله من لطائف ما لا يقدر عليه غيره ثوابًا لطاعته، ومن كفر ضيق الله صدره عقابًا لذلك، وهو تحريف للكتاب ونقل للكلم عن مواضعها وليس بتأويل وذلك لأن الله تعالى أثبت له الإسلام إذا شرح صدره، والكفر إذا صير قلبه ضيقًا حرجًا ولم يوجب شرح القلب لأنه أسلم، ولا ضيق القلب لأنه لم يؤمن، فكان ما قاله جعل ما هو جار مجرى الحكم علة، وجعل ما هو جار مجرى العلة حكما وفساده لا يخفى

واعتراض البصريين أنه أراد بالهداية البيان وبالإضلال التسمية فاسد جدًا، لأن شرح الصدر لو كان يقع للبيان والبيان واقع للكل، لكان كل من وقع له البيان وقع له شرح الصدر، فكان كل كافر مشروح الصدر لحصول البيان له، ولكان يقع له ولكان يقع ضيق الصدر لتسميته تعالى إياه ضالاً، فكان كل كافر مشروح الصدر لحصول البيان له، ضيق الصدر لتسميته تعالى إياه ضالاً وهو محال، وكذا قسمة الله تعالى الخلق إلى من شرح له الصدر ، وإلى من جعل صدره ضيقًا باطلة إذ كل لما كان البيان واقعا وكان شرح الصدر له حاصلاً لم يبق أحد ليس بمشروح الصدر البتة، ونسبة الله تعالى إلى الخطأ فى القسمة كفر وضلال، وبالله العصمة. ثم نقول لهم : لما كان معنى قوله : ( وَمَن يُرِدان يُضِلَّهُ ) أي من يرد أن يسميه ضالاً كانت تسميته ضالاً داخله تحت الإرادة، فبعد ذلك نقول: إذا أراد أن يسميه ضالاً، هل يريد ضلالته؟

فإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا مذهبهم وانقادوا للحق، وإن قالوا: لا.

قيل لهم : إذا أرادوا تسميته ضالاً ولم يرد ضلالة بل أراد اهتداءه، فلو حصل ما أراد وهو الاهتداء (ولم يحصل ما لم يرد) وهو الضلال، أكان في تسميته
And He made it a cause by which misguidance occurs, and He informed that whenever He wills to guide a person, He expands his chest to be guided, and whenever He wills his misguidance, He makes his chest tight and constricted so that he does not believe, and he remains upon disbelief. Al-Ka'bi, due to his confusion, objected—though treatment would have been more appropriate for him if he had thought this objection correct—and said: The meaning of the verse is that whoever submits, God gives him from subtle favors that no one else can give as a reward for his obedience, and whoever disbelieves, God constricts his chest as a punishment for that. This is a distortion of the Book and a displacement of words from their places, and it is not an interpretation. That is because God Almighty attributed Islam to him when He expanded his chest, and disbelief when He made his heart tight and constricted, and He did not make the expansion of the heart because he submitted, nor the tightness of the heart because he did not believe. So what he said was making what is in the position of the ruling into a cause, and making what is in the position of the cause into a ruling, and its corruption is not hidden. And the objection of the Basrians that He intended by 'guidance' clarification and by 'misguidance' naming is extremely corrupt, because if the expansion of the chest were to occur for clarification, and clarification occurs for everyone, then everyone for whom clarification occurs would have expansion of the chest. Thus every disbeliever would have an expanded chest due to the occurrence of clarification for him, and it would occur for him, and tightness of the chest would occur for him due to His naming him as misguided. So every disbeliever would have an expanded chest due to the occurrence of clarification for him, and a tight chest due to His naming him as misguided—and this is impossible. Likewise, God's division of creation into those for whom He expanded the chest and those whose chest He made tight would be invalid, since for everyone clarification occurs and expansion of the chest occurs for them, there would remain no one at all who does not have an expanded chest. And attributing error to God in division is disbelief and misguidance, and in God is protection. Then we say to them: Since the meaning of His statement, 'And whoever He wills to misguide,' is 'whoever He wills to name as misguided,' then His naming him as misguided is included under the will. Then after that we say: If He wills to name him misguided, does He will his misguidance? If they say: Yes, they abandon their doctrine and submit to the truth. And if they say: No, it is said to them: If He wills to name him misguided and does not will misguidance but wills his guidance, then if what He wills occurs—which is guidance—and what He did not will—which is misguidance—does not occur, would [it be said] in His naming...
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two Mu'tazili interpretations of divine guidance and misguidance in the Quran. The author begins by affirming that God creates both guidance and misguidance directly, as indicated by the verse about expanding the chest for Islam or making it tight. He then refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that the verse refers to reward and punishment. Al-Ka'bi argued that if someone submits, God gives him special gifts as reward, and if someone disbelieves, God constricts his chest as punishment. The author rejects this as distortion, because the verse links the expansion or tightness of the chest directly to God's will, not to a person's prior action. In the source text, God attributes Islam to the person when He expands the chest, and disbelief when He makes the heart tight, not the reverse. Al-Ka'bi mistakenly reversed the causal relationship, making the ruling (Islam or disbelief) into a cause (reward or punishment) and the cause (divine action) into a ruling. Then the author refutes the Basrian view that 'guidance' means 'clarification' and 'misguidance' means 'naming'. This is refuted by a reductio ad absurdum: If guidance were merely clarification, and clarification is universal (reaches everyone), then every disbeliever would also receive the expansion of the chest (شرح الصدر) because they all receive clarification. Similarly, if misguidance were just naming, then the disbeliever would simultaneously have an expanded chest (due to receiving clarification) and a tight chest (due to being named misguided), which is impossible. Consequently, God's division of people into those with expanded chests and those with tight chests would be meaningless, since everyone would have an expanded chest. Attributing such an erroneous division to God is blasphemy. Finally, the author presents a dialectical argument: If the Mu'tazila say 'misguidance' in the verse means 'naming', then God's naming someone misguided must be an act of will. Then they are asked: Does God also will the actual misguidance of that person? If they say yes, they concede the Ash'ari position; if they say no, they face the contradiction that God wills to name someone misguided while willing them to be guided, which would mean the naming is false.

Key Terms
الهداية
الإضلال
شرح الصدر
ضيق الصدر
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bi's interpretation is a valid alternative reading that does not change the meaning.
Correction: The passage shows it literally reverses the causal relationship stated in the verse, making it a distortion, not a valid interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what did al-Ka'bi incorrectly treat as a cause?
A) The expansion of the chest as a reward.
B) The tightening of the chest as a punishment.
C) Both the expansion and tightening as effects of prior actions.
D) The divine will as the cause of belief.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's error was making God's direct causation into a secondary reward, reversing the Quran's clear causal statement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a moment of spiritual openness or closure, do you immediately see it as God's direct action upon you, or do you instinctively look for something you did to earn or lose it?
?
If you truly believed that your very ability to obey comes entirely from God's unearned expansion of your chest, how would it change the way you pray and ask for guidance?
?
Al-Ka'bi reversed the order to make human action primary. In your daily life, are there areas where you subtly treat God's blessings as reactions to your own goodness rather than as pure grace? What would it cost you to surrender that logic?

Refutation of the Basrian Interpretation of Guidance as Clarification and Misguidance as Naming
Refutation of Basrian clarification/naming interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting 'guidance' as mere clarification and 'misguidance' as mere naming leads to absurd contradictions and blasphemy.

Core Idea
If guidance is clarification given to all, then all disbelievers would have expanded chests, contradicting the verse.

The Basrian Mu'tazila claimed that God's guidance and misguidance are just His clarifying the truth and naming people accordingly. Imam Nasafi shows this destroys the meaning of the verse and implies an impossible contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Basrian claim
The Basrians said 'guidance' means 'clarification' and 'misguidance' means 'naming'. Find this in the passage.
2
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi argues that if guidance is clarification, and clarification is universal, then every disbeliever would also have an expanded chest. But the verse says disbelievers have tight chests. This is a contradiction.
This refutes the core of the Basrian view.
3
Analyze the dialectical argument
Nasafi then shows that even on their own terms, if misguidance means naming, then God's naming someone misguided must be an act of will. He then asks if God also wills actual misguidance. Either answer leads to their defeat.
This is a classic reductio.
4
Conclude the necessity of the Ash'ari position
The Basrian view collapses into absurdity. The only coherent reading is that God directly creates guidance and misguidance.
