Refuting the Jabri Interpretation of Divine Will
Al-Nasafi refutes the Jabri interpretation of the verse about divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's argument that divine will does not imply compulsion, using a logical analysis of the Qur'anic verse.

Core Idea
Divine will does not mean compulsion; interpreting it so leads to absurdity.

The Jabriyya claim that divine will (mashi'a) necessarily implies compulsion (jabr) in all human actions. Al-Nasafi refutes this by examining the verse 'If Allah had willed, He would have gathered them to guidance' (6:35). He demonstrates that interpreting this verse as compulsion leads to two impossible scenarios, thus the only valid understanding is that divine will does not force faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear before interpretation.
Notice the verse citation and the logical reasoning that follows.
2
Identify the two hypothetical scenarios
The passage presents two possible ways the verse could be interpreted under compulsion. Find both and understand why each is problematic.
These scenarios are the core of the refutation.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow the argument to its conclusion: since both options are unwise, the verse must mean something else, and thus divine will does not imply compulsion.
The conclusion directly refutes the Jabri position.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider how this passage guards against the misconception that divine will equals compulsion. Write a sentence summarizing the refutation in your own words.
This helps internalize the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أنه تعالى قال: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ لَجَمَعَهُمْ عَلَى الْهُدَى ﴾ وإنما يكون جمعهم على الهدى أن لو كان هدى الكل جنساً واحداً فأما إذا كان هدى البعض جبرًا ولا تنال به الجنة، ولا يتخلص به عن النار وهدى البعض اختيارا، وتنال به الجنة ويتخلص به عن النار فلا يكون هذا جمعهم على الهدى وهو يكون بأحد وجهين إما أن يهدي الكفار باختيارهم حتى يكونوا مع الذين آمنوا باختيارهم مجموعين على الهدى، وإما أن يخلق الاهتداء جبرًا في الكفار ومنع الذين اهتدوا باختيارهم عن الهدى الاختياري وأثبت فيهم الهدى بطريق الجبر، وهذا منه صرف للمؤمنين عن الإيمان قهرا وجبرا، وهذا ليس بحكمة فلم يبق إلا أن يكون المراد هو جمع الكل على الهدى وهو ما بينا، فإذا لم يشأ ذلك دل أنه لم يشأ إيمان الكفرة، والله الموفق.
He confirms it that He, may He be exalted, said: 'And if Allah had willed, He would have gathered them to guidance.' And their gathering to guidance would only be if the guidance of all were one kind. But if the guidance of some is by compulsion and they do not attain Paradise thereby, nor are they saved from the Fire, and the guidance of others is by choice, and they attain Paradise thereby and are saved from the Fire, then this would not be their gathering to guidance. And it would be in one of two ways: either He guides the disbelievers by their choice so that they are gathered with those who believed by their choice upon guidance, or He creates guidance by compulsion in the disbelievers and prevents those who were guided by choice from optional guidance and establishes in them guidance by way of compulsion. This would be from Him a turning away of the believers from faith by force and compulsion, and this is not wisdom. So nothing remains except that the meaning is the gathering of all to guidance, which is what we have explained. So when He did not will that, it indicates that He did not will the faith of the disbelievers. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the Jabriyya claim that 'mashi'a' (divine will) means compulsion (jabr). Al-Nasafi begins by citing the verse 'And if Allah had willed, He would have gathered them to guidance' (Qur'an 6:35). He argues that gathering all to guidance would only be coherent if all guidance were of one kind. However, if some guidance is by compulsion (not leading to Paradise) and some by choice (leading to Paradise), then gathering them is not possible. He then shows two hypothetical ways this verse could be interpreted under compulsion: either God guides disbelievers by choice (which would make them like believers, but that contradicts the situation where they remain disbelievers), or He creates guidance by compulsion in disbelievers while removing voluntary guidance from believers. The latter involves forcefully turning believers away from faith, which is not wise (hukm). Therefore, the only valid interpretation is that 'gathering to guidance' means all are guided by choice, which did not happen, so God did not will the faith of the disbelievers by compulsion. The conclusion is that the verse refutes the Jabri understanding of will.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'a, will)
الجبر (al-jabr, compulsion)
الهدى (al-huda, guidance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The misconception that divine will (mashi'a) necessarily implies compulsion (jabr) in all human actions, and that the verse supports this.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that interpreting the verse as compulsion leads to absurd consequences: either guiding disbelievers by choice (contradiction) or forcing believers away from faith (unwise). Thus, divine will does not mean compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the absurd consequence of interpreting the verse 'ولو شاء الله لجمعهم على الهدى' as compulsion?
A) It would mean God does not will guidance for anyone.
B) It would require God to either guide disbelievers by choice or prevent believers from voluntary faith, both unwise.
C) It would contradict the verse about hellfire.
D) It would mean God's will is ineffective.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's logical analysis proves that divine will does not imply compulsion; the verse instead indicates that God did not will the faith of disbelievers by force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever tell yourself 'it was Allah's will' to avoid taking responsibility? How does this lesson confront that excuse, and what will you do differently when you feel tempted to sin?
?
Look at someone you know who rejects faith. Do you deep down think Allah could have forced them to believe? According to this lesson, why is that not the case, and how should that change how you engage with them?
?
Are you relying on a 'compulsion' mindset—expecting Allah to guide you without your own choice and effort? What one concrete step can you take today to actively choose guidance, knowing that Allah wills it through your free will?

Refuting the Jabriyya: The Dilemma of Compulsion and Divine Will
Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma against the Jabriyya using aslah and the verses of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi uses a dilemma to refute the Jabriyya interpretation of Quranic verses about God's will.

Core Idea
A wise God does not leave what is most beneficial, nor speaks of foolish acts.

This passage presents a powerful objection against those who claim God gives faith by compulsion. Al-Nasafi uses a dilemma to show that such a view contradicts both reason and the majestic speech of the Quran. By examining what it would mean for compulsion to be 'most beneficial,' he forces the opponent to choose between an absurd consequence and a blasphemous implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before you interpret.
Notice the compact, dialectical style of kalam.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find where Al-Nasafi presents the two possibilities: compulsion being 'aslah' or not. See how each leads to a contradiction.
The first horn leads to observable disbelief, the second to absurd speech.
3
Analyse the reductio on the Quranic verses
Consider how Al-Nasafi shows that the Jabri interpretation would render the verses about will as implying injustice and foolishness, which is impossible for God.
Compare with the analogy of a person threatening self-harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال : ماذا تزعمون أن إعطاء الإيمان للكافر بطريق الجبر هل هو أصلح له أم ليس بأصلح ؟ فإن قالوا: هو أصلح، كان ينبغي أن يعطى كل كافر إيمانا جبرًا فلا يبقى في الأرض كافر ، وحيث رأينا ما لا يحصى من الكفر دل أنه لم يفعل بهم ذلك فكان تاركا ما هو أصلح لهم ، وإن لم يكن ذلك أصلح لهم فلا يجوز له أن يفعل ذلك فلا معنى لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ لأنه يصير في التقدير : لو شئنا لفعلنا ما هو ظلم وسفه وأبطلنا ألوهيتنا وأزلنا ربوبيتنا، والتكلم بمثل هذا في حال بيان كمال قدرته وإظهار الاستغناء له عن غيره لا يتصور إلا من سفيه جاهل فإنه بمنزلة قول من يقول لو شئت لأوقعت نفسي في النيران الجامحة وأسقطت نفسي من رؤوس الجبال الشاهقة ولأخذت بيدي الحيات الناهشة - جل ربنا وتعالى عن التكلم بمثل هذا الكلام.
Then it is said: What do you claim? Is giving faith to the disbeliever by way of compulsion most beneficial (aslah) for him or not most beneficial? If they say: It is most beneficial, then it should be that every disbeliever is given faith by compulsion, and no disbeliever would remain on earth. But since we see countless disbelief, it indicates that He did not do that to them, so He would be leaving what is most beneficial for them. And if that is not most beneficial for them, then it is not permissible for Him to do that. Then there would be no meaning to His saying, Exalted: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' and His saying: 'And if your Lord had willed, whoever is on earth would have believed all together' because it would become in estimation: 'If We had willed, We would have done what is injustice and foolishness, and we would have nullified our divinity and removed our lordship.' And speaking like this in the context of declaring His perfect power and showing His independence from others is only conceivable from a foolish ignorant person. For it is like the saying of one who says: 'If I had willed, I would have thrown myself into raging fires and fallen myself from lofty mountain peaks and taken in my hand venomous snakes.' Exalted is our Lord, far above speaking such speech.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument against the Jabriyya position that God gives faith by compulsion. Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma: If giving faith by compulsion is 'aslah' (most beneficial) for the disbeliever, then God, being wise, should do it for every disbeliever, but in reality many remain in disbelief, meaning He leaves what is most beneficial, which is problematic. If it is not most beneficial, then God should not do it, so the verses that say 'If We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' (32:13) and 'If your Lord had willed, whoever is on earth would have believed all together' (10:99) would be meaningless because they would imply God speaking of doing something unwise and unjust—like a person foolishly threatening to harm himself. Since God speaks in the context of showing power and independence, such speech cannot be attributed to Him. Thus, the Jabri interpretation is refuted.

Key Terms
أصلح (aslah, most beneficial)
الجبر (al-jabr, compulsion)
سفه (safah, foolishness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quranic verses about God's hypothetical will (e.g., 'If We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance') imply that God could actually force faith on everyone, but He chooses not to, and this is compatible with His wisdom.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that if forcing faith were most beneficial, God would have done it; if not, the verses would imply injustice and foolishness, which is impossible for God. Thus, those verses cannot be interpreted as supporting compulsion.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi argue would be the consequence if giving faith by compulsion were 'aslah'?
A) All disbelievers would become believers by compulsion.
B) God would not need to guide anyone.
C) Faith would no longer be a test.
D) Disbelievers would choose faith on their own.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's dilemma shows that the Jabriyya interpretation of divine will leads to either an observable contradiction (disbelief persists) or a theological impossibility (God speaking foolishly).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God could will everyone's faith but doesn't, do you feel a sense of relief that God's wisdom overrides mere power? Or does it unsettle you that disbelief persists? How does this affect your trust in God's plan for those you love who disbelieve?
?
Al-Nasafi's analogy compares the idea of God doing something foolish to someone throwing themselves into fire. Have you ever thought about God's actions as if He were capricious? What does this passage demand you to believe about God's character, and how might you need to repent for any irreverent thoughts?
?
If you apply this reasoning to your own life, where do you tend to think God 'should' have done something different? How does the aslah principle challenge your expectations of God's actions in your personal trials? Will you accept that God's wisdom always chooses the best, even when it hurts?

Divine Decree
beginner
4 min
Impossibility of Compulsion with Remaining Power
Al-Nasafi proves that giving faith by compulsion is impossible while capacity remains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that God cannot compel faith in a person who still possesses capacity (qudra), and how this refutes the Jabriyya position.

Core Idea
Compulsion requires removing capacity, but capacity is essential for attaining faith's reward.

Al-Nasafi continues his critique of the Jabriyya by showing that their view leads to absurdity. If God were to compel faith, He would have to remove the very capacity He created for acquiring faith. This would nullify the greatest benefit for the servant, which according to the opponents themselves is foolish. Let's see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the argument's logical flow come through.
Notice the structure: a claim followed by a chain of implications.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the main assertion: giving faith by compulsion while capacity remains is impossible.
3
Understand the necessary removal of capacity
Realize that compulsion requires removing capacity. Why? Because if capacity remains, the person can choose otherwise.
4
Grasp the nature and purpose of capacity
Capacity is neutral (for opposites) and created by Allah for performing faith to attain the highest station.
5
See the reductio ad absurdum
Removing capacity nullifies the most beneficial act (faith), which is foolishness. Since Allah is above foolishness, compulsion is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إعطاء الإيمان بطريق الجبر مع بقاء القدرة محال، ولابد لذلك من سلب القدرة، والقدرة عندكم تصلح للضدين، وخلقها الله تعالى ليفعل بها الإيمان وعرضه بها لأعلى المنزلتين فصار سلبها مبطلاً هذا التصديق الذي هو أصلح للعبد، فصار بذلك مبطلاً الأصلح للعبد وهو عندكم سفه – جل الله عن ذلك.
Then we say: Giving faith by way of compulsion while capacity remains is impossible. Therefore it requires the removal of capacity. And capacity, according to you, is suitable for opposites. And Allah, Exalted, created it so that one may perform faith with it and thereby attain the highest of the two stations. So removing it nullifies this belief (tasdiq) which is most beneficial (aslah) for the servant. Thus it nullifies the most beneficial for the servant, and that according to you is foolishness. Exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a proof that compulsion (jabr) in matters of faith is impossible given the nature of human capacity (qudra). Al-Nasafi argues that imposing faith by compulsion while the person retains their capacity (qudra) is inconceivable; therefore, capacity must be removed. But capacity, in the Maturidi view, is created by God and is suitable for both good and evil (i.e., it is neutral). God created capacity so that the servant can perform faith and thereby attain the highest station (i.e., Paradise). Removing capacity would thus nullify the servant's ability to perform the very belief (tasdiq) that is most beneficial for him. And nullifying what is most beneficial is considered foolishness (safah) by the opponents. Since God is far above foolishness, the compulsionist position is absurd.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra, capacity)
الجبر (al-jabr, compulsion)
أصلح (aslah, most beneficial)
سفه (safah, foolishness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can compel someone to believe while leaving their capacity intact.
Correction: This is impossible because compulsion entails removing the capacity to choose, but capacity is necessary for the act of faith to be meaningful and to attain reward.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi consider giving faith by compulsion while capacity remains impossible?
A) Because capacity is not needed for faith.
B) Because capacity must be removed, but capacity is created for good and evil, and removing it nullifies the most beneficial.
C) Because faith is only possible without capacity.
D) Because compulsion would require removing God's power.

Key Takeaway
Compulsion in faith is impossible because it would require removing the very capacity God created for achieving the greatest benefit.

?
إذا كان الجبر ممكناً لكان إيمانك بلا قيمة. هل تتعامل مع اختيارك للإيمان كأمانة عظيمة أم كأمر مسلم به؟

Consensus and Refutation of Ka'bi's Objections
Al-Nasafi invokes consensus and refutes Ka'bi's objections about divine love and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi uses consensus to defend the Sunni view on divine will and refutes al-Ka'bi's objections by distinguishing between God's will, love, and the two meanings of command.

Core Idea
God's will and love are distinct; command is either creative (always effective) or prescriptive (obligation, not realization).

In this passage, Al-Nasafi defends the Sunni position that everything that happens is by God's will. He first invokes the consensus of the Muslim community as a decisive proof, then responds to two objections raised by al-Ka'bi. The first objection confuses love with will, and the second misunderstands the nature of God's command. As you read, pay attention to how Al-Nasafi clarifies these terms to preserve the theological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: a consensus statement, then two objections with refutations.
The text may feel dense; take your time.
2
Identify the consensus argument
Al-Nasafi begins by citing the statement 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not' as the belief of all Muslims. He claims this consensus is a definitive proof against opponents.
This is a classic Maturidi appeal to ijma'.
3
Examine the refutation of al-Ka'bi's first objection
Al-Ka'bi objects that Muslims also say 'What Allah loves is, and what He does not love is not.' Al-Nasafi calls this a false accusation, since no Muslim has said that. Love and will are distinct.
Notice the dismissal as taqawwul (false assertion).
4
Understand the second objection and its refutation
Al-Ka'bi argues that Muslims say 'The command of Allah is effective' yet many commands are not obeyed. Al-Nasafi distinguishes two types of command: creative (takwin) which is always effective and identical to will, and prescriptive (taklif) which only obligates, not necessitates existence.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قول الأمة: ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن من غير اضطراب قلب أحد دل أن اعتقاد جميع المسلمين أولاً وأخيراً ما ذهبنا إليه، وقولهم مخالف لإجماع المسلمين وهو حجة موجبة للعلم بقطع القول على خطأ من خالفهم، ومعارضة الكعبي هذا بقول المسلمين ما أحب الله تعالى كان وما لم يحب لم يكن معارضة بما هو تقول على المسلمين وتخرص إذ لم يسمع أحد، ومعارضته لقول المسلمين أمر الله نافذ وكم من أمر لم يؤتمر معارضة فاسدة، لأن المراد بقولهم أمر الله نافذ أن أراد به أمر تكوين فهو مما له نفاذ، ولا يتصور أن لا ينفذ، فهذا والمشيئة سواء وإن أريد به أمر إيجاب وتكليف فحكمة وجوب الائتمار لا وجوده.
Then the saying of the community: 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not' without any disturbance of heart indicates that the belief of all Muslims, first and last, is what we have gone to. And their saying contradicts the consensus of the Muslims, and it is a proof necessitating knowledge by decisive judgment on the error of those who oppose them. And al-Ka'bi's objection to this by the saying of the Muslims: 'What Allah loves is, and what He does not love is not' is an objection that is a false accusation against the Muslims and conjecture, since no one has heard it. And his objection to the saying of the Muslims: 'The command of Allah is effective; how many a command is not obeyed' is a corrupt objection. Because the meaning of their saying 'the command of Allah is effective'—if what is meant is the command of creation (takwin), then it is something that is effective, and it is inconceivable that it is not effective; this and the will are the same. And if what is meant is the command of obligation and prescription (taklif), then its wisdom is the obligation of compliance, not its existence.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's objections using the consensus of the Muslim community. Al-Nasafi begins by citing the famous Sunni statement: 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not' —a formula that supports the Ash'ari-Maturidi position that everything that happens is by God's will, but not necessarily by compulsion (since human choice is also willed). He claims this is the belief of all Muslims from the beginning, and those who oppose it (i.e., the Jabriyya) contradict consensus, which is a definitive proof. Then he refutes al-Ka'bi's objection that Muslims also say 'What Allah loves is, and what He does not love is not'—al-Nasafi says this is a false accusation because no one has said that; rather, love (hubb) is different from will. Next, he refutes the objection that Muslims say 'The command of Allah is effective' despite many commands not being obeyed. Al-Nasafi explains that 'command' (amr) can mean either the creative command (amr takwini) which always takes effect—this is synonymous with will—or the prescriptive command (amr taklifi) which requires obedience but is about obligation, not existence. Thus, the presence of disobedience does not contradict the effectiveness of the prescriptive command because its 'effect' is the obligation itself, not its realization.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma', consensus)
الأمر (al-amr, command)
المحبة (al-mahabbah, love)
التكوين (al-takwin, creation)
التكليف (al-taklif, obligation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's love and will are identical, and that God's command always results in obedience.
Correction: God's love and will are distinct; the command can be creative (always effective) or prescriptive (obligation, not necessarily implemented).

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi refute al-Ka'bi's objection that Muslims say 'What Allah loves is, and what He does not love is not'?
A) He says it is a false accusation because no Muslim has ever said that.
B) He says it is true but irrelevant.
C) He says it refers only to the created command.
D) He says it is a matter of interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defends the Sunni position by invoking consensus and clarifying that God's will and love are distinct, and that 'command' refers either to creative decrees (always effective) or prescriptive obligations (not necessarily realized).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of Allah that seems difficult to obey, do you secretly doubt that He will give you the ability, or do you embrace the obligation regardless of the outcome? What would change if you truly believed that the command's effectiveness is in the obligation itself, not in your success?
?
Have you ever confused what Allah wills (which happens) with what He loves (which may not happen)? How does that confusion affect your view of trials and disobedience in your life? Will you now consciously distinguish between them in your heart?
?
The phrase 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not' is simple yet profound. Do you truly live by it, accepting both blessings and hardships as from His will without protest? If not, what specific situation today challenges your submission to His will?

The Purpose of Command for the Disobedient
Response to Mu'tazilite objection about commanding the disobedient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's command to those He knows will disobey is not pointless but serves to realize His foreknowledge and just punishment.

Core Idea
God's command to the disobedient realizes His eternal knowledge and justice, not ignorance.

This passage addresses a key Mu'tazilite objection to divine commands: if God knows someone will disobey, why command him? The author explains that command and prohibition are not meant to change God's knowledge but to bring about what He has eternally known. Without them, there would be no basis for reward and punishment. Understanding this refutes the accusation that God commands out of ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the author's vigorous response style and the logical flow.
The passage uses a dialectical style, addressing the Mu'tazilites directly.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite objection
Find where the author mentions the Mu'tazilite claim that commanding the disobedient would make God ignorant (tajhil). Note how he reframes the issue.
3
Understand the Sunni response
The author argues that command and prohibition are for 'realization of what Allah knows'. Focus on why command is necessary for obedience to be obligatory and disobedience to be punishable.
Without command, there would be no basis for reward or punishment.
4
Examine the example of Pharaoh
The author uses Pharaoh as a concrete example to show that God's prior knowledge of his disobedience is fulfilled through the command itself, not despite it.
5
Reflect on the critique of the 'motive' claim
Notice the author's critique of those who say obedience requires a motive. He traces this idea to the Mu'tazila and warns against it.
This relates to broader debates about free will and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: هذا كلام تمسكتم به لجهلكم بمذاهب خصومكم ولو عرفتم ذلك حقيقة لما اشتغلتم به، وذلك لأن الأمر والنهي عند خصومكم ورد لتحقيق ما علم الله تعالى وإن كان يتراءى من حيث الظاهر أنهما وردا لمخالفة العلم، وبيان ذلك أنه تعالى علم بسابق علمه أن فرعون يكفر ويعصى ويعاقبه الله تعالى في النار، وكذا غيره من الكفرة وأخبر بذلك ثم لا يعذب إلا على العصيان ولا عصيان بدون الأمر والنهي إذ لو لم يأمر بالإيمان لما وجب تحصيله ولا حرم تركه ولو لم ينه عن الكفر لما حرم تحصيله ولما وجب تركه ولو لم يجب الإيمان ولم يحرم الكفر لكان لا يعاقب الكافر ولا يملأ منه جهنم، فلا يتحقق ما علمه وأخبر به من إدخاله الجنة وإكرامه إياه بالثواب وأمر ونهى من علم منه المعصية لا ليأتمر وينتهي بل ليترك الائتمار والانتهاء فعاقبه الله ويدخله النار، فيتحقق ما علم وما أخبر به، ولولا الأمر والنهي لما تحقق ذلك فكان أمره ونهيه لتحقيق ما علمه وأخبر به، وإنما يكون الأمر على ما زعمت المعتزلة أن لو كان أمر الكافر ليطيعه لا ليعصيه فحينئذ يكون الأمر والنهي لتجهيله وتكذيبه، وأما إذا كان ذلك ليعصي لا ليطيع فكان لتحقيق ما علم وأخبر لا لتجهيله، وصار الحاصل أن من علم الله منه أهل طاعة أمره ليطيع لتتحقق الطاعة إذ لا تحقق لها بدون الأمر، ومن علم منه المعصية أمره لئلا يفعل بل يعصي إذ لا تحقق للمعصية بدون الأمر، وما وقع في أفواه متفقهة زماننا أن فائدة الوجوب بلا داع تلقنوه من المعتزلة فيتكلمون به جهلاً منهم بما يؤول إليه من المذهب الباطل، وتقرر بالوقوف على هذه الجملة أن الأمر في الحقيقة لتحقيق ما علم وإن كان يتراءى أنه للتجهيل، والله الموفق.
Then we say: This is a statement you have clung to out of ignorance of the doctrines of your opponents. If you truly knew that, you would not have occupied yourselves with it. That is because command and prohibition according to your opponents come for the realization of what Allah knows, even though it appears outwardly that they come contrary to [His] knowledge. The explanation of that is that Allah, the Exalted, knew by His prior knowledge that Pharaoh would disbelieve and disobey, and that Allah would punish him in the Fire, and likewise others among the disbelievers, and He informed of that. Then He does not punish except for disobedience, and there is no disobedience without command and prohibition. For if He had not commanded faith, its acquisition would not be obligatory nor its abandonment forbidden; and if He had not prohibited disbelief, its acquisition would not be forbidden nor its abandonment obligatory. And if faith were not obligatory and disbelief not forbidden, then the disbeliever would not be punished, nor would Hell be filled from him, and what He knew and informed of—of admitting him to Paradise and honoring him with reward—would not be realized. So He commanded and prohibited one from whom He knew disobedience, not that he would obey and abstain, but rather that he would abandon obedience and embrace disobedience. Then Allah punishes him and admits him to the Fire, and thus what He knew and informed of is realized. Without command and prohibition, that would not be realized. So His command and prohibition are for the realization of what He knew and informed of. And the command would only be as the Mu'tazila claim if He had commanded the disbeliever to obey him, not to disobey him; then the command and prohibition would be for making Him ignorant and belying Him. But if it is for disobedience, not obedience, then it is for the realization of what He knew and informed, not for making Him ignorant. The result is that one from whom Allah knows obedience, He commands him to obey so that obedience is realized, for there is no realization of it without command. And one from whom He knows disobedience, He commands him so that he does not do [obedience] but rather disobeys, for there is no realization of disobedience without command. What has fallen into the mouths of the pseudo-jurists of our time—that the benefit of obligation without a motive—they have learned it from the Mu'tazila and speak out of ignorance of where it leads to of false doctrine. It is established by understanding this summary that the command is in reality for the realization of what He knew, even though it appears to be for making Him ignorant. And Allah is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a response to a Mu'tazilite objection that commanding those whom God knows will disobey makes God ignorant (tajhil). The author (likely Imam Nasafi) explains that according to the opponents (the Ahl al-Sunna), command and prohibition are not meant to change God's knowledge but to realize what He has eternally known. If God had not commanded faith and prohibited disbelief, there would be no basis for reward and punishment, and thus His prior knowledge of who would obey and who would disobey would not be realized in actuality. For example, God knew Pharaoh would disbelieve and be punished; without command and prohibition, disobedience could not occur and punishment would not be just. Therefore, the command to the one known to disobey is not for him to obey, but to have the occasion for disobedience so that God's justice is manifested. The author contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite view, which would require that the command be for obedience, otherwise it is 'tajhil'. The author concludes that the widespread misconception among some pseudo-jurists ('mutafaqqiha') that obligation requires a motive (da'i) is learned from the Mu'tazila and leads to false doctrine. The core point is that God's command serves His eternal knowledge and justice, even if it appears to be a means of 'making ignorance'.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
تجهيل
تحقيق العلم
مشيئة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a command known to be disobeyed is pointless and makes God ignorant.
Correction: The command serves to actualize God's foreknowledge and just punishment; without it, disobedience and punishment would have no basis, thus God's knowledge would not be realized.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of God's command and prohibition to the one whom He knows will disobey?
A) To make him obey and avoid sin
B) To realize His foreknowledge and just punishment
C) To make Him ignorant of future events
D) To test his free will

Key Takeaway
God's commands and prohibitions are given to actualize His foreknowledge, enabling just reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that you know you will struggle to obey, does this lesson change how you view your potential failure? Will you still attempt obedience, trusting that even if you fail, the command serves a deeper purpose in Allah's knowledge? How can you cultivate that trust?
?
The author rebukes 'pseudo-jurists' who speak about obligation without understanding its true purpose. Have you ever dismissed a divine command as 'too hard' or 'unreasonable'? What would it mean for you to submit wholeheartedly, even when you don't see the wisdom?
?
This passage says that without divine commands, there would be no basis for punishment. Think of a time you disobeyed Allah. Do you realize that your disobedience only exists because Allah gave you the command? How does that awareness affect your gratitude for His mercy and your fear of His justice?

Refuting the Polytheists' Claim about God's Will
Al-Nasafi provides four interpretations and a concluding proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four interpretations Al-Nasafi gives for the polytheists' statement 'if Allah had willed' and the logical proof that refutes their claim.

Core Idea
The polytheists' claim about God's will is based on misinterpretation, not divine determinism.

Al-Nasafi addresses a famous objection: the polytheists used the verse 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' to argue they were following God's will. He explains four possible meanings of their statement and then provides a logical proof from the verse itself showing its true meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Nasafi uses a very structured enumeration here.
2
Identify the four interpretations
The passage presents four distinct interpretations of the polytheists' statement. Find each one and notice how Al-Nasafi introduces them with 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
Each interpretation reflects a different misconception about God's will.
3
Understand the concluding proof
After the four interpretations, Al-Nasafi provides a proof from the end of the verse. Read it carefully and see how it shows that the polytheists' literal reading would contradict the verse's conclusion.
This proof is key to the refutation.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Think about why Al-Nasafi's approach is effective. How does distinguishing between command and will, or between respite and approval, refute the polytheists' excuse?
The lesson guards against the misconception that the verse supports predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقه بقوله تعالى : سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا الآية فنقول: مراد الكفرة من ذكر المشيئة الأمر لا حقيقة المشيئة كما أخبر الله عنهم بقوله: ﴿ وَإِذَا فَعَلُوا فَاحِشَةً قَالُوا وجَدْنَا عَلَيْهَا آبَاءَنَا وَالله أمرنا بها والثاني أنهم لما أوعدوا في ذلك وأمهلوا ظنوا كذب الرسل عليهم السلام، وظنوا أن ذلك مما الله تعالى فيه رضا إذ أمهلهم ولم يأخذهم مع قدرته عليهم بل أراد عليهم النعم وفتح عليهم أبواب كل خير؛ فالإمهال والإحسان مع القدرة على الأخذ دليل الرضا بالصنع في الشاهد فظنوا في الغائب كذلك فكان معنى قوله: لو لم يرض الله تعالى ما أشركنا – والثالث أنهم جعلوا المشيئة حجة لهم فيما فعلوا وزعموا أن الله تعالى لو شاء ما أشركنا، فإذا أشركنا بمشيئته فلا يتصور أن يعاقبنا على شيء فعلنا بمشيئته، وظنوا أنفسهم معذورين في ذلك، فرد عليهم الله تعالى بقوله : قُلْ فَلِلَّهِ الْحُجَّةُ الْبَلِعَةُ والرابع أنهم قالوا ذلك احتجاجا لهم على المسلمين أن - على زعمكم - لو شاء الله ما أشركنا فكيف يعاقب عليه؟!. والدليل على أن الآية محمولة على بعض هذه الوجوه أنه قال في آخر الآية: فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَيْكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ ولو لم يكن صدر الآية محمولاً على ما تأولنا لنقض آخر الآية أولها وهذا محال ، ولا وجه لحملهم آخر الآية على مشيئة الجبر
As for his attachment to His statement: 'Those who associated partners will say...' the verse, we say: The polytheists meant by mentioning the will the command, not the reality of will, as Allah informed about them in His statement: 'And when they commit an immorality, they say, "We found our fathers doing it, and Allah has commanded us to do it."' And the second (interpretation) is that when they were threatened in that and given respite, they thought the messengers (peace be upon them) were lying, and they thought that that (respite) was something with which Allah was pleased, because He gave them respite and did not seize them despite His ability over them, rather He bestowed favors upon them and opened for them the doors of every good. So granting respite and beneficence with the ability to seize is evidence of pleasure with the action in the witnessed realm, so they thought similarly in the unseen. So the meaning of his statement: 'If Allah had not willed, we would not have associated partners' became clear. And the third (interpretation) is that they made the will a proof for themselves regarding what they did, and they claimed that if Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners. So since we have associated partners by His will, it is inconceivable that He would punish us for something we did by His will, and they thought themselves excused in that. So Allah refuted them with His statement: 'Say: "To Allah belongs the conclusive argument."' And the fourth (interpretation) is that they said that as an argument against the Muslims: 'According to your claim, if Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners, so how can He punish for it?!' And the proof that the verse is interpreted according to some of these aspects is that He said at the end of the verse: 'For if He had willed, He would have guided you all.' And if the beginning of the verse were not interpreted as we have understood, then the end of the verse would contradict its beginning, and that is impossible. And there is no way to interpret the end of the verse as referring to the will of predestination.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection based on the polytheists' statement in Surah al-An'am (6:148-149). The author provides four interpretations of what the polytheists meant by 'if Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners': (1) they meant 'command' rather than actual will; (2) they misinterpreted God's respite as approval; (3) they used it as an excuse for their actions, thinking they are immune to punishment; (4) they argued against Muslims that if God willed it, they should not be punished. Then he gives a proof: the end of the verse (فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَيْكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ) shows that if the beginning were taken literally, it would contradict the end, which is impossible. Therefore, the beginning must be understood in one of the mentioned ways, and the end cannot mean predestining will.

Key Terms
مشيئة
الحجة البالغة
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The polytheists' statement in the Quran proves that God predestines shirk and therefore they should not be punished.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that their statement is not an assertion of divine determinism but reflects multiple misinterpretations of God's will (e.g., confusing command with will, misinterpreting respite as approval, using it as an excuse). The concluding proof from the verse itself demonstrates that a literal reading would create a contradiction.

Checkpoint
How many interpretations does al-Nasafi provide for the polytheists' statement?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 4
D) 5

Key Takeaway
The polytheists' claim is refuted by distinguishing their intended meaning from literal determinism and by the logical structure of the verse itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself thinking that since God allowed it, He must be okay with it? How does this lesson challenge that excuse?
?
The polytheists mistook God's patience for approval. In your own life, are there times you have taken God's blessings for granted, assuming they mean He is pleased with you?
?
If you were in the polytheists' position, would you use God's will as an excuse to avoid taking responsibility? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of using divine decree to justify disobedience?

Divine Will and Human Freedom: Nasafi's Refutation of Compulsion
Nasafi refutes the claim that divine will compels human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that divine will compels human actions by arguing that God wills human voluntary acts, preserving human choice.

Core Idea
God's will does not compel; He wills that we act voluntarily.

The objection that divine will leaves no room for human freedom is a classic theological challenge. Nasafi addresses it head-on, insisting that we know with certainty that we are not compelled. This lesson examines his refutation, which turns on the nature of human intention and the parallel with divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Nasafi structures his argument with certainty, then a dilemma, then a parallel.
The passage ends mid-sentence with an analogy; do not worry about the incomplete part.
2
Identify the two parallel arguments
First, note the argument from human intention: the servant intends his voluntary act, so it is not compelled. Second, note the argument from divine knowledge: just as God is not compelled by His knowledge, the servant is not compelled.
These two arguments mirror each other.
3
Understand the dilemma on divine knowledge
Nasafi poses a dilemma to the opponent: if they say the servant is compelled because he cannot escape God's knowledge, they contradict their own doctrine. If they say no, their sophism is refuted.
This is a classic tautological argument.
4
Reflect on the unfinished analogy
The passage begins an analogy of a wise master disciplining a rebellious slave. Consider how this analogy would support Nasafi's point about God's will not being foolish or compelling.
The analogy is cut off, but the intended lesson is clear.

وما يقولون : إن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن إرادة الله تعالى فيصير مجبوراً. قلنا : ونعلم يقينًا أنه ليس بمجبور، وهذا لأنه يريد أن يوجد فعله الاختياري لا الاضطراري فيخرج اختياريًا لا اضطراريًا كما أراد. ثم يقال : والعبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن علم الله تعالى فيكون مجبوراً فإن قال: نعم. أبطل مذهبه وارتكب محالاً، وإن قال : لا ، أبطل شبهته، وكذا الله تعالى لا يخرج عن معلومه وليس بمضطر لما علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلم يكن هو مضطرًا ولا العبد ، فكذا إذا أراد أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره لم يصر مضطرًا. والله الموفق. وقولهم: إن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه فقد سبق عنه الجواب، وكذا قولهم: إن الأمر بما لا يريد سفه، فقد سبق عنه الجواب. ثم نقول: إن رجلاً من حكماء البشر لو كان له عبد عاص متمرد لا يطيعه فيما يأمره ولا ينزجر عما يزجره، فأخذ في تأديبه وتثقيفه، فرآه بعض أصحابه أو
And what they say: The servant cannot exit the will of God Almighty, so he becomes compelled. We say: And we know with certainty that he is not compelled, and this is because he (the servant) intends to bring about his voluntary act, not the compulsory one, so it comes out voluntary, not compulsory, as he intended. Then it is said: And the servant cannot exit the knowledge of God Almighty, so he is compelled. If he says: Yes, he invalidates his doctrine and commits an impossibility; and if he says: No, he invalidates his sophism. And similarly, God Almighty does not exit what is known to Him, and He is not compelled because He knows that He does what He does by His choice. So He is not compelled, nor is the servant. So likewise, when He wills that the servant does what he does by his choice, he does not become compelled. And God is the grantor of success. And their statement: Intending what is not approved is foolishness—its answer has already preceded. And likewise their statement: Commanding what one does not intend is foolishness—its answer has already preceded. Then we say: If a man of human wisdom had a rebellious, obstinate slave who does not obey him in what he commands nor desists from what he forbids, and he began to discipline and educate him, and some of his companions saw him and...
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that divine will compels human actions, leading to jabr (compulsion). The author begins by stating with certainty that man is not compelled. This is because man intends his own voluntary act, not a compulsory one, and it occurs as he intends. Then the author draws a parallel with divine knowledge: if one argues that man cannot escape God's knowledge and is thereby compelled, the opponent faces a dilemma: if they affirm compulsion, they invalidate their own doctrine (since they often deny compulsion) and commit an absurdity; if they deny it, their sophism is refuted. Similarly, God is not compelled by His own knowledge—He knows He acts by choice. Thus, divine will does not compel man, because God wills that man acts by his own choice. The author then notes that earlier answers already addressed the objection that intending something not approved is foolish, and commanding what one does not intend is foolish. Finally, an analogy is introduced (but incomplete) of a wise master disciplining a rebellious slave: onlookers might misinterpret the master's intention, but the wise master's actions are not foolish because they aim at reform. The analogy is cut off, but the intended point is clear: just as the master's will to discipline is not foolish, so God's will regarding apparent evils is not foolish.

جبر
إرادة
اختيار
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God's will and knowledge encompass all things, humans are forced to act and have no real choice.
Correction: Nasafi shows that God's will and knowledge do not compel, because God wills human voluntary acts and knows that they occur by choice.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that divine will compels man?
A) God's will does not extend to human actions.
B) Man's will overrides God's will.
C) God wills that man acts by his own choice, so no compulsion.
D) Compulsion only applies to animals, not humans.

Key Takeaway
Divine will does not compel human action because God wills that humans act by their own choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Analogy of the Master and the Disobedient Slave
Analogy defending divine will for disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of a master and a disobedient slave refutes the objection that God willing disbelief is foolish.

Core Idea
God's will for disbelief reveals truth and justice, not foolishness.

The author addresses a recurring objection: that willing what is displeasing is foolish. To clarify, he presents a nuanced analogy from human experience. By examining a master's response to a rebellious slave, we see that willing disobedience can be a means to expose the truth, not a sign of folly. This passage invites us to think about divine will in a more sophisticated way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and context
Read the Arabic passage and translation once. Note that the author begins by referencing earlier answers to objections before introducing the analogy.
The passage is dense; take time to understand the flow.
2
Identify the characters and situation
The analogy involves a wise master, a rebellious slave, and critics. Understand the dynamic: the master disciplines the slave, but critics blame him for harshness.
The master's character is wise, not arbitrary.
3
Analyze the two possible scenarios
The master commands the slave to do an errand. Consider both outcomes: if the slave obeys, the master's complaint is proven false; if he disobeys, it is proven true.
The dilemma forces the critics to commit.
4
Examine the critics' dilemma
If critics say the master wants obedience, they appear foolish; if they say he wants disobedience, their objection collapses. The author uses this to defend divine will for disbelief.
The analogy is a logical reductio ad absurdum.
5
Apply to divine will
Reflect on how the analogy parallels God's will for disbelief: it serves to manifest justice and the servant's guilt, not arbitrary foolishness.
This is a rational defense of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه فقد سبق عنه الجواب، وكذا قولهم: إن الأمر بما لا يريد ،سفه، فقد سبق عنه الجواب. ثم نقول: إن رجلاً من حكماء البشر لو كان له عبد عاص متمرد لا يطيعه فيما يأمره ولا ينزجر عما يزجره، فأخذ في تأديبه وتثقيفه، فرآه بعض أصحابه أو جماعة أقربائه وأوليائه، فلاموه على الضرب والتعذيب الذي رأوه منه ونسبوه إلى القساوة وقلة المرحمة وسوء الملكة فاعتذر إليهم وقال : إن عبدي هذا لا يطيعني : فيما آمره به وأنهاه عنه يستخف بأموري، فكذبوه في ذلك وزعموا أن هذا العبد موصوف بضد ما وصفته، وإنما الحامل لك على ضربه بعض ما أنت جبلت عليه من القسوة وطبعت عليه من غلظ الطبع والجفوة، فأراد أن يظهر عذره عندهم فأمر العبد ببعض مصالحه أفيريد أن يأتمر العبد ويطيعه فيظهر حينئذ كذبه في مقالته وقساوته وجفوته؟ أم يريد أن يعصيه فيثبت صدقه ويظهر براءة ساحته عما عرف به من الجفوة والقسوة؟ فإن قالوا: يريد أن يطيعه ظهر سفههم وعنادهم، وإن قالوا: يريد أن يعصيه بطلت شبهتهم، والله الموفق.
And their statement: Willing what is not pleasing is foolishness – the answer to it has already preceded. Likewise, their statement: Commanding what one does not will is foolishness – the answer to it has already preceded. Then we say: If a man from among the wise of mankind had a disobedient, rebellious slave who does not obey him in what he commands nor desist from what he forbids, so he began to discipline and correct him. Then some of his companions or a group of his relatives and close ones saw him, and they blamed him for the beating and punishment they witnessed from him, attributing it to harshness, lack of mercy, and bad character. So he apologized to them and said: This slave of mine does not obey me in what I command him and what I forbid him; he treats my affairs lightly. But they denied this and claimed that this slave is characterized by the opposite of what he described, and that the only reason for his beating is some of the harshness he was created with and the roughness of nature and severity he was stamped with. So he wanted to show his excuse to them, so he commanded the slave to do some of his affairs. Does he want the slave to carry out his command and obey him, thus showing his lie in his statement and his harshness and severity? Or does he want him to disobey him, thus proving his truthfulness and showing his innocence from the harshness and severity he was known for? So if they say: He wants him to obey him, their foolishness and obstinacy appear; and if they say: He wants him to disobey him, their objection is invalidated. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage uses an analogy to defend the idea that God may will disbelief without being unjust. The author first notes that the objection about willing unpleasing things has been answered before. Then he presents a scenario: a wise man has a rebellious slave and disciplines him. When criticized, the master orders the slave to do something. If the slave obeys, the master's complaint is proven false; if the slave disobeys, the master's complaint is proven true. The author then asks: does the master will obedience or disobedience? If the critics say he wills obedience, they show their foolishness; if they say he wills disobedience, their objection collapses. The analogy demonstrates that willing an action does not make one unjust if the action is used to reveal truth. Thus, God's will for disbelief serves to manifest His justice and the servant's guilt.

إرادة

معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy implies that God is like a human master with human emotions and flaws.
Correction: The analogy is a logical tool; it does not attribute human limitations to God but illustrates that willing disobedience can be rationally justified in a context of revealing truth and justice.

Checkpoint

A) The master is justified.
B) The critics appear foolish and obstinate.
C) The slave is punished.
D) The master is proven wrong.

Key Takeaway
The analogy demonstrates that willing disobedience can be a means to reveal truth and justice, thus defending the rationality of God's will for disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God truly wills the disbelief of some, what does that mean for your own call to invite others? Are you obeying His command to convey the truth even when you do not see its immediate effect?

Defining Qada': Divine Judgment
Author introduces qada' wa qadr and defines qada' as judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational meaning of qada' as divine judgment and its connection to God's creation of actions.

Core Idea
Qada' is not fate but God's judgment and decree over creation.

Before diving into the complex debates on predestination, the author establishes a key premise: since God creates all actions, He also decrees and determines them. Now, the discussion turns to the precise meaning of qada'. This lesson unpacks the first and most basic definition—qada' as divine judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then follow with the English translation. Notice the logical flow: from established premise to definition.
The passage has two clear parts: a connecting argument and a lexical definition.
2
Understand the argument's role
The author states that because it is established that God creates actions, it follows that He decrees their existence and moral qualities. This sets the foundation for why we are now discussing meanings.
The phrase 'so the need to speak about this matter falls away' shows the author is building on prior consensus.
3
Focus on the definition of qada'
The passage explicitly defines qada' as 'judgment' (hukm), illustrated by a judge's ruling. This is the first of several meanings
4
Contrast with common misunderstandings
Note that qada' here is not about blind fate but about God's just judgment. Many misconceptions arise when equating qada' with fatalistic determinism.
The commentary clarifies this distinction.

The Parable of the Master and the Disobedient Slave
A parable refuting an objection about divine justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a parable of a master and his disobedient slave refutes the objection that God is unjust in punishing human disobedience.

Core Idea
A master's justice is proven when his slave disobeys; likewise, God's justice is proven when we disobey.

In this lesson, we examine a powerful parable that the author uses to counter an objection against divine justice. The parable presents a scenario where a master is blamed for punishing his servant, but the logic of the situation reveals that the blame is misplaced. By analyzing the choices the master faces, we see how the objection collapses. This parable addresses a core issue in theodicy: does punishment stem from God's harshness or from our own disobedience?

Guided Path
1
Read the Parable
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full story.
The parable uses concrete imagery to make a logical point.
2
Identify the Two Options
Notice the master's dilemma: he commands the servant, and there are two possible outcomes – the servant obeys or disobeys. Each outcome leads to a different conclusion about the master's character.
3
Understand the Logical Trap
See how both options force the critics into a contradiction. If the servant obeys, the master's earlier claim is proven false. If the servant disobeys, the master's truthfulness is established and the critics' objection is invalidated.
This is the key refutation.
4
Connect to Divine Justice
Reflect on how the parable applies to God and humans. God commands us, and when we disobey, His justice is not diminished – rather, our disobedience justifies His punishment.
The analogy shows that punishment is a response to our actions, not God's harshness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جماعة أقربائه وأوليائه، فلاموه على الضرب والتعذيب الذي رأوه منه ونسبوه إلى القساوة وقلة المرحمة وسوء الملكة فاعتذر إليهم وقال : إن عبدي هذا لا يطيعني فيما آمره به وأنهاه عنه يستخف بأموري، فكذبوه في ذلك وزعموا أن هذا العبد موصوف بضد ما وصفته، وإنما الحامل لك على ضربه بعض ما أنت جبلت عليه من القسوة وطبعت عليه من غلظ الطبع والجفوة، فأراد أن يظهر عذره عندهم فأمر العبد ببعض مصالحه أفيريد أن يأتمر العبد ويطيعه فيظهر حينئذ كذبه في مقالته وقساوته وجفوته؟ أم يريد أن يعصيه فيثبت صدقه ويظهر براءة ساحته عما عرف به من الجفوة والقسوة؟ فإن قالوا: يريد أن يطيعه ظهر سفههم وعنادهم، وإن قالوا: يريد أن يعصيه بطلت شبهتهم، والله الموفق.
A group of his relatives and close ones blamed him for the beating and torture they saw from him and attributed it to harshness, lack of mercy, and bad disposition. He apologized to them and said: 'This servant of mine does not obey me in what I command him and forbid him; he treats my matters lightly.' They denied him in that and claimed that this servant is described with the opposite of what he described, and that what drove you to beat him is some of the harshness you are naturally disposed to and the roughness of nature and severity you are stamped with. So he wanted to show his excuse to them, so he commanded the servant with some of his interests. Does he want the servant to follow and obey him, so his lie in his statement, his harshness, and his severity become apparent? Or does he want him to disobey him so that his truthfulness is established and his innocence from the severity and harshness he was known for becomes apparent? If they say: 'He wants him to obey him,' their foolishness and obstinacy become apparent. If they say: 'He wants him to disobey him,' their false argument is invalidated. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument in the form of a parable. The author presents a scenario where a master beats his disobedient servant, and his relatives blame him, claiming that the beating is due to the master's own harsh nature rather than the servant's fault. The master denies this. To prove his point, the master orders the servant to perform a task. The master now faces two possibilities: he either wants the servant to obey, which would expose the master's previous claim about disobedience as a lie, or he wants the servant to disobey, which would confirm the master's truthfulness about the servant's disobedience. The objectors, when forced to choose, either must say the master wants obedience (making them foolish for blaming him) or must say he wants disobedience (which invalidates their entire objection). Thus, the parable demonstrates that the objectors' position is self-contradictory and cannot stand.

Key Terms
القسوة
المرحمة
الطاعة
المعصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parable is about a human master and has no bearing on divine justice.
Correction: The parable is an analogy for God's relationship with humans; it refutes the claim that God is unjust in punishing disobedience.

Checkpoint
In the parable, what do the objectors claim is the real reason the master beats the servant?
A) The servant's disobedience.
B) The master's inherent harshness and lack of mercy.
C) The servant's poor work performance.
D) The master's desire to test the servant.

Key Takeaway
The parable shows that the objection to divine justice fails because it forces the objector into a self-defeating choice: either admit the master is truthful or admit their own blame is baseless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or punishment, do you immediately blame God's harshness, or do you examine your own disobedience? What would it take for you to honestly look at your actions before accusing God?

The master's relatives saw the beating and assumed harshness. In your life, when you see others suffering, do you rush to assume injustice on God's part? How can you train yourself to pause and consider the possibility of divine wisdom?
?
If you were the slave in the parable, would you obey the master to prove him wrong, or would you disobey and confirm his claim? What does your answer reveal about your heart towards God's commands?

The Two Aspects of Qadr: Measure and Determination
The author divides qadr into two aspects and explains their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that qadr (predestination) comprises two distinct aspects: the inherent measure of things (al-hadd) and the determination of circumstances (al-bayan), and how both are from Allah.

Core Idea
Qadr is not just fate; it is both the measure of things and the determination of their time and place.

The author begins by clarifying the meaning of qadr (predestination) by dividing it into two clear aspects. Before discussing human actions, it is essential to understand what qadr truly means. This passage lays the foundation for a nuanced understanding of divine decree, avoiding fatalistic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the author's systematic division.
2
Identify the two aspects
The author explicitly lists two aspects of qadr. Find the key terms: al-hadd and al-bayan.
3
Understand the proof
Notice how the author argues that each aspect is beyond human capacity, proving they are from Allah.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that all actions are by Allah's decree. Connect this to the two aspects.

وأما القدر فهو على وجهين: أحدهما: الحد الذي يخرج عليه الشيء وهو جعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه من خير أو شر من حسن أو قبح من حكمة أو سفه وهو تأويل الحكمة أن يجعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه ويقدر كل شيء على ما هو الأولى به، ولهذا قلنا: إن خلق فعل الكفر ليس بسفه، وقال تعالى: إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَاهُ بِقَدَرٍ والثاني: بيان ما يقع عليه كل شيء من زمان ومكان وما له من الثواب والعقاب، فالأول قائم في أفعال الخلق من خروجه على ما لا تبلغه أوهامهم من الحسن والقبح ولا تقدره عقولهم، فثبت أنها خرجت على ذلك بالله تعالى، والثاني لا يحتمل بينهم أفعالهم من الزمان والمكان ولا يبلغه علمهم، فلا يحتمل أن يكون من ذلك الوجه لهم، وإذا عرف أن المراد من القضاء والقدر ما هو وقد أثبتنا بالدليل أن ذلك كله من الله تعالى صح قولنا: إن أفعالنا كلها بقضاء الله تعالى.
As for al-qadr (predestination), it is of two aspects: One of them: the limit upon which the thing comes out, and it is making every thing as it is of good or evil, of beauty or ugliness, of wisdom or foolishness, and it is the interpretation of wisdom that He makes every thing as it is and determines every thing as is most befitting it. For this reason we said: The creation of the act of disbelief is not foolishness. And He the Exalted said: 'Indeed, We have created everything by a measure (qadr).' And the second: the explanation of what every thing occurs upon of time and place and what it has of reward and punishment. So the first is established in the actions of creation from their coming out upon what their imaginations do not reach of beauty and ugliness and their intellects do not determine, so it is established that they came out upon that by Allah the Exalted. And the second, their actions from time and place are not encompassed by them nor does their knowledge reach it, so it is not possible that from that aspect they have it. And when it is known that what is intended by al-qada' and al-qadr is that, and we have established by evidence that all of that is from Allah the Exalted, our statement becomes correct: all our actions are by the qada' of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage defines qadr (predestination) by dividing it into two aspects. The first aspect is al-hadd (the limit or measure) upon which a thing comes into existence, which encompasses both good and evil, beauty and ugliness, wisdom and foolishness. This is the 'interpretation of wisdom' (ta'wil al-hikma) that Allah makes everything as it is and measures it as is most fitting. The author uses the example that creating the act of disbelief is not foolish, citing the Quranic verse 'We have created everything by a measure.' The second aspect is the determination of the specific time, place, and the reward or punishment that accompanies each action. The author then explains that the first aspect is beyond human intellect and imagination, so it is established that actions come out as they do by Allah. The second aspect, concerning time and place, is also beyond human knowledge, so humans cannot truly encompass it. The conclusion is that both qada' and qadr in these senses are from Allah, affirming that all actions are by Allah's decree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadr only means fate or predestination in a fatalistic sense
Correction: The author clarifies qadr includes both the inherent measure of things (good/evil, beauty/ugliness) and the determination of time and place.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of qadr according to the author?
A) The specification of time and place
B) The limit upon which a thing comes out, encompassing good and evil
C) The reward and punishment for actions
D) The free will of humans

Key Takeaway
Qadr has two aspects: the inherent measure of everything by Allah and the determination of its temporal and spatial circumstances, both of which are beyond human capacity and are from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, which part of qadr do you tend to ignore—the inherent nature of the act or its timing and consequences? How might acknowledging both change the way you evaluate your mistakes?
?
The passage says the first aspect of qadr is beyond human imagination. In what areas of your life are you trying to 'measure' things that only Allah can truly measure—like the ultimate good or evil of a situation? What would it look like to trust His measure instead?
?
If Allah has determined even the act of disbelief as part of His wise decree, does that unsettle your sense of justice? How can you reconcile divine wisdom with your own limited understanding, especially when facing evil in the world?

The Two Aspects of Qadar
al-Nasafi defines qadar and divides it into two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two aspects of qadar as defined by Imam al-Nasafi and how they establish that all actions are by Allah's decree.

Core Idea
Qadar has two aspects: Allah's wise measure of everything and His determination of its circumstances.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi defines the term 'qadar' (divine measure) and explains that it encompasses two complementary aspects. Understanding these aspects is crucial to grasp the Maturidi position on divine decree and human actions. Let's explore the text directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing how the author introduces two aspects after affirming that all actions are by Allah's decree.
The passage begins with a statement about qada and then defines qadar.
2
Identify the first aspect
Look for the definition: 'the measure upon which a thing issues' and the explanation about wisdom. The author argues that even creating disbelief is not foolishness.
This aspect is about the inherent nature and quality of each thing.
3
Identify the second aspect
Notice it concerns time, place, reward, and punishment, and why it cannot be attributed to creatures.
This aspect is about the external circumstances of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلها بقضاء الله تعالى أي بخلقه وتكوينه، وقد أقمنا الدلالة عليه بحمد الله تعالى. [معنى القدر]: وأما القدر فهو على وجهين: أحدهما: الحد الذي يخرج عليه الشيء وهو جعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه من خير أو شر من حسن أو قبح من حكمة أو سفه وهو تأويل الحكمة أن يجعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه ويقدر كل شيء على ما هو الأولى به، ولهذا قلنا: إن خلق فعل الكفر ليس بسفه، وقال تعالى: إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْتَهُ بِقَدَرٍ. والثاني: بيان ما يقع عليه كل شيء من زمان ومكان وما له من الثواب والعقاب، فالأول قائم في أفعال الخلق من خروجه على ما لا تبلغه أوهامهم من الحسن والقبح ولا تقدره عقولهم، فثبت أنها خرجت على ذلك بالله تعالى، والثاني لا يحتمل بينهم أفعالهم من الزمان والمكان ولا يبلغه علمهم، فلا يحتمل أن يكون من ذلك الوجه لهم، وإذا عرف أن المراد من القضاء والقدر ما هو وقد أثبتنا بالدليل أن ذلك كله من الله تعالى صح قولنا: إن أفعالنا كلها بقضاء الله تعالى.
All of them are by the decree of Allah the Exalted, meaning by His creation and bringing into being, and we have established the evidence for that by the praise of Allah the Exalted. [Meaning of qadar]: As for qadar, it is of two aspects: One of them: the measure upon which a thing issues, and it is the making of everything as it is, of good or evil, of beauty or ugliness, of wisdom or foolishness, and it is the interpretation of wisdom that everything is made as it is and everything is measured according to what is most appropriate for it. For this reason we said: The creation of the act of disbelief is not foolishness. And He said, the Exalted: 'Indeed, all things We created with a measure.' (54:49) And the second: the clarification of what every thing occurs upon of time and place and what it has of reward and punishment. The first is established in the actions of creatures, as they issue in a manner that their imaginations do not reach of beauty and ugliness and their intellects do not measure, so it is established that they issued in that manner by Allah the Exalted. And the second: it is not possible that it be between them concerning their actions of time and place, and their knowledge does not reach it, so it is not possible that it be from that aspect for them. And when it is known that what is meant by qada and qadar is that, and we have established by evidence that all of that is from Allah the Exalted, our statement becomes correct: Our actions are all by the decree of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qadar' and divides it into two aspects. The author begins by affirming that all actions are by Allah's decree, meaning His creation and bringing into being. He then introduces the meaning of qadar with two aspects. The first aspect is the measure or limit upon which a thing comes into existence; it is Allah's placing everything in its proper state, whether good or evil, beautiful or ugly, wise or foolish. This is the 'interpretation of wisdom' (ta'wil al-hikma), meaning that the wisdom of Allah consists in making everything as it is and measuring it according to what is most appropriate. The author argues that even creating the act of disbelief is not foolishness, citing the Quranic verse 'We created everything with a measure' (54:49). The second aspect is the determination of circumstances such as time, place, reward, and punishment for each action. The first aspect is beyond human imagination and intellect, proving that actions issue from Allah. The second aspect is beyond human knowledge, so it cannot be attributed to creatures. Therefore, it is established that all actions are by Allah's decree.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that qadar means only predetermination of actions without a wise measure or that it is simply arbitrary fate.
Correction: Qadar includes two aspects: a wise measure that gives everything its proper nature and a determination of external circumstances. Both are from Allah and reflect His wisdom.

Checkpoint

A) The measure of creation and the determination of time, place, reward, and punishment.
B) Predestination of good and evil and human free will.
C) The decree of Allah and human responsibility.
D) The eternal and temporal aspects.

Key Takeaway
Qadar consists of two aspects: the wise measure of all things and the determination of their circumstances, both from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems evil or unfair, do you truly believe that Allah has measured it with wisdom and appropriateness, even if you cannot perceive it? How does that belief affect your reaction in that moment? Commit to saying 'Allah knows best and His measure is wise' the next time you are tempted to complain.
?
Consider an act of disobedience you have committed. This lesson teaches that even such an act is created by Allah with a wise measure and is not foolishness. Does this realization make you more or less accountable for your sin? How will you strengthen your reliance on Allah's mercy while taking full responsibility for your choices starting today?
?
You often plan your future assuming you know what is best for you. But this lesson says that Allah measures everything according to what is most appropriate (الاولى). Are you willing to surrender your plans to His measure, even when it clashes with your desires? What is one specific plan you will consciously entrust to Allah's determination tonight before sleeping?

Divine Decree
