Allah's Decree and the Distinction Between Qada and Maqdi
Refutation of Mu'tazila on Allah not decreeing kufr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazila claim that Allah does not decree disbelief, and the crucial distinction between the decree itself (qada) and its object (maqdi).

Core Idea
Allah's decree is always wise, even when the decreed thing is evil.

The Mu'tazila argued that since disbelief is evil, Allah cannot decree it. But the Maturidi response clarifies a key conceptual distinction: there is a difference between the act of decreeing (which is always good and wise) and the thing decreed (which may be evil). This passage from Imam al-Nasafi explains that distinction and then outlines the proper attitude a believer should have toward Allah's decree of disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The passage opens with the Mu'tazila claim that Allah does not decree kufr because kufr is false. Note their premise: what Allah decrees must be true and correct.
2
Learn the key distinction: qada vs. maqdi
The author introduces that kufr is maqdi, not qada. Allah's decree (qada) is always true and wise, but the object decreed (maqdi) can be false and evil. The decreeing of this object is correct due to the wisdom in it.
3
Explore the correct attitude toward qada
The author then explains that being pleased with Allah's decree does not mean being pleased with evil actions. One can be pleased with the divine wisdom in decreeing disbelief while personally rejecting disbelief and not loving to commit it.
4
Reflect on the implications
This distinction protects against both fatalism and denial of divine decree. It affirms that Allah's decree is just and wise, while human responsibility for evil remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المعتزلة أن الله تعالى لا يقضي الكفر لأن الكفر متفاوت باطل، وقضاء الله تعالى حق وصواب وبه احتج الكعبي. وعندنا أن الكفر مقضي الله تعالى لا قضاؤه، وقضاؤه حق وصواب، ومقضيه باطل، وقضاء هذا المقضي صواب، لما فيه من الحكمة على ما بينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فمن رضي بأن يجعل الله تعالى الكفر باطلاً قبيحًا شرًا فقد رضى بقضاء الله تعالى، ومن لم يرض بذلك فهو غير راض بقضاء الله، ومن رضي بذلك ولم يرض أن يكون الكفر صفة له ولم يحب أن يفعله في نفسه فقد رضي بقضاء الله تعالى ولم يرضى بما يوجب مقته وتعذيبه.
The Mu'tazila claimed that Allah the Exalted does not decree disbelief because disbelief is evil and false. The decree of Allah the Exalted is truth and correctness, and with it al-Ka'bi argued. And with us, disbelief is the object of Allah's decree (maqdi), not His decree itself (qada). His decree is truth and correctness, and its object (maqdi) is false. The decree of this object (i.e., the act of decreeing disbelief) is correct, because of the wisdom in it, as we explained in the issue of the creation of actions. So whoever is pleased that Allah the Exalted makes disbelief false, ugly, and evil has been pleased with the decree of Allah the Exalted. And whoever is not pleased with that is not pleased with Allah's decree. And whoever is pleased with that but is not pleased that disbelief be a description of him and does not love to do it himself, has been pleased with Allah's decree but is not pleased with what necessitates His wrath and punishment.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila claim that Allah does not decree disbelief because disbelief is evil. The author introduces a crucial distinction between the decree itself (qada) and the object of the decree (maqdi). Allah's act of decreeing is always truth and wisdom, even if the thing decreed (e.g., disbelief) is false and evil. The decreeing of disbelief is therefore wise and correct, as explained in the discussion on the creation of actions. The author then clarifies the correct attitude: one can be pleased with Allah's decree (i.e., with the divine wisdom in decreeing disbelief) while simultaneously rejecting disbelief as a personal attribute and not loving it. This shows that being pleased with qada does not mean being pleased with evil actions themselves.

Key Terms
قضاء
مقضي
كفر
معتزلة
حكمة
رضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being pleased with Allah's decree means approving of evil deeds.
Correction: One can be pleased with Allah's decree (the act of decreeing) while rejecting evil actions themselves, as the passage explains.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the difference between qada (decree) and maqdi (the object of decree)?
A) Qada is Allah's attribute, maqdi is the action.
B) Qada is always true and wise, maqdi can be false and evil.
C) Qada is eternal, maqdi is temporal.
D) Qada is divine, maqdi is human.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi distinction between qada and maqdi upholds that Allah's decree is always wise and just, while the decreed object may be evil, and a believer can be pleased with the decree without approving of the evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you saw or experienced something evil that you know Allah decreed. Did you find yourself secretly resenting Allah's decree, or were you able to separate the evil act itself from the wisdom of Allah's decree? What does this passage require you to change in your heart?
?
The passage says whoever is pleased that Allah makes disbelief false, ugly, and evil is pleased with Allah's decree. Are you truly pleased that Allah defines disbelief as evil? Or do you find yourself defending or excusing some forms of disbelief? Be honest about where your heart stands.
?
You claim to be pleased with Allah's decree, but do you also avoid being pleased with the evil itself? Is there an evil act you secretly admire or wish you could commit? What would it mean for you to be pleased with the decree but not with the maqdi?

Refuting al-Ka'bi's Hadith Argument on Qada
The author refutes al-Ka'bi's hadith argument by contextualizing it and exposing inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi author refutes al-Ka'bi's misuse of a hadith about being pleased with qada, distinguishing between afflictions and moral evil.

Core Idea
The hadith about being pleased with qada applies to afflictions, not to sins.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili scholar, cites a prophetic hadith commanding contentment with Allah's decree to argue against the orthodox position on qada. The author responds by clarifying the hadith's true reference: it concerns illnesses and calamities, not moral evil. He also exposes the Mu'tazila's inconsistent stance on eternal punishment, forcing al-Ka'bi to confront his own selective use of scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the hadith cited by al-Ka'bi and the author's immediate qualification.
Pay attention to the phrase 'the true meaning of the report is concerning illnesses and calamities'.
2
Identify the author's main argument
The author argues that the hadith is specifically about afflictions, not all decrees. He then points out a contradiction: the Mu'tazila believe that eternity in Hell is from Allah's decree, so they should be pleased with it according to their own logic.
The reductio ad absurdum forces al-Ka'bi to apply his own principle consistently.
3
Understand the clarification about disbelief
The author explains that the disbeliever is actually pleased with his disbelief, while it is afflictions that people find unpleasant. This further supports that the hadith pertains to afflictions.
The author distinguishes between the pleasure a disbeliever has in his disbelief and the dislike of calamities.
4
Reflect on the selective quoting criticism
The author ends by criticizing al-Ka'bi for ignoring widespread hadith that would counter his view. Consider why selective proof-texting is a weak argumentative strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الكعبي بقول النبي عليه السلام: من لم يرض بقضائي ولم يصبر على بلائي فليطلب ربا سواي. وقد بينا أنا رضينا بقضاء الله تعالى وأعلمنا كيفيته على أن حقيقة الخبر في الأمراض والمصائب ألا ترى أن الخلود في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة إذ لا يثبتون الله تعالى تخليدًا سوى معنى الخلود، فليرض به الكعبي لنفسه، وإلا فليطلب ربا سواه. يحققه أن الكفر عندنا وإن كان بقضاء الله تعالى إلا أن من قضى عليه ذلك یرضی به ويتمسك به تمسكًا لا يرضى بالزوال عنه، وإنما الذي لا يرضى به المقضي عليه ويضجر منه في الأحايين هو الأمراض والمصائب، والمعتزلة لا يرضون بها إلا بعوض فليطلبوا ربًّا سوى من قضى بها عليهم، ثم العجب من الكعبي حيث سمع هذا الحديث فأخذ به ولم يسمع ما استفاض واشتهر وهو قوله
Al-Ka'bi argued with the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree and does not bear with patience My affliction, let him seek a lord other than Me.' And we have explained that we are pleased with the decree of Allah the Exalted and we know its modality, but the true meaning of the report is concerning illnesses and calamities. Do you not see that eternity in the Fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, since they do not affirm any eternal punishment from Allah other than the meaning of eternity? So let al-Ka'bi be pleased with it for himself, otherwise let him seek a lord other than Him. It verifies that disbelief, in our view, even if it is by the decree of Allah the Exalted, yet the one upon whom it is decreed is pleased with it and clings to it with a clinging that is not pleased to be removed from it. But that which the one decreed upon is not pleased with and is annoyed by at times is illnesses and calamities. And the Mu'tazila are not pleased with them except with compensation, so let them seek a lord other than the One who decreed them upon them. Then the wonder is at al-Ka'bi: he heard this hadith and adopted it, but did not hear what is widespread and well-known, which is his saying...
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage addresses al-Ka'bi's use of a prophetic hadith to argue against the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on divine decree. The author argues that the hadith refers specifically to afflictions like illnesses and calamities, not to moral evil. He points out an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila: they believe that eternity in Hell is from Allah's decree, so they should be pleased with it according to their own logic. The author then explains that the disbeliever is actually pleased with his disbelief, while it is afflictions that people naturally dislike. The hadith commands patience with afflictions, not approval of sin. The final remark criticizes al-Ka'bi for selectively quoting the hadith while ignoring more well-known texts.

قضاء
بلاء
رضا
حديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about being pleased with qada requires approval of all decrees, including sins and disbelief.
Correction: The author shows it refers specifically to afflictions like illnesses and calamities, not moral evil.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct interpretation of the hadith 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree...'?
A) It applies to all decrees including sins.
B) It applies specifically to illnesses and calamities.
C) It only applies to the decree of disbelief.
D) It is a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
The hadith about being pleased with qada is about afflictions, not moral evil; selective quoting can be refuted by pointing out logical inconsistencies and other texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity, do you find yourself internally complaining or feeling entitled to compensation, like the Mu'tazila? This lesson exposes that such an attitude is akin to seeking another lord. Will you sincerely repent and strive to accept afflictions as from Allah, without demanding compensation?
?
The author distinguishes between being pleased with disbelief (which the disbeliever loves) and being pleased with afflictions (which we naturally dislike). In your own heart, do you ever 'cling' to a sin as if you are pleased with it, not wanting it to be removed? This lesson warns that such pleasure in sin is contrary to true faith. What specific sin are you reluctant to abandon, and what concrete step will you take today to start hating it and seeking its removal?
?
Al-Ka'bi selectively used a hadith to support his view while ignoring other texts. Are there verses or hadith you conveniently overlook because they would challenge your lifestyle or beliefs? Name one such text and commit to living by it this week, explaining how you will implement it.

No Excuse in Divine Decree: Actions Are by Choice
Argument that divine decree does not excuse sin because actions are chosen freely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine decree does not excuse sin, based on the Maturidi argument that human actions are performed by free choice and not compelled by divine attributes.

Core Idea
God's decree creates the context for actions, not the compulsion to do them.

This passage tackles one of the most common objections to predestination: if God decrees everything, why are sinners to blame? Imam al-Nasafi (or the author) argues that divine creation, will, decree, and predestination do not necessitate compulsion. Instead, they are like the times and places in which actions occur — necessary conditions, but not causes. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Notice the key terms: تخليق, إرادة, قضاء, قدر, اضطرار, اختيار, عذر.
The passage is a continuous argument with several sub-points.
2
Identify the main thesis
The passage begins by stating that there is no excuse in these divine attributes. Underline the key negation: لا عذر لأحد.
3
Analyze the analogy with times/places
The author compares divine decree to the creation of times and places. Why does this analogy refute the fatalist excuse?
Times and places are required for actions but do not force the action.
4
Consider the psychological argument
The passage argues that people do not actually think about divine decree at the moment of sin. How does this strengthen the case?
If decree were the true cause, it would be in their conscious intent.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by rejecting reliance on God's mercy or knowledge as excuses. What is the overall message for personal responsibility?
Divine attributes do not remove human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعملوا أن لا عذر لأحد في التخليق والإرادة والقضاء والقدر لأن هذه المعاني لم تجعلهم مضطرين إلى ما فعلوا بل فعلوا ما فعلوا مختارين فصار خلق الفعل وإرادته والقضاء به وتقديره كخلق الأوقات والأمكنة التي تقع فيها الأفعال ولا تقع بدونها، ولم يصر تخليق شيء من ذلك عذرا لأنه لا يوجب اضطرارهم وكذا هذا ، ولأنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم وقت الفعل أنهم يفعلون لأجله فكان الاعتذار به والاحتجاج باطلاً، ولو كان لهم به الاحتجاج لكان بالعلم والتقوية ونحوهما احتجاج، ولأنه لا عذر لهم بأنه علمه بما يكون منهم، وبأنهم فعلوا ما فعلوا معتمدين على كرمه وجوده خلقهم مع
Then know that there is no excuse for anyone in (the matter of) creation (takhliq), will (irada), decree (qada'), and predestination (qadr), because these meanings did not make them compelled (mudtarrin) to what they did. Rather, they did what they did as free choosers (mukhtarin). Thus, creating the act, willing it, decreeing it, and predestining it became like creating the times and places in which actions occur and without which they do not occur. And creating any of that does not become an excuse because it does not necessitate their compulsion. And likewise this. And because none of that occurred to their minds at the time of action, that they act for its sake; thus, making an excuse by it and using it as an argument is invalid. And if they could argue by it, they would argue by knowledge and strengthening (al-quwwa) and the like. And because there is no excuse for them in that He knew what would be from them, and that they did what they did relying on His generosity and bounty while He created them with...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage provides a key argument against using divine decree as an excuse for sin. It states that divine attributes of creation, will, decree, and predestination do not compel human actions; rather, humans act by their own choice. The analogy is given: creating an act and decreeing it is like creating the times and places in which actions occur—necessary conditions but not causes of the action itself. Just as times and places do not compel actions, divine decree does not compel. Also, people do not actually think about these divine attributes at the moment of committing sin; they act for other reasons, so claiming divine decree as an excuse is invalid. Moreover, if one could argue by divine decree, they might argue by God's knowledge and power, which would be absurd because knowledge doesn't necessitate action. Finally, the passage mentions that sinners cannot claim they relied on God's mercy because that would be presumptuous. This refutes the fatalist excuse.

إرادة

اضطرار
اختيار
عذر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in divine decree (qadr) means that all actions are forced, so sinners are not responsible.
Correction: The passage explains that divine decree does not compel actions; people act by free choice, and decree is merely a condition like time and place.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is divine decree not a valid excuse for sin?
A) Because decree does not exist.
B) Because decree compels humans to act against their will.
C) Because decree does not compel humans; they act by free choice.
D) Because humans are not aware of decree at the time of action, so they cannot use it as an excuse.

Key Takeaway
Divine decree creates the context for human actions but does not compel them; therefore, humans are morally responsible for their choices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever silently blame your circumstances, upbringing, or even God's decree? How does this passage demand you take full responsibility for your choice? Commit to one specific sin you will stop excusing today.
?
Are there any sins you have normalized by telling yourself 'it's my nature' or 'God willed it'? Write down one such sin and what you will do today to stop treating it as inevitable.
?
The author says at the moment of sin, people do not think of decree as the cause. Think of a specific sin you committed. What were you actually thinking about? How does recalling that expose your excuse as false?

The Hadith on Qadar as an Essential of Faith
Nasafi cites a hadith on the four essentials of faith and warns against selective belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in qadar (both its good and evil) is a foundational pillar of faith according to the Prophet, and that selective belief is a sign of disbelief.

Core Idea
Belief in qadar is one of four essentials; partial belief is disbelief.

Nasafi begins by citing a Prophetic hadith that lists qadar as one of four essential beliefs. He then identifies a dangerous practice: accepting only what suits one’s desires. This lesson examines that hadith and its implication for the qadar excuse.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic passage containing the hadith once, then its literal translation. Notice that the Prophet lists four qualities that define a believer.
2
Identify the four essentials
Find the four items mentioned: testimony of tawhid and prophethood, belief in resurrection after death, and faith in qadar, its good and evil.
The hadith emphasizes that hiding even one of these constitutes disbelief.
3
Understand the warning about selective acceptance
Nasafi comments that some people take what agrees with their desire and leave what contradicts it. This shows that using qadar as an excuse is a form of selective belief.
This sets up the refutation to come.
4
Reflect on the connection to the excuse of qadar
Consider how the misuse of qadar—blaming God for one's sins—contradicts the complete belief in qadar required by the hadith.
Belief in qadar includes both good and evil; using it to excuse sin is a distortion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه السلام: "القدر خيره وشره من الله وحديث سعد بن أبي وقاص به عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال : أربع من كن فيه فهو مؤمن ومن جاء بثلاث وكتم واحدة فقد كفر، شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وأني رسول الله، وأنه مبعوث من بعد الموت، وإيمان بالقدر خيره وشره فمن جاء بثلاث وكتم واحدة فقد كفر لكنه رجل يأخذ ما وافق هواه ويترك ما خالف ذلك، ثم اعملوا أن لا عذر لأحد في التخليق والإرادة والقضاء والقدر لأن هذه المعاني لم تجعلهم مضطرين إلى ما فعلوا بل فعلوا ما فعلوا مختارين فصار خلق الفعل وإرادته والقضاء به وتقديره كخلق الأوقات والأمكنة التي تقع فيها الأفعال ولا تقع بدونها، ولم يصر تخليق شيء من ذلك عذرا لأنه لا يوجب اضطرارهم وكذا هذا، ولأنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم وقت الفعل أنهم يفعلون لأجله فكان الاعتذار به والاحتجاج باطلاً، ولو كان لهم به الاحتجاج لكان بالعلم والتقوية ونحوهما احتجاج، ولأنه لا عذر لهم بأنه علمه بما يكون منهم، وبأنهم فعلوا ما فعلوا معتمدين على كرمه وجوده خلقهم مع والغناء عن تعذيبهم، وعلى أنه عفو غفور، وعلى أنه ليس له في طاعتهم نفع ولا عليه في معصيتهم ضرر فكذا هذا، على أنا أقمنا الدلالة على أن لهم فعلاً مختارون فيعاقبهم على ذلك، والله الموفق.
Upon him be peace: 'Qadar, its good and its evil, is from Allah.' And the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas from the Prophet, upon him be peace, that he said: 'Four (qualities) – whoever possesses them is a believer, and whoever comes with three and conceals one has disbelieved: the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah, and that he is resurrected after death, and faith in qadar, its good and its evil. So whoever comes with three and conceals one has disbelieved' – but (such) a man takes what agrees with his desire and leaves what contradicts that. Then know that there is no excuse for anyone in (matters of) creation, will, decree, and predestination, because these meanings did not compel them to do what they did; rather, they did what they did as free choosers. So the creation of the act, its willing, decreeing, and predestining are like the creation of the times and places in which acts occur and without which they do not occur. And the creation of none of that becomes an excuse because it does not necessitate compulsion for them, and likewise this. And because none of that occurred to their minds at the time of the act that they do it for its sake, so the excuse by it and the argument are null. And if they had an argument by it, it would be an argument by knowledge, strengthening, and the like. And because there is no excuse for them in that He knew what would be from them, and that they did what they did relying on His generosity and sufficiency from punishing them, and on His being Pardoning, Forgiving, and on that there is no benefit to Him in their obedience nor harm to Him in their disobedience – so likewise this. Moreover, we have established the proof that they have an act while being free choosers, so He punishes them for that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the excuse that divine predestination (qadar) compels humans to sin, thereby absolving them of responsibility. Nasafi begins by citing a hadith from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas reporting the Prophet's statement that belief in qadar—both its good and evil—is one of four essential qualifications of faith; whoever hides one of these has disbelieved. He then identifies a common misuse: a person selectively accepts what suits his desire and rejects what contradicts it. Nasafi proceeds to argue that creation (takhlīq), will (irāda), decree (qaḍā'), and predestination (qadar) do not force anyone to act. Humans choose their acts freely; therefore, these divine attributes are like the creation of times and places—they are necessary conditions for action but do not compel. Since people do not consciously commit acts because of these attributes, using them as an excuse is invalid. He further refutes arguments based on God's foreknowledge or reliance on His mercy, as such considerations do not remove human choice and responsibility. The passage concludes by affirming that rational proof has been established that humans possess voluntary acts for which they are justly punished.

خير
شر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in qadar is optional or one can believe only in good qadar
Correction: The Prophet explicitly includes belief in both good and evil qadar as essential; hiding any one of the four is disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited by Nasafi, how many essential qualities must a person possess to be a believer?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Belief in qadar, both its good and evil, is a non-negotiable pillar of faith; picking and choosing undermines faith itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself accepting a blessing as from Allah but blaming your own actions when something bad happens, thereby rejecting the evil of qadar? How does this hadith confront your heart about that inconsistency? What can you do to submit completely to Allah's decree, whether it brings good or evil?
?
The hadith warns that concealing any one of the four essentials is disbelief. Is there any aspect of qadar that you have been avoiding, distorting, or explaining away because it makes you uncomfortable? What would it cost you to embrace it fully, and how would that change your relationship with Allah?
?
Nasafi says some people take what agrees with their desire and leave what contradicts it. In what areas of your life—your understanding of Allah's decree, your concept of mercy and justice, or your attitude toward trial—have you been guilty of this selective belief? What concrete step can you take today to repent from this attitude and submit to the whole truth?

Divine Decree

Why Qadar Does Not Excuse Sin
Nasafi argues that divine creation, will, decree, and predestination do not compel humans; thus no excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi’s core argument that divine predestination does not compel human actions, and therefore cannot be used as an excuse for sin.

Core Idea
God’s creation of acts does not compel; humans choose freely.

Having established the importance of believing in qadar, Nasafi now refutes the misuse of qadar as an excuse. He argues that creation, will, decree, and predestination do not force anyone to act; they are like times and places—necessary conditions, not causes of compulsion. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'thumma i'lamū' to the end, along with its translation. This contains Nasafi's full refutation.
2
Understand the key claim: no compulsion
Nasafi states that divine attributes (creation, will, decree, predestination) did not make people compelled (mudtarrīn). They acted as free choosers (mukhtārīn).
This is the central point.
3
Examine the analogy with time and place
Nasafi compares the creation of the act to the creation of times and places: necessary conditions for action, but not causes that force the action.
Just as a place does not force you to sin, so divine predestination does not force you.
4
Note the psychological and logical refutations
Nasafi adds that people do not think of qadar at the moment of action, and that arguments from foreknowledge or God's mercy are invalid excuses.
This strengthens the refutation.
5
Affirm human accountability
The passage concludes by stating that proof has been established that humans have free acts and are justly punished.
This is the practical outcome of the argument.

Mu'tazili Interpretation of Guidance and Misguidance
Nasafi contrasts the Maturidi and Mu'tazilite views on guidance and misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili interpretation of divine guidance and misguidance and how it differs from the Maturidi view presented by Nasafi.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites reinterpret guidance as clarification, not creation, to preserve human agency.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a key intra-Islamic theological debate on divine guidance and misguidance. After stating the Maturidi position that Allah creates all human acts, Nasafi outlines the Mu'tazilite alternative, which reinterprets scriptural attributions of guidance and misguidance to Allah. Understanding this contrasting view is essential for grasping the broader kalam discourse on human responsibility and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the contrast between the Maturidi premise and the Mu'tazili response.
The Arabic may have OCR artifacts; refer to the translation for clarity.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the Maturidi claim that Allah creates the act of guidance and misguidance, and the Mu'tazili rejection of that. The Mu'tazilites deny that Allah creates human acts.
3
Examine the Mu'tazili reinterpretations
See how the Mu'tazilites explain verses attributing guidance to Allah as 'clarification of the path,' and verses attributing misguidance as attributing acts to their cause or condition.
This preserves human agency while maintaining Allah's role as ultimate cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الهدى والإضلال لما ثبت أن الله تعالى خلق أفعال العباد فكان هو الذي خلق فيهم فعل الاهتداء وفعل الضلال، فوجد منه الهدى والإضلال. رأى المعتزلة في الهدى والإضلال وعند المعتزلة لما لم يجز أن يخلق أفعالهم لم يوجد منه خلق فعل الاهتداء ولا خلق فعل الضلال، ويقولون ما أضيف إلى الله تعالى من الهداية فالمراد منه بيان طريق الدين لا تخليق فعل الاهتداء، وما أضيف إليه من الإزاغة والإضلال والخذلان والطبع بقوله: ( وَطَبَعَ اللهُ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ ) والمد بقوله: ﴿ وَيَمُدُّهُمْ فِي طُغْيَانِهِمْ يَعْمَهُونَ ) فلأن السبب الذي كان به منهم الأفعال كان من الله تعالى كالقدر والآلات، وقد تضاف الأفعال إلى مسببها، وكذا في الهداية والعصمة قد تضاف على هذا الطريق، وقد يقولون بأنها تضاف إليه على حسب إضافة الأشياء إلى شروطها التي توجد هي عندها إذ لولا المحنة والتكليف لما وجدت منهم هذه
The discourse on guidance (hudā) and misguidance (ḍalāl). Since it is established that Allah, exalted, created the acts of servants, so He it is who created in them the act of guidance and the act of misguidance; thus guidance and misguidance are from Him. The Mu'tazilites' view on guidance and misguidance: According to the Mu'tazilites, since it is not permissible that He creates their acts, there is from Him no creation of the act of guidance nor creation of the act of misguidance. And they say: what is attributed to Allah of guidance—the intended meaning is the clarification of the path of religion, not the creation of the act of guidance. And what is attributed to Him of causing deviation, misguidance, abandonment, and sealing according to His saying: 'And Allah has sealed their hearts' and prolonging according to His saying: 'And He prolongs them in their transgression, wandering blindly'—then because the cause from which acts proceed on their part is from Allah, like power (qudra) and instruments (ālāt), and acts may be attributed to their cause. Likewise, in guidance and protection (ʿiṣma), they may be attributed in this way. And they may say that they are attributed to Him according to the attribution of things to their conditions in whose presence they occur—since were it not for trial and obligation, these would not have occurred from them.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the Mu'tazilite position on divine guidance (hudā) and misguidance (ḍalāl) within a larger Maturidi framework. Nasafi first asserts the Ash'ari/Maturidi premise that Allah creates all human acts, including the act of guidance and the act of misguidance. Then he presents the Mu'tazilite view: since they hold that Allah does not create human acts, He does not create the act of guidance or misguidance. They reinterpret scriptural attributions of guidance to Allah as meaning 'clarification of the way' (bayān ṭarīq al-dīn), not creation of the act of being guided. Similarly, verses attributing misguidance, sealing of hearts, and prolonging in transgression are explained by saying that the cause (sabab) from which acts arise—like qudra (ability) and ālāt (instruments)—is from Allah, and acts may be metaphorically attributed to their cause. They also analogize the attribution of guidance and protection (ʿiṣma) to Allah to the attribution of things to their conditions (shurūṭ): if trial and obligation were absent, these acts would not occur. Thus, the Mu'tazilites preserve human agency while maintaining that all efficient causation is from Allah.

Key Terms
هدى
إضلال
معتزلة
خلق الأفعال
بيان طريق الدين
إضافة الأفعال إلى مسببها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilites deny any involvement of Allah in guidance.
Correction: They affirm guidance as clarification of the path, but deny that Allah creates the act of being guided in the servant.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazilites interpret the Qur'anic attribution of guidance to Allah?
A) They affirm that Allah creates the act of being guided in the servant.
B) They interpret it as Allah's clarification of the path of religion, not creation of the act of guidance.
C) They reject all such attributions as metaphorical.
D) They attribute guidance solely to human effort.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites preserve human agency by interpreting divine guidance as clarification and misguidance as attributable to Allah only as cause or condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Both the Maturidi and Mu'tazilite views attribute guidance ultimately to Allah, but differ on whether He creates the very act of acceptance. When you reflect on a moment you were guided to truth, do you sense that Allah created your acceptance, or that He clarified while you chose? Which view resonates with your experience, and what does that say about your understanding of your own responsibility?
?
The Mu'tazilites explain misguidance as stemming from conditions Allah set (trial and obligation). Is there an area of your life where you blame your circumstances or inherent difficulties for your spiritual state, rather than seeing them as conditions demanding your active response? What would change if you fully owned your choices while acknowledging Allah's causal role?
?
This passage shows that even those who deny predestination still affirm Allah as the ultimate cause and condition of every act. If every moment of guidance and misguidance ties back to Him, how does that reshape your gratitude for being guided and your fear of misguidance? Are you living with a constant sense of dependence on Him in your spiritual state, or do you take it for granted?

Mu'tazilite Interpretations: Tasmīyah and Wujūd
Examination of two Mu'tazilite interpretations of divine misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite interpretations of God's misguidance as naming (tasmīyah) and finding (wujūd), and how they aim to preserve human free will.

Core Idea
God's misguidance is a declaration of one's state, not a creation of disbelief.

The Mu'tazilites sought to reconcile divine verses about God leading astray with human responsibility. They reinterpreted 'iḍlāl' as either naming someone misguided (tasmīyah) or finding them already in that state (wujūd). This lesson examines their linguistic and poetic arguments from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the first part of the Arabic passage which presents the Mu'tazilite view. Then read the literal translation to understand the gist.
Notice the poetic citations from al-Kumayt and Ṭarafa.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two distinct interpretations of God's misguidance: tasmīyah (naming) and wujūd (finding). Identify where each is introduced.
The phrase 'ربما يقولون معناه وجده ضالاً' indicates the wujūd interpretation.
3
Analyze the poetic evidence
Look at the lines from al-Kumayt and Ṭarafa. How do they support the tasmīyah interpretation? The poet uses 'akfarūnī' to mean 'they named me a disbeliever', not 'they made me a disbeliever'.
The linguistic usage demonstrates that verbs like 'adalla' can have a declarative meaning.
4
Understand the theological motive
Why did the Mu'tazilites reinterpret these verses? To avoid attributing the creation of disbelief to God, thus preserving human free will and divine justice.
This interpretation is attributed to al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazilite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقولون ما يضاف إليه من الهداية فالمراد منه هداية المحسنين طريق الجنة في الآخرة، وهذا التأويل محكي عن الجبائي، ويقولون في معنى الإضلال المضاف إلى الله تعالى : إنه ليس بتخليق فعل الضلال بل هو تسميته إياه ضالاً يقال: أضله أي سماه ضالاً. قال الكميت: فطائفة قد أكفروني بحبكم وطائفة قالوا مسيء ومذنب ثم قوله: أكفروني أي سموني كافراً. وقال طرفة: وما زال شربي الراح حتى أشرني خليلي وحتى ساءني بعض ذلك أي سماني بشرير، أو ربما يقولون: معناه وجده ضالاً يقال: أنحلت فلانا وأجبنته أي وجدته نحيلاً جبانًا. ونحن لما أقمنا الدلالة على أن الله تعالى خالق أفعال العباد كان هاديًا لتخليقه فعل الاهتداء، ومضلاً لتخليقه فعل الضلال
And perhaps they say that what is attributed to Him of guidance means the guidance of the virtuous to the path of Paradise in the Hereafter, and this interpretation is related from al-Jubba'i. And they say regarding the meaning of misguidance (iḍlāl) attributed to God Almighty: It is not creating the act of straying (ḍalāl), but rather His naming him 'misguided' (ḍāll). It is said: 'He misguided him' meaning He named him misguided. Al-Kumayt said: 'A faction has declared me disbeliever (akfarūnī) for my love of you, and a faction said: sinful and guilty.' Then his statement: 'akfarūnī' meaning they named me a disbeliever (kāfir). And Ṭarafa said: 'And my drinking of wine has not ceased until my friend called me evil (asharra-nī) and until that displeased me.' That is, he named me evil (sharīr). Or perhaps they say: Its meaning is He found him misguided. It is said: 'I made him emaciated (anḥaltu) and I made him cowardly (ajbantu)' meaning I found him emaciated and cowardly. And we, when we have established the proof that God Almighty is the creator of the actions of servants, He is the Guide (hādī) by His creating the act of guidance, and the Misguider (muḍill) by His creating the act of straying.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents two interpretations of divine guidance and misguidance from the Mu'tazilite perspective, followed by the author's Ash'ari affirmation. The first interpretation, attributed to al-Jubba'i, holds that guidance attributed to God refers specifically to leading believers to Paradise in the afterlife, not to guiding in this world. The second deals with misguidance (iḍlāl): it is not the creation of disbelief but rather God's act of naming someone 'misguided' (tasmīyah). The author supports this linguistic usage with poetry: al-Kumayt's line where 'akfarūnī' means 'they named me a disbeliever', and Ṭarafa's line where 'asharra-nī' means 'he named me evil'. Another possible meaning is 'wujūd' (finding), as in 'anḥaltu' meaning 'I found him emaciated'. Then the author states the Ash'ari position: having established that God creates all human actions, He is the Guide because He creates the act of guidance, and the Misguider because He creates the act of straying.

Key Terms
إضلال
تسمية
وجود
ضال
هداية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's misguidance means He directly creates disbelief in a person.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue it merely declares or finds the person's existing state, not creating disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite interpretation of tasmīyah, what does 'God misguided him' mean?
A) God created disbelief in him.
B) God named him misguided.
C) God forced him to stray.
D) God abandoned him.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites reinterpreted divine misguidance as a declarative act (naming or finding) to avoid attributing the creation of disbelief to God, thereby preserving human free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazilites say God 'names' you misguided based on your own state, does that make you feel more responsible for your sins or less? What would change in your daily choices if you truly believed that God's declaration of your state is a direct reflection of your own actions?
?
Think of a time you rationalized your sins by blaming your circumstances or upbringing. The Mu'tazilites insisted that God does not create disbelief in you—you choose it. Are you willing to stop making excuses and own that choice? What would it cost you to live with that accountability?

Ash'ari Affirmation: God Creates Guidance and Misguidance
Affirmation of God as creator of guidance and misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that God is the Guide (Hādī) and Misguider (Muḍill) because He creates the acts of guidance and straying in His servants.

Core Idea
God's guidance and misguidance are real creative acts, not mere declarations.

Having presented the Mu'tazilite interpretations, Imam Nasafi now states the Ash'ari position succinctly. The key premise is that God is the creator of all human actions. Consequently, when a person performs an act of guidance, God is the one who creates that act, making Him truly the Guide. Similarly, when a person strays, God creates that act of straying, making Him the Misguider.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second part of the Arabic passage which states the Ash'ari view. Pay attention to the logical structure: 'when we have established... then He is the Guide...'
The phrase 'لما أقمنا الدلالة' indicates a conditional argument.
2
Understand the logical argument
The argument is: God creates all actions of servants. Therefore, He creates the act of guidance (ihtidā') and the act of straying (ḍalāl). Hence, He is truly the Guide and the Misguider.
This directly contrasts with the Mu'tazilite view that actions are created by humans.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view
Compare this affirmation with the previous interpretations. The Ash'ari view emphasizes God's omnipotence and direct creation of all things, including human acts.
Both schools use the same Quranic verses but interpret them differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Mu'tazila's Self-Contradiction on Guidance and Will
Exposes Mu'tazila's self-contradiction in interpreting guidance and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi author argues that the Mu'tazila themselves indirectly affirm the Maturidi interpretation of guidance and will.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's attempt to reinterpret verses exposes their own inconsistency.

In this passage, the Maturidi author builds a powerful argument: the Mu'tazila, when forced to confront the plain meaning of the Qur'anic verses, actually reinterpret guidance in a way that matches the Maturidi view. However, they try to distance themselves by labeling it 'the will of compulsion' (mashi'at al-jabr). The author shows that this label is unnecessary and reveals a fundamental contradiction in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's claim
Identify the main claim: the Mu'tazila recognized their falsehood and diverted the guidance interpretation to match the Maturidi view.
2
Understand the reinterpretation
Notice that they reinterpreted 'will' to mean 'will of compulsion' (jabr), which the author argues is only needed for a specific understanding of guidance.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Consider why the author says the reinterpretation is unnecessary: guidance in the verses is not about exposition or naming, but about direct divine causality.
4
Conclude the argument
The author concludes that all verses speak in favor of the Maturidi view and against the Mu'tazila.
This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يحقق بطلان ذلك أنهم عرفوا بطلانهم حتى صرفوا الهداية المذكورة في هذه الآيات إلى ما صرفنا نحن، وفسروا المشيئة بمشيئة الجبر، وإنما يحتاج إلى صرف المشيئة إلى مشيئة الجبر عند كون الهداية ما بينا دون بيان الدين بدلائله والتسمية والوجود وإراءه طريق الجنة في الآخرة، ثم قد بينا إبطال مشيئة الجبر وبهذه الآيات بطل إضافته ذلك إلى الله تعالى لوجود السبب منه أو الشرط أو الحالة الحاملة على ذلك، لأن هذه المعاني عامة في حق الناس كافة، فلم تكن هداية من اهتدى عندهم بمشيئته ولا ضلال من ضل فلم يكن لتعليق ذلك بالمشيئة معنى، فصح أن الأمر كما بينا، وأن الآيات كلها ناطقة بصحة ما ذهبنا إليه وبطلان ما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة، والله الموفق. ولكون هذه المسألة عين مسألة خلق أفعال العباد لم نشتغل بتطويلها، وإيراد ما أورده سلف الأمة من الدلائل السمعية والعقلية. ولو تلقت المعتزلة ما نطق به كتاب الله تعالى من إضافة الهداية والإضلال إليه بالقبول، وتعلموا من أهل الحق دفع ما تتمسك به الثنوية من الشبهة، وهو أن الحكيم لا يفعل القبيح لكان خيراً لهم من أن تلقنوا من الثنوية هذه الشبهة، وتشبثوا
And what confirms the falsehood of that is that they recognized their own falsehood to the point that they diverted the guidance mentioned in these verses to what we diverted it to, and they interpreted the will as the will of compulsion. But they only need to divert the will to the will of compulsion when the guidance is as we explained, not the exposition of religion with its proofs, nor naming, nor finding, nor showing the path to Paradise in the Hereafter. Then we have already shown the invalidity of the will of compulsion, and with these verses, their attribution of that to Allah is invalidated due to the existence of the cause from Him, or the condition, or the state that impels to that, because these meanings are general for all people. So in their view, there was no guidance for the one who is guided by His will, nor misguidance for the one who is misguided, so there was no meaning to attaching that to will. So it is correct that the matter is as we have explained, and that all the verses speak in favor of what we have gone to and the invalidity of what the Mu'tazila have gone to. And Allah is the Granter of Success. And because this issue is the very issue of the creation of the actions of servants, we did not occupy ourselves with lengthening it and presenting what the predecessors of the nation have reported of scriptural and rational proofs. And if the Mu'tazila had accepted what the Book of Allah spoke of regarding the attribution of guidance and misguidance to Him, and had learned from the People of Truth the repelling of the doubt that the Dualists hold onto, namely that the wise does not do ugly [actions], it would have been better for them than that they learned from the Dualists this doubt, and clung [to it].
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage confirms the Maturidi position. The author points out that the Mu'tazila themselves, when forced, reinterpret the verses in a way similar to the Maturidis, but they call it 'the will of compulsion' (jabr). The author argues that the verses cannot be interpreted as showing the path or naming, because those are general matters. If they were, then every person would be guided or misguided, but the verses speak of will. Therefore, the correct interpretation is that Allah directly creates guidance and misguidance. The author then notes that this issue is the same as the creation of human actions, so he does not elaborate. Finally, he accuses the Mu'tazila of adopting the doubt of the Dualists (Manichaeans) that the Wise does not do evil, and that it would have been better for them to learn from the People of Truth.

Key Terms
مشيئة الجبر
هداية
إضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidi and Mu'tazilite views on guidance are identical because both use the same verses.
Correction: The author shows that the Mu'tazila's reinterpretation reveals they actually agree with the Maturidi interpretation but disguise it by calling it 'will of compulsion' to avoid contradiction.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider that the Mu'tazila 'recognized their own falsehood'?
A) Because they changed their interpretation of the verses to match the Maturidi view
B) Because they admitted defeat in debate
C) Because they stopped arguing
D) Because they accepted the will of compulsion

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's need to reinterpret guidance reveals that their own position is untenable and indirectly affirms the Maturidi view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself reinterpreting a clear truth simply because accepting it would mean admitting you were wrong? What difference would it make in your life if you stopped resisting and submitted today?
?
This lesson exposes how the Mu'tazila were forced to align with the truth despite their denial. What truth about yourself are you currently refusing to admit—about your relationship with Allah, your character, or your faith—even though deep down you know it? Will you wait until the truth overwhelms you, or will you surrender now?
?
The Mu'tazila’s reinterpretation shows they knew the Maturidi view was correct but lacked the courage to accept it. In what area of your life are you holding onto a position out of pride or stubbornness, even though you know it is not the truth? What would it take for you to let go today?

Refutation of Mu'tazila: Divine Wisdom and the Dualist Influence
Links the issue to creation of actions and accuses Mu'tazila of Dualist influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi author refutes the Mu'tazilite position by arguing that their reasoning originates from Dualist sources and that divine wisdom does not preclude creating good and evil.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila learned their doubt from Dualists, not from revelation.

In the conclusion of his argument, the Maturidi author links the issue of guidance and misguidance to the broader question of the creation of human actions. He then levels a serious accusation: the Mu'tazila have adopted a key doubt from the Dualists (Manichaeans) — the idea that 'the Wise does not do ugly actions.' The author insists that if they had simply accepted the Qur'anic attribute of all things to Allah and learned from the People of Truth, they would have avoided this error. This lesson unpacks that accusation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Notice the author's transition: the issue of guidance is identical to the creation of actions, so he will not elaborate further.
2
Identify the Dualist doubt
The doubt is: 'The Wise does not do ugly actions' (al-ḥakīm lā yaf‘alu al-qabīḥ). The author claims the Mu'tazila learned this from the Dualists.
3
Understand the accusation
The Mu'tazila are accused of borrowing a doubt from the Dualists and clinging to it, rather than learning from the People of Truth how to refute it.
4
Reflect on the recommendation
The author says it would have been better for them to accept the Qur'anic attribution of guidance and misguidance to Allah and learn the proper refutation from the People of Truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If they were, then every person would be guided or misguided, but the verses speak of will. Therefore, the correct interpretation is that Allah directly creates guidance and misguidance. The author then notes that this issue is the same as the creation of human actions, so he does not elaborate. Finally, he accuses the Mu'tazila of adopting the doubt of the Dualists (Manichaeans) that the Wise does not do evil, and that it would have been better for them to learn from the People of Truth.

Key Terms
خلق أفعال العباد
ثنوية
الحكيم لا يفعل القبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidi view on divine wisdom is similar to the Mu'tazilite view that Allah cannot do evil.
Correction: The author argues that the Mu'tazilite view originates from Dualists, not revelation, while the Maturidi view affirms that Allah's wisdom is beyond human analogy and that He does as He wills, including creating actions.

Checkpoint
What specific doubt does the author accuse the Mu'tazila of learning from the Dualists?
A) That Allah does not create evil actions
B) That the Wise does not do ugly actions
C) That guidance and misguidance are from Allah
D) That the Qur'an is created

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument from divine wisdom is borrowed from Dualists and does not align with the Qur'anic attribution of all things to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the Mu'tazila clung to the Dualist doubt that 'the wise does not do ugly' instead of accepting the Qur'anic attribution of guidance and misguidance to Allah. Is there any belief you hold about Allah that He would never do something you consider 'ugly'? Are you willing to submit to the Qur'an even if it attributes something to Him that your mind finds difficult?
?
The author says the Mu'tazila would have been better off if they had learned from the People of Truth. When you encounter a theological issue, do you seek out the People of Truth, or do you cling to doubts that comfort your intellect? What is one doubt you need to let go of today?
?
The passage links the issue of guidance and misguidance to the creation of actions. Do you truly believe that every action, even your own sins, is under Allah's creation? If so, how does that change your sense of humility and dependence on Him?

The Proof of Ahl al-Haqq Against Al-Aslah
The author presents the proof of Ahl al-Haqq from Quran and existence against al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Haqq use Quranic verses and the reality of sin to refute the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must always do what is best for people.

Core Idea
God's power to guide all proves He is not obligated to do the most beneficial.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God must always do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His creatures, especially in matters of guidance. Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox Sunnis) rejected this as an imposition on God's will. In this passage, the author presents two key proofs — from the Quran and from observable existence — demonstrating that God does not always guide people, and that sin exists, which contradicts the doctrine of al-aslah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage contains OCR issues; focus on the translation for clarity.
2
Identify the four sources of proof
The passage opens by enumerating four sources: the Book (Quran), existence, consensus of religions, and intellectual proof. Only the first two are elaborated here.
3
Analyze the Quranic verses
Examine the three verses cited (32:13, 6:149, 10:99). Notice that each states that if God willed, He could guide all. The author argues this proves God has power to guide all, yet He does not do so, which refutes the necessity of al-aslah.
Consider the author's analogy of a lying boastful person to highlight the absurdity of interpreting these verses otherwise.
4
Understand the argument from existence
The author notes that disbelief and sin exist. Since it is established that actions of servants are created by God, the presence of sin shows that God does not always do the most beneficial for those individuals.
5
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite counter-interpretation
The author anticipates the Mu'tazilite attempt to reinterpret the verses as referring to a will of compulsion (jabr) and rejects it as invalid, referring to earlier arguments.
Think about why the concept of compulsion would not save the doctrine of al-aslah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[دليل أهل الحق]:
والدليل لأهل الحق في المسألة كتاب الله تعالى والوجود، وإجماع أهل الأديان والدليل العقلي.

[من الكتاب]
أما الكتاب فقول الله تعالى: وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَا [نَيْنَا؟] كُلَّ نَفْسٍ [هُدَنهَا؟] وقوله تعالى: فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَ [نَكُمْ؟] أَجْمَعِينَ وقوله تعالى ( وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جميعا، ولو لم يكن في مقدوره ما لو فعل بهم لأمنوا لم يكن لهذه الآيات فائدة سوی ادعاء قدرة ومشيئة ليستا له كفعل الكذوب المتصلف الذي يتحلى بما ليس فيه ما لا يحسن، وحملهم الآيات على مشيئة الجبر والقسر باطل على ما مر .

[دليل أهل الحق من الوجود]
وأما الوجود فإن الكفر والمعاصي قد وجدت، وإنا أثبتنا بالدلائل السمعية والعقلية التي لا مدفع لها ولا اعتراض عليها ولا ريب فيها لمن أنصف من نفسه ولم يكابر عقله أن أفعال العباد مخلوقة الله، وفيها الكفر والمعاصي، وهم يتضررون
[The Proof of Ahl al-Haqq]:
And the proof for Ahl al-Haqq in this matter is the Book of Allah Almighty, existence, consensus of the religions, and intellectual proof.

[From the Book]
As for the Book, it is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' [32:13], and His saying: 'For if He had willed, He would have guided you all' [6:149], and His saying: 'And if your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them together' [10:99]. And if it were not within His power that if He did something they would believe, these verses would have no benefit except claiming a power and will that are not His, like the action of a lying boastful person who adorns himself with what he does not possess. And their interpretation of the verses to mean a will of compulsion and force is invalid, as has passed.

[The Proof of Ahl al-Haqq from Existence]
As for existence, disbelief and sins have indeed occurred. And we have established by textual and rational proofs, which are irrefutable and unquestionable and without doubt for whoever is fair to himself and does not argue with his intellect, that the actions of servants are created by Allah, and among them are disbelief and sins, and they suffer harm.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the counter-proof of Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox Sunni position) against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah. It is structured as a proof from four sources: the Quran, existence, consensus of religions, and reason, though only the Quran and existence are elaborated in the available text.

The Quranic proof cites three verses: (32:13) 'If We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance'; (6:149) 'If He had willed, He would have guided you all'; and (10:99) 'If your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed.' The point is that these verses imply that God has the power to guide all, but He does not do so. If it were true that God always does the most beneficial, then guiding everyone would be the most beneficial, but these verses indicate that He does not guide all, so the Mu'tazilite claim fails. Furthermore, if God did not have the power to guide all, these verses would be meaningless—they would be like a boastful liar claiming ability he does not have. The Mu'tazilite attempt to reinterpret these verses as referring to a will of compulsion (jabr) is rejected as invalid because it contradicts earlier arguments.

The proof from existence is simple: disbelief and sins exist in the world. The author asserts that it has been established by both textual and rational proofs that actions of servants are created by God. Since God creates these actions, and they include disbelief and sins, it follows that God does not always do the most beneficial for those individuals, as they suffer harm. This refutes the necessity of al-aslah.

Key Terms
أهل الحق
الكتاب
الوجود
الجبر
القسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the argument denies God's wisdom or mercy, or that it implies God is unjust.
Correction: The proof does not deny God's wisdom or mercy; it affirms that God's will is supreme and that He is not obligated to do what humans consider 'best'. The existence of sin is part of a larger divine plan that may include justice and punishment.

Checkpoint
What is the main Quranic argument used by Ahl al-Haqq to refute the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah?
A) God's verses show that He is not powerful enough to guide all.
B) God's verses indicate that He could have guided all but did not, proving that He does not always do the most beneficial.
C) The Quran commands people to choose belief freely.
D) The Quran explicitly denies that God does the most beneficial.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq refute al-aslah by showing that God's choice not to guide all and the existence of sin demonstrate that God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial for every individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial or see others suffering, do you ever feel that God owes you an easier path? How does this passage's message that God is not obligated to do what is 'best' in your eyes change your perspective on your own entitlements?
?
If you truly believe that God could have guided every soul but chose not to, how does that affect the way you view those who disbelieve? Does it remove any arrogance or despair from your heart?
?
The passage says God creates all actions, including sins. When you look at your own sins, do you see them as solely your fault, or do you acknowledge God's ultimate will? How does this balance lead you to both repent and trust in His mercy without feeling that He is unjust?

The Reductio Ad Absurdum of the Opponent's View on Child Death and Apostasy
A dialogue demonstrating the absurd implications of the opponent's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a reductio ad absurdum argument is used to refute the Mu'tazilite view that God must always do what is most beneficial for each individual.

Core Idea
A dilemma exposing contradiction: why kill one to save from apostasy but not another?

The argument presents a dialogue where the consequences of the opponent's position are followed through. By assuming that God kills a child because He knows the child would apostatize, the opponent is forced into a contradiction when considering an apostate who was kept alive. This lesson traces the logical steps of this reductio.

Guided Path
1
Establish the three cases
Identify the three scenarios presented: child dies young, believer dies in faith, apostate dies in disbelief. Each is acknowledged by the opponent.
2
Follow the child's complaint
The child questions why he was not kept alive to earn more reward, given the believer's high station.
3
Examine the opponent's response
The opponent claims God killed the child because He knew the child would apostatize if he lived.
4
Consider the apostate's complaint
The apostate asks why God did not kill him as a child, since He knew he would apostatize.
5
Identify the contradiction
The opponent's justifications for killing the child and sparing the apostate are inconsistent. The author concludes there is no escape from this dilemma.
The dilemma is that if killing the child was beneficial, then sparing the apostate cannot be beneficial; and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يقطع شغبه أنا نقول له: هل رأيت طفلاً مات؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل وهل رأيت رجلاً بلغ وآمن وختم له به؟ فلا بد من بلى قيل: وهل رأيت أو سمعت إنسانًا ارتد عن الإسلام بعد ما بلغ عاقلاً؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: فلو أن الله تعالى أثاب البالغ الذي ختم له بالإيمان ورفع له الدرجات في الجنة وأوصل إليه على ما سبق منه من الطاعات ما لا عين رأت ولا أذن سمعت من أنواع النعم الأبدية فعاين ذلك الطفل المتوفى في حال صغره قصور ثوابه ومنزلته عن ذلك فناجي ربه وقال يا رب لم توفيتني في حال صغري وانعدام عقلي، ولم تبقني مع تركب العقل في إلى وقت البلوغ بل لم تمهلني مع ذلك إلى وقت الشيب لأكتسب من الطاعات ما أنال عليها من الثواب ما يوازي ثواب هذا، فماذا يقول له الله تعالى؟ فإن قال : يقول له أن الأصلح لك كان أن أميتك وأتوفاك في حال الصغر وانعدام العقل لأني علمت بسابق علمي أنك لو بلغت عاقلاً لكفرت واستوجبت التخليد في النار فتوفيتك في حال الصغر لئلا تبقى في النار خالدًا مخلدًا، قيل له فلو سمع الكبير الذي مات على الكفر وارتد بعد ما زجى أكثر عمره على الإيمان فناجي فقال : يا رب لما كنت تعلم أني أكفر بعد البلوغ أو أرتد لما لم تمتني في حال صغري كما أمت هذا لئلا أبقى خالدًا مخلدًا في النار؟ فماذا يجيبه الله تعالى؟ فإن قال : يجيبه أن ذلك كان أصلح لك لأني عرضتك لأعلى المنزلتين، قيل : فيقول الصغير : إذا كان ذلك أصلح له فلم لم تفعل بي ذلك فلم يبق إلا الانقطاع والقصيرة عن الطويلة أن إماتة الصغير وإن كان أصلح له فلماذا أبقى الذي علم أنه يكفر ؟ وإن كان الإبقاء أصلح لما فيه من التعريض فلماذا أمات هذا الصغير ؟ وهذا مما لا انفصال لهم إلى أن يبيض الفار ويشيب الغراب، فاعترض
Then what cuts off his mischief is that we say to him: Have you seen a child who died? There is no avoiding 'yes'. He said: And have you seen a man who reached maturity and believed and ended his life with it? There is no avoiding 'yes'? He said: And have you seen or heard of a person who apostatized from Islam after having reached maturity and being rational? There is no avoiding 'yes'. He said: So if God rewarded the adult who ended his life with faith and raised for him degrees in Paradise and granted him, on account of what preceded of acts of obedience, what no eye has seen nor ear has heard of kinds of eternal bounties, and that child who died in his childhood sees the deficiency of his reward and station compared to that, and he complains to his Lord and says: O Lord, why did You make me die in my childhood and lack of intellect, and did not keep me with the intellect composed until the time of reaching maturity? Rather, You did not even delay me with that until the time of old age so that I could gain from acts of obedience such reward as would equal the reward of this one. Then what does God say to him? If he says: He says to him that it was more beneficial for you that I caused you to die and made you die in childhood and lack of intellect, because I knew by My prior knowledge that if you reached maturity with intellect, you would disbelieve and deserve eternal staying in the Fire, so I caused you to die in childhood so that you would not remain in the Fire eternally. It is said to him: So if the elder who died in disbelief and apostatized after having spent most of his life in faith hears and complains and says: O Lord, since You knew that I would disbelieve after maturity or apostatize, why did You not cause me to die in my childhood as You caused this one to die, so that I would not remain eternally in the Fire? Then what does God answer him? If he says: He answers him that that was more beneficial for you because I exposed you to the highest of the two stations. It is said: So the child says: If that was more beneficial for him, why did You not do that for me? Then nothing remains except disconnection and the short (argument) against the long: that causing the child to die, even if it was more beneficial for him, then why did He keep alive the one He knew would disbelieve? And if keeping alive was more beneficial because of the exposure therein, then why did He cause this child to die? And this is something from which they have no escape until the mouse turns white and the raven turns grey. Then he objected...
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a reductio ad absurdum presented as a dialogue. The author first establishes three factual cases: a child who dies young, a believer who dies in faith, and an apostate who apostatizes after maturity. The child complains that he was not kept alive to gain reward like the believer. The opponent (likely Mu'tazila) responds that God killed him because He knew he would apostatize. Then the apostate complains: if God knew I would apostatize, why didn't He kill me as a child to save me from Hell? The opponent then says that keeping the apostate alive was beneficial because it exposed him to the highest station (i.e., opportunity for reward). The child then retorts: if that exposure is beneficial, why was I denied it? This reveals a contradiction: the opponent cannot consistently maintain that killing the child was beneficial and that keeping the apostate alive was also beneficial. The author concludes that the opponent has no escape from this dilemma, comparing it to impossible things like a mouse turning white or a raven turning grey.

Key Terms
انقطاع
القصيرة عن الطويلة
خالدًا مخلدًا
فاعترض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must act in the most beneficial way (al-aṣlaḥ) for each individual in every circumstance.
Correction: The passage shows that this principle leads to contradiction; if God always does what is most beneficial, He cannot simultaneously benefit the child by death and the apostate by life, as their situations are parallel. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite notion of al-aṣlaḥ is untenable.

Checkpoint
In the dialogue, what complaint does the child who died young make?
A) That he was not given wealth.
B) That he was not kept alive to gain more reward.
C) That he was punished unjustly.
D) That he wishes he had apostatized.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's position leads to a self-contradictory dilemma: if killing the child is beneficial because it prevents apostasy, then sparing the apostate cannot also be beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage reveals that demanding God's actions always appear beneficial from our limited perspective leads to self-contradiction. Where in your own life have you questioned God's wisdom because a situation seemed unfair or disadvantageous? Will you now consciously surrender that complaint to His infinite wisdom, even if you never understand?
?
The child's complaint is about not being given the same opportunities as the believer. Do you hold any hidden resentment towards God for not giving you certain abilities, wealth, or life circumstances that others have? What would it mean for you today to truly accept that His plan for you is better than any alternative you could imagine?
?
The apostate's hypothetical shows that even a lifetime of faith can be followed by loss. Does this thought create enough fear in you to constantly examine the state of your own iman? What specific, concrete step will you take today to safeguard your faith from future apostasy?

The Three Premises and the Child's Complaint
Imam al-Nasafi elicits three premises and presents the child's hypothetical challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi sets up the conditions for the Mu'tazilite dilemma by establishing three observable cases and the child's hypothetical question.

Core Idea
Three universal facts set the stage for a devastating dilemma against the Mu'tazila.

Before the dilemma can hit, the opponent must first concede three undeniable observations: children die, some believers die faithful, and some apostatize. Imam al-Nasafi then imagines a child who died young seeing the reward of a faithful adult and questioning why he was not kept alive. This sets the hook for the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Carefully read the three questions that begin with 'هل رأيت...'. Each one forces a 'yes' answer. Notice how each case is distinct: a child, a faithful adult, and an apostate.
These three are the factual basis for the argument.
2
Understand the child's complaint
Read the child's imagined speech to Allah. The child compares his meager reward to the faithful adult's immense reward and asks why he was not given a long life to earn more.
This complaint only makes sense if the child is assumed to deserve fairness.
3
Pause on the question
The passage ends with 'فماذا يقول له الله تعالى؟' (What would Allah say to him?). Before moving on, consider what the Mu'tazilite must answer. This creates suspense.
The answer will be the key to the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يقطع شغبه أنا نقول له: هل رأيت طفلاً مات؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل وهل رأيت رجلاً بلغ وآمن وختم له به؟ فلا بد من بلى قيل: وهل رأيت أو سمعت إنسانًا ارتد عن الإسلام بعد ما بلغ عاقلاً؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: فلو أن الله تعالى أثاب البالغ الذي ختم له بالإيمان ورفع له الدرجات في الجنة وأوصل إليه على ما سبق منه من الطاعات ما لا عين رأت ولا أذن سمعت من أنواع النعم الأبدية فعاين ذلك الطفل المتوفى في حال صغره قصور ثوابه ومنزلته عن ذلك فناجي ربه :وقال يا رب لم توفيتني في حال صغري وانعدام عقلي، ولم تبقني مع تركب العقل في إلى وقت البلوغ بل لم تمهلني مع ذلك إلى وقت الشيب لأكتسب من الطاعات ما أنال عليها من الثواب ما يوازي ثواب هذا، فماذا يقول له الله تعالى؟ فإن قال : يقول له أن الأصلح لك كان أن أميتك وأتوفاك في حال الصغر وانعدام العقل لأني علمت بسابق علمي أنك لو بلغت عاقلاً لكفرت واستوجبت التخليد في النار فتوفيتك في حال الصغر لئلا تبقى في النار خالدًا مخلدًا، قيل له فلو سمع الكبير الذي مات على الكفر وارتد بعد ما زجى أكثر عمره على الإيمان فناجي فقال : يا رب لما كنت تعلم أني أكفر بعد البلوغ أو أرتد لما لم تمتني في حال صغري كما أمت هذا لئلا أبقى خالدًا مخلدًا في النار؟ فماذا يجيبه الله تعالى؟ فإن قال : يجيبه أن ذلك كان أصلح لك لأني عرضتك لأعلى المنزلتين، قيل : فيقول الصغير : إذا كان ذلك أصلح له فلم لم تفعل بي ذلك فلم يبق إلا الانقطاع والقصيرة عن الطويلة أن إماتة الصغير وإن كان أصلح له فلماذا أبقى الذي علم أنه يكفر ؟ وإن كان الإبقاء أصلح لما فيه من التعريض فلماذا أمات هذا الصغير ؟ وهذا مما لا انفصال لهم إلى أن يبيض الفار ويشيب الغراب
Then what cuts off his disputation is that we say to him: Did you see a child who died? There must be a yes. It is said: Did you see a man who reached puberty, believed, and ended with it? There must be a yes. It is said: Did you see or hear of a person who apostatized from Islam after reaching puberty as a sane person? There must be a yes. It is said: If God Almighty rewarded the adult who ended with faith, elevated his ranks in Paradise, and gave him, for the obedience that preceded from him, what no eye has seen nor ear heard of eternal blessings, and that child who died in his infancy saw the deficiency of his reward and station compared to that, and he whispered to his Lord and said: O Lord, why did You cause me to die in my infancy and lack of intellect, and did not keep me until the time I acquired intellect and reached puberty, nor did You give me respite until old age to gain obedience through which I might attain a reward equal to this person's? Then what would God Almighty say to him? If he says: He says to him: What was best for you was that I cause you to die and take you in the state of infancy and lack of intellect, because I knew by My prior knowledge that if you reached puberty sane, you would disbelieve and deserve eternal punishment in the Fire, so I caused you to die in infancy so that you would not remain in the Fire eternally. It is said to him: If the adult who died in disbelief, after having spent most of his life in faith, apostatized and whispered and said: O Lord, since You knew that I would disbelieve after puberty or apostatize, why did You not cause me to die in my infancy as You caused this one to die, so that I would not remain eternally in the Fire? Then what would God Almighty answer him? If he says: He answers him: That was better for you because I exposed you to the highest of the two stations. It is said: Then the child says: If that was better for him, why did You not do that for me? So nothing remains but defeat and the shortcoming from the long, that causing the child to die, even if it was best for him, then why did He keep alive the one He knew would disbelieve? And if keeping alive was better because of the exposure [to the highest station], then why did He cause this child to die? And this is something they cannot escape until the fox turns white and the raven turns white-haired.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation. The author presents an argument in the form of a dialogue that traps the opponent (likely the Mu'tazila) who holds the doctrine of al-aslah (that God must do what is best for each individual). First, three premises are elicited: yes, children die; yes, some adults believe until the end; yes, some apostatize. Then the author imagines a child who died young seeing the immense reward of a faithful adult and questioning why he was not kept alive to earn similar rewards. The opponent's hypothetical answer is that God knew the child would have disbelieved if he lived, so He caused his death to save him from eternal punishment. The author then turns this against the opponent by imagining an apostate who lived a long life of faith then died in disbelief: this apostate questions why God did not kill him in infancy as He did the child, since God knew he would apostatize and end in hell. The opponent's answer is that it was better for the apostate to be exposed to the highest station (i.e., the chance to earn eternal reward through faith and good deeds). The child then retorts that if exposure to the highest station is better, why was he deprived of it? The argument becomes a dilemma: if causing the child's death was best for him, then why did God spare the one He knew would disbelieve? And if sparing the apostate was better because of the exposure, then why did God kill the child? The author concludes that the opponents have no escape from this contradiction.

?

?
