The Apostate's Objection and the Dilemma
Imam al-Nasafi completes the dilemma with the apostate's counter-question and the child's reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp the logical dilemma Imam al-Nasafi constructs: the opponent cannot consistently apply the principle of 'what is best' (al-aslah) to both the child and the apostate.

Core Idea
A double-edged question leaves the Mu'tazila with no consistent answer.

The child's question forces the opponent to give an answer: God killed him because He knew he would disbelieve. But then an apostate raises the mirror question: why was he not killed in infancy? The opponent's answer—that it was better to give him the chance for higher reward—contradicts the earlier answer. Imam al-Nasafi then exposes the inescapable dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's answer to the child
The opponent says God caused the child's death because He knew the child would have disbelieved and deserved eternal punishment. This seems reasonable at first.
This answer relies on God's foreknowledge of disbelief.
2
Introduce the apostate's question
Now an apostate (who was a believer for most of his life) asks: why did God not kill me in infancy too, since He knew I would apostatize?
The apostate's situation mirrors the child's but in reverse.
3
See the opponent's second answer
The opponent says the apostate was kept alive because it was better for him to be exposed to the highest station (i.e., the chance to earn great reward).
This introduces the concept of 'exposure to the highest station' (تعريض لأعلى المنزلتين).
4
Identify the dilemma
The child then retorts: if exposure is better, why was I deprived? The author then states the two horns: if death was best for the child, why spare the apostate? And if spare was best, why kill the child?
The opponent cannot hold both positions consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يقطع شغبه أنا نقول له: هل رأيت طفلاً مات؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل وهل رأيت رجلاً بلغ وآمن وختم له به؟ فلا بد من بلى قيل: وهل رأيت أو سمعت إنسانًا ارتد عن الإسلام بعد ما بلغ عاقلاً؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل: فلو أن الله تعالى أثاب البالغ الذي ختم له بالإيمان ورفع له الدرجات في الجنة وأوصل إليه على ما سبق منه من الطاعات ما لا عين رأت ولا أذن سمعت من أنواع النعم الأبدية فعاين ذلك الطفل المتوفى في حال صغره قصور ثوابه ومنزلته عن ذلك فناجي ربه :وقال يا رب لم توفيتني في حال صغري وانعدام عقلي، ولم تبقني مع تركب العقل في إلى وقت البلوغ بل لم تمهلني مع ذلك إلى وقت الشيب لأكتسب من الطاعات ما أنال عليها من الثواب ما يوازي ثواب هذا، فماذا يقول له الله تعالى؟ فإن قال : يقول له أن الأصلح لك كان أن أميتك وأتوفاك في حال الصغر وانعدام العقل لأني علمت بسابق علمي أنك لو بلغت عاقلاً لكفرت واستوجبت التخليد في النار فتوفيتك في حال الصغر لئلا تبقى في النار خالدًا مخلدًا، قيل له فلو سمع الكبير الذي مات على الكفر وارتد بعد ما زجى أكثر عمره على الإيمان فناجي فقال : يا رب لما كنت تعلم أني أكفر بعد البلوغ أو أرتد لما لم تمتني في حال صغري كما أمت هذا لئلا أبقى خالدًا مخلدًا في النار؟ فماذا يجيبه الله تعالى؟ فإن قال : يجيبه أن ذلك كان أصلح لك لأني عرضتك لأعلى المنزلتين، قيل : فيقول الصغير : إذا كان ذلك أصلح له فلم لم تفعل بي ذلك فلم يبق إلا الانقطاع والقصيرة عن الطويلة أن إماتة الصغير وإن كان أصلح له فلماذا أبقى الذي علم أنه يكفر ؟ وإن كان الإبقاء أصلح لما فيه من التعريض فلماذا أمات هذا الصغير ؟ وهذا مما لا انفصال لهم إلى أن يبيض الفار ويشيب الغراب
Then what cuts off his disputation is that we say to him: Did you see a child who died? There must be a yes. It is said: Did you see a man who reached puberty, believed, and ended with it? There must be a yes. It is said: Did you see or hear of a person who apostatized from Islam after reaching puberty as a sane person? There must be a yes. It is said: If God Almighty rewarded the adult who ended with faith, elevated his ranks in Paradise, and gave him, for the obedience that preceded from him, what no eye has seen nor ear heard of eternal blessings, and that child who died in his infancy saw the deficiency of his reward and station compared to that, and he whispered to his Lord and said: O Lord, why did You cause me to die in my infancy and lack of intellect, and did not keep me until the time I acquired intellect and reached puberty, nor did You give me respite until old age to gain obedience through which I might attain a reward equal to this person's? Then what would God Almighty say to him? If he says: He says to him: What was best for you was that I cause you to die and take you in the state of infancy and lack of intellect, because I knew by My prior knowledge that if you reached puberty sane, you would disbelieve and deserve eternal punishment in the Fire, so I caused you to die in infancy so that you would not remain in the Fire eternally. It is said to him: If the adult who died in disbelief, after having spent most of his life in faith, apostatized and whispered and said: O Lord, since You knew that I would disbelieve after puberty or apostatize, why did You not cause me to die in my infancy as You caused this one to die, so that I would not remain eternally in the Fire? Then what would God Almighty answer him? If he says: He answers him: That was better for you because I exposed you to the highest of the two stations. It is said: Then the child says: If that was better for him, why did You not do that for me? So nothing remains but defeat and the shortcoming from the long, that causing the child to die, even if it was best for him, then why did He keep alive the one He knew would disbelieve? And if keeping alive was better because of the exposure [to the highest station], then why did He cause this child to die? And this is something they cannot escape until the fox turns white and the raven turns white-haired.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation. The author presents an argument in the form of a dialogue that traps the opponent (likely the Mu'tazila) who holds the doctrine of al-aslah (that God must do what is best for each individual). First, three premises are elicited: yes, children die; yes, some adults believe until the end; yes, some apostatize. Then the author imagines a child who died young seeing the immense reward of a faithful adult and questioning why he was not kept alive to earn similar rewards. The opponent's hypothetical answer is that God knew the child would have disbelieved if he lived, so He caused his death to save him from eternal punishment. The author then turns this against the opponent by imagining an apostate who lived a long life of faith then died in disbelief: this apostate questions why God did not kill him in infancy as He did the child, since God knew he would apostatize and end in hell. The opponent's answer is that it was better for the apostate to be exposed to the highest station (i.e., the chance to earn eternal reward through faith and good deeds). The child then retorts that if exposure to the highest station is better, why was he deprived of it? The argument becomes a dilemma: if causing the child's death was best for him, then why did God spare the one He knew would disbelieve? And if sparing the apostate was better because of the exposure, then why did God kill the child? The author concludes that the opponents have no escape from this contradiction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument is about God's injustice or arbitrariness, but it is specifically a logical refutation of the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah (that God must do what is best for each individual).
Correction: The argument does not claim God is unjust; it shows that the Mu'tazilite principle leads to contradiction when applied to these cases.

Checkpoint

D) The apostate should have been killed in infancy.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah collapses when faced with the symmetrical cases of the child and the apostate.

?

?
The apostate in the passage spent most of his life in faith. Have you ever relied on your past good deeds to feel secure, forgetting that your ending is unknown? How does this dilemma expose the danger of presumption?
?
The Mu'tazilite tried to make God's actions fit human logic. When have you tried to box God into your own sense of fairness? Will you repent of that and submit to His hidden wisdom today?

Refuting the Objection: Child's Death for Others
An objection and its multi-step refutation by Imam al-Nasafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection that a child's early death can be justified by preventing future harm to others, exposing its internal contradictions and inconsistency with the principle of al-aslah.

Core Idea
The child's best interest cannot be sacrificed for others without injustice.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi confronts a Mu'tazilite objection aimed at preserving the principle of al-aslah. The objector argues that God caused a child to die early to prevent future harm to others. The Imam systematically dismantles this argument, showing that it still violates the child's own best interest and leads to absurd consequences when applied consistently. This lesson traces his logical refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the first part where the objector claims that killing the child prevents future disbelief. Then read Imam al-Nasafi's rebuttal that this admission still deprives the child of the higher station (تعريض لأعلى المنزلتين).
Notice that the Imam immediately points out the neglect of the child's own best interest.
2
Understand the dilemma
Analyze the two opposite outcomes: death benefits the adult but corrupts the child; life benefits the child but corrupts the adult. The Imam forces the opponent to admit that in either case there is corruption and abandonment of benefit.
This dilemma shows that the opponent's position cannot avoid harm.
3
Apply the historical test
The Imam uses known historical figures (Mani, Zoroaster, etc.) to test the opponent's logic. If God knew they would cause harm but did not kill them, the objection is inconsistent.
If the opponent says God did not know, that is blasphemy; if He knew, then why did He let them live?
4
Reflect on the final conclusion
The opponent's last resort claim that everything God does is best, even if we do not understand it, is an admission of defeat. It shows they cannot rationally defend their principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعترض بعضهم على هذا وقال : إنه أمات الصغير في حال صغره لأنه علم أنه لو بلغ لكفر وأضل غيره وأكفره فأماته في حال صغره لما فيه من مصلحة الغير قلنا هذا منكم إقرار أنه منع ما هو الأصح للصغير وهو التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين الذي أمهل ذلك البالغ لأجله، إلا أنه منع هذا الأصلح لما فيه من صلاح ذلك الغير، وعندكم منع النفع عن لا جناية له لإصلاح غيره ظلم على هذا. يحققه أن عندكم إنما يجب على الله تعالى بذل الأصلح إذا كان بذله لا يخرجه من استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة، وإماتة هذا الصغير لما فيه من صلاح ذلك الغير منع لمصلحة التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين في حق هذا الصغير، وهذا يخرجه عن استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة فينبغي أن لا يفوت على هذا الصغير مصلحة التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين لمكان مصلحة الصغير، ومن الحاصل أنتم بين طرفي نقيض فإنكم إن قلتم : إماتته في الصغر مصلحة لذلك الكبير فهو مفسدة ولو لم يمته حتى بلغ فله فيه مصلحة التعريض ومفسدة لذلك الغير، فلم يخل كيف ما كان عن مفسدة وترك مصلحة ، ثم هل كان الله تعالى علم أن ماني وزرادشت ومزدك ومسيلمة والمقنع وبابك وقبل ذلك فرعون لو بلغوا لكفروا وأكفروا الناس؟ فإن قالوا : لا . كفروا لتجهيلهم ربهم، وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا: فلم أمهلهم الله تعالى وقد علم أنهم يكفرون ويضلون الناس وهلا أماتهم مصلحة للناس، وهذا مما لا اعتراض لهم عليه، فلما تحيروا في هذا زعم بعضهم: ثبت لنا أن الله تعالى لا يفعل إلا ما هو الأصلح فيكون جميع ما يفعل أصلح، وإن كنا لا نعقل نحن له
Then some of them objected to this and said: He caused the child to die in his infancy because He knew that if he reached puberty he would disbelieve and mislead others and cause them to disbelieve, so He caused him to die in his infancy for the benefit of others. We said: This from you is an admission that He prevented what is more correct for the child, which is exposure to the highest station, for the sake of which He spared that adult. But He prevented this most beneficial thing because of the benefit of that other. And according to you, preventing benefit from one who has no offense to rectify another is injustice. This is verified by the fact that according to you, it is only obligatory for God Almighty to give what is best if giving it does not remove Him from deserving the attribute of wisdom. And causing this child to die for the benefit of that other prevents the benefit of exposure to the highest station for this child, and this removes Him from deserving the attribute of wisdom. So He should not deprive this child of the benefit of exposure to the highest station for the sake of the child's own benefit. And the outcome is that you are between two opposites: if you say his death in infancy is a benefit for that adult, it is a corruption for the child; and if you do not cause him to die until he reaches puberty, then there is the benefit of exposure for him and corruption for that other. So it does not remain free, however it is, from corruption and leaving a benefit. Then, does God Almighty know that Mani, Zoroaster, Mazdak, Musaylima, al-Muqanna', Babak, and before them Pharaoh, if they had reached puberty, would disbelieve and cause others to disbelieve? If they say no, they disbelieve by ignorantly attributing ignorance to their Lord. If they say yes, we say: Then why did God Almighty give them respite when He knew they would disbelieve and mislead people? Why did He not cause them to die for the benefit of the people? And this is something they cannot object to. Then when they were confused about this, some of them claimed: It is established to us that God Almighty does not do except what is best, so everything He does is best, even if we do not understand Him.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its refutation. The objector attempts to save the principle of al-aslah by arguing that causing the child's death was for the benefit of others (preventing him from leading others astray). The author refutes this by pointing out that this admission still violates the child's own best interest (exposure to the highest station). According to the opponent's own rules, God is only required to give what is best if doing so does not compromise His attribute of wisdom; but here, depriving the child of the higher station harms the child without his offense, which is unjust. Then the author reduces the opponent's position to a dilemma: either causing death benefits the adult but corrupts the child, or sparing the child benefits the child but corrupts the adult; in either case, there is corruption and abandonment of benefit. Next, the author uses historical examples (Mani, Zoroaster, etc.) to force the opponent to admit that God knew they would disbelieve and mislead others, yet He let them live. If the opponent says God did not know, that is blasphemy; if He knew, then why did He not kill them to prevent harm? The opponent is left confused. The final claim that everything God does is best regardless shows their desperation.

Correction: The Imam argues that this still violates the child's own best interest (exposure to the highest station) without any offense on the child's part, making it unjust according to the opponent's own criteria.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it ignores the child's own best interest and leads to absurd consequences when applied consistently.

God's Power to Prevent Disbelief by Early Death
Al-Nasafi argues that God can kill disbelievers before sin for their benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's argument that God can prevent disbelief by causing early death, and the opponent's dilemma.

Core Idea
God can prevent disbelief by early death, and that would be more beneficial for the disbeliever.

Al-Nasafi presents a powerful argument to demonstrate that God can do what is more beneficial for individuals. He uses a dilemma: either the opponent admits God's power to cause early death for those He knows will disbelieve, or they deny it and fall into error. This passage shows how the concept of 'al-aslah' (the more beneficial) is used in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two responses
Read the passage and identify the two possible responses Al-Nasafi expects from the opponent: 'No' or 'Yes'.
Notice the immediate consequences of each response.
2
Understand the implications
If the opponent says 'No', they declare God incapable. If 'Yes', they admit God's power over the more beneficial (al-aslah).
This is a classic dilemma in kalam.
3
Reflect on the evasion and final point
Consider Al-Nasafi's rebuttal of the opponent's possible evasion, and his additional point that God could simply not give intellects.
This reinforces the argument's strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : أليس أن الله تعالى كان قادرًا على أن يميت قبل البلوغ كل من يعلم منه الكفر بعد البلوغ؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد عجزوه، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أثبتوا له القدرة على الأصلح إذا كانت الإماتة أصلح له في هذه الحالة لما يحصل له بها النجاة من الخلود في أسفل دركات النيران، وإن عادوا إلى تجاهلهم أن المعترض لا على المنزلتين كان له أصلح - عدنا إلى ما أجبنا، وكذا الله تعالى قادر على أن لا يركب فيهم العقول.
Then we say to them: Is it not that Allah was able to cause to die before puberty everyone whom He knows will disbelieve after puberty? If they say: No. They have declared Him incapable. If they say: Yes. Then they have established for Him power over the more beneficial, since causing death is more beneficial for him in this state because it brings him salvation from eternal punishment in the lowest depths of hellfire. And if they return to their ignorance that the objector is not upon the two stations, it was more beneficial for him—we return to what we answered. Likewise, Allah is able not to give them intellects.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma to the opponent. Al-Nasafi asks: Does God have the power to cause the death of those He knows will disbelieve, before they reach the age of moral responsibility (bulugh)? If the opponent denies that power, they commit the error of declaring God incapable. If they affirm it, then they must admit that God has power to do what is more beneficial (al-aslah) for that person, because early death saves him from eternal hellfire. The opponent might try to evade by claiming that the objector (the person in question) is not in either of the two states, but that is dismissed. Al-Nasafi also adds that God could simply not give them intellects, avoiding their disbelief altogether.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causing early death is unjust because it deprives the person of life.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that early death is more beneficial for the person because it saves them from eternal hellfire, thus it is an act of mercy.

Checkpoint

A) They say 'Yes', resulting in admitting God's power over the more beneficial
B) They say 'No', resulting in declaring God incapable
C) They say 'We do not know'
D) They say 'It is not relevant'

Key Takeaway
God's power extends to doing what is more beneficial for individuals, such as preventing disbelief by early death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could end your life before you commit your next sin, would you welcome or fear that? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to this world versus your desire for salvation?
?

?
Al-Nasafi argues that God could also refrain from giving intellects to prevent disbelief. If you had the choice to be born without intellect to guarantee heaven, would you take it? What does your hesitation say about your pride in your own understanding?

Giving the Disbeliever Power to Disbelieve
Al-Nasafi argues it is not beneficial to give power to those who will misuse it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's argument that giving the disbeliever the power to disbelieve is not the most beneficial act from the perspective of divine optimality (al-aslah).

Core Idea
If God knows a person will disbelieve, giving them that power cannot be the most beneficial act.

In this passage, al-Nasafi continues the debate on divine optimality (al-aslah). He questions whether it is truly beneficial for God to grant the disbeliever the power to disbelieve, especially given His foreknowledge. Through a series of logical alternatives, al-Nasafi forces the opponent to confront the implications of their position. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Al-Nasafi starts by confirming that God gives the disbeliever the power (qudra) to disbelieve. This is the basis of the discussion.
2
Examine the two alternatives
He poses two options: either it is more beneficial not to give the power, or it is more beneficial to give it. Evaluate each.
3
Respond to the first option
If the opponent says it is better not to give, then God's actual act of giving shows He abandoned the optimal, which is problematic.
4
Consider the second option and the distinction of ta'rid
If the opponent says it is better to give despite foreknowledge, that is obstinate. The appeal to ta'rid (conditioning) is already refuted.
5
Address the responsibility argument
The opponent argues that without power there is no moral responsibility. Al-Nasafi counters that exiting responsibility is actually beneficial because it avoids eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن الله تعالى أعطى الكافر قدرة الكفر ؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل فأي الأمرين أصلح أن لا يعطى له القدرة إذا علم أنه يكفر أو أن يعطى القدرة؟ فإن قال: الأصلح أن لا يعطي له القدرة. قلنا : فإذا أعطى فقد ترك الأصلح، وكذا إذا كان قادرا أن لا يعطي فكان إذا قادرًا على أن يفعل به ما هو الأصلح ولم يفعل إذا أعطى القدرة. وإن قال: الأصلح له أن يعطى القدرة مع علمه أنه يكفر بها لا محالة، فقد كابر وعاند. وإن عادوا إلى فصل التعريض ، فقد سبق الكلام فيه . وإن قالوا: لو لم يعطه القدرة لم يبق مكلفا. قلنا وأي ضرر عليه بخروجه عن التكليف بل له فيه أعظم نفع حيث لم يستحق العذاب بالمؤبد.
Then we say: Is it not that Allah gave the disbeliever the power to disbelieve? There is no avoiding 'yes'. It was said: Which of the two matters is more beneficial: that He not give him the power if He knows he will disbelieve, or that He gives him the power? If he says: The more beneficial is that He not give him the power. We say: Then if He gives, He has abandoned the more beneficial. Likewise, since He is able not to give, then He is able to do what is more beneficial for him, but He did not do it when He gave the power. If he says: The more beneficial for him is to give him the power despite His knowledge that he will inevitably disbelieve with it, then he has acted obstinately and stubbornly. If they return to the distinction of the case (ta'rid), it has already been discussed. If they say: If He did not give him the power, he would not remain a responsible being (mukallaf). We say: What harm is there to him in exiting from responsibility? Rather, in that there is greater benefit for him, since he would not deserve eternal punishment.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage continues the debate on the optimal (al-aslah). Al-Nasafi puts forth a question: Does God give the disbeliever the power (qudra) to disbelieve? The answer is yes. He then asks which is more beneficial: to give that power or not, given that God knows the outcome. If the opponent says it is better not to give it, then God's act of giving it means He abandoned the optimal, which contradicts the belief that God always does what is most beneficial. If the opponent says it is better to give the power despite foreknowledge of disbelief, that is simply obstinate. If the opponent appeals to the necessity of moral responsibility (taklif) — without power, there is no responsibility — Al-Nasafi responds that exiting responsibility is actually a great benefit because it saves one from eternal punishment. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
قدرة الكفر
أصلح
تكليف
تعريض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral responsibility (taklif) is inherently good and should be preserved at all costs.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that exiting responsibility can be beneficial if it saves one from eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the response to the argument that without giving power the disbeliever would not be morally responsible (mukallaf)?
A) Responsibility is necessary for reward
B) Exiting responsibility is actually beneficial because it avoids eternal punishment
C) God's justice requires that everyone be tested
D) The disbeliever would still be responsible without power

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that giving power to a disbeliever cannot be the most beneficial act because it leads to eternal punishment, and even if it removes responsibility, that exit is actually beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you aware that every ability you have, including the ability to sin, is a gift from Allah? If Allah knows you will misuse an ability, would it be better for you if He withheld it? How does this change your attitude towards your own weaknesses and choices?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that exiting moral responsibility (taklif) is a great benefit because it avoids punishment. Honestly, would you rather have no accountability if it meant no risk of hellfire? Or do you see the risk as worth the potential reward? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom?

God's Power to Cause Death Before Disbelief
Argues that God could cause early death to prevent disbelief, proving power over al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a hypothetical question about God's power to cause early death to argue for the concept of al-aslah against the Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
God's power to cause early death before disbelief proves He also has power over what is most beneficial.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical argument with the Mu'tazilites over the concept of al-aslah (the most beneficial). He poses a simple question: could God have caused a potential disbeliever to die before reaching puberty, thus preventing his disbelief? Depending on the opponent's answer, either they admit God's incapability or they affirm His power over al-aslah. This short but sharp argument reveals a key Maturidi position on divine power and human destiny.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dialectical structure: a question, two possible answers, and a conclusion.
The argument is presented in the form of a dilemma.
2
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms 'al-aslah' (الأصلح) and 'imata' (الإماتة). Understand how they relate to God's power and human salvation.
Al-aslah is the Mu'tazilite concept that God must do what is most beneficial for His creatures.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the argument: If the opponent denies God's power, they attribute incapability to Him. If they affirm it, they must accept God's power over al-aslah. Then note the additional point about withholding intellects.
This is a typical kalam technique using a conditional question.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does early death count as 'more beneficial'? How does this argument undermine the Mu'tazilite insistence on al-aslah?
The benefit is salvation from eternal punishment, not worldly comfort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : أليس أن الله تعالى كان قادرًا على أن يميت قبل البلوغ كل من يعلم منه الكفر بعد البلوغ؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد عجزوه، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أثبتوا له القدرة على الأصلح إذا كانت الإماتة أصلح له في هذه الحالة لما يحصل له بها النجاة من الخلود في أسفل دركات النيران، وإن عادوا إلى تجاهلهم أن المعترض لا على المنزلتين كان له أصلح - عدنا إلى ما أجبنا، وكذا الله تعالى قادر على أن لا يركب فيهم العقول.
Then we say to them: Is it not that God Almighty was able to cause the death before puberty of everyone from whom He knows that [he] will disbelieve after puberty? If they say: No, they have rendered Him incapable. If they say: Yes, then they have affirmed for Him power over what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), since causing death would be more beneficial for him in this state, because it would obtain for him salvation from eternal abiding in the lowest depths of the Fire. And if they return to their ignorance that the objection is not that the two states have something most beneficial for him, we return to what we answered. Likewise, God Almighty is able not to instill intellects in them.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument against the Mu'tazilite concept of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial). The author asks whether God has the power to cause the death of a person before puberty, if He knows that person will disbelieve after puberty. If the opponent denies this, they are attributing incapability to God. If they affirm it, then they must admit that God has power over what is most beneficial for that person, because early death would save him from eternal punishment in Hell. The author then notes that if the opponent retreats to the claim that the objection is not about the two states having a most beneficial option, the author returns to his previous answer. He adds that God also has the power not to give intellects to people, implying that He could prevent disbelief by withholding rationality.

Key Terms

الإماتة
خلود
العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causing early death is always harmful and cannot be considered al-aslah.
Correction: The passage shows that early death can be most beneficial if it saves a person from eternal punishment in Hell.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would be the benefit of God causing early death to a potential disbeliever?
A) It would prevent him from committing sins.
B) It would save him from eternal punishment in Hell.
C) It would make him a believer.
D) It would increase his reward.

Key Takeaway
God's power to cause early death to prevent disbelief demonstrates that He has power over what is most beneficial for His creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God could have ended your life before your first sin, sparing you from the Fire. He did not. Does this truth stir gratitude for His patience or fear of His justice? How will you respond today?
?
If God can prevent disbelief by early death, why do you continue to disobey Him? Will you let this reminder break your complacency?
?
The passage says God could have withheld intellect entirely, yet you have a mind that can choose belief. Will you use it today to affirm His sovereignty over your life?

The Dilemma of al-Aṣlaḥ: Giving Power to a Known Disbeliever
Poses a dilemma against Mu'tazilite al-aṣlaḥ regarding granting power to a disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aṣlaḥ faces when a known disbeliever is given the power to disbelieve.

Core Idea
Granting power to a known disbeliever contradicts al-aṣlaḥ.

This passage presents a key argument against the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants. The author poses a dilemma: either God withholds the power of disbelief from a known disbeliever, which would be the most beneficial, or He grants it, which would contradict al-aṣlaḥ. The author also addresses the objection that withholding power nullifies moral obligation, showing that removal from obligation can itself be a greater benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the dialectical style: the author asks questions and anticipates responses.
The passage uses a series of conditional statements and expected replies.
2
Identify the core dilemma
The author asks: which is more beneficial — to give or not give the disbeliever power, given God knows he will disbelieve? Trace the two possible answers and their consequences.
The opponent's first answer leads to contradiction of al-aṣlaḥ; the second is labeled as obstinacy.
3
Examine the objection and response
The opponent argues that withholding power would remove moral obligation (taklīf). The author responds that removal from obligation is actually beneficial, saving the person from eternal punishment.
This turns the objection into a supporting point for the author's position.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument undermines the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is most beneficial. The passage shows that al-aṣlaḥ cannot be consistently applied to the act of granting power.
This is a classic theological dilemma against the Mu'tazilites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن الله تعالى أعطى الكافر قدرة الكفر ؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل فأي الأمرين أصلح أن لا يعطى له القدرة إذا علم أنه يكفر أو أن يعطى القدرة؟ فإن قال: الأصلح أن لا يعطي له القدرة. قلنا : فإذا أعطى فقد ترك الأصلح، وكذا إذا كان قادرا أن لا يعطي فكان إذا قادرًا على أن يفعل به ما هو الأصلح ولم يفعل إذا أعطى القدرة. وإن قال: الأصلح له أن يعطى القدرة مع علمه أنه يكفر بها لا محالة، فقد كابر وعاند. وإن عادوا إلى فصل التعريض ، فقد سبق الكلام فيه . وإن قالوا: لو لم يعطه القدرة لم يبق مكلفا. قلنا وأي ضرر عليه بخروجه عن التكليف بل له فيه أعظم نفع حيث لم يستحق العذاب بالمؤبد.
Then we say: Is it not that God Almighty gave the disbeliever the power of disbelief? There must be an affirmative. It was said: Which of the two matters is most beneficial: that He should not give him the power, when He knows that he will disbelieve, or that He should give him the power? If he says: The most beneficial is that He should not give him the power. We say: Then if He gave it, He has abandoned what is most beneficial. Likewise, if He is able not to give it, then He was able to do for him what is most beneficial, but He did not do it when He gave the power. And if he says: It is most beneficial for him to give him the power, despite His knowledge that he will inevitably disbelieve by it, then he has been obstinate and contentious. And if they return to the chapter of indirect expression, the speech about it has already preceded. And if they say: If He had not given him the power, he would not have remained obligated (mukallaf). We say: What harm is there upon him in his removal from obligation? Rather, there is for him the greatest benefit in it, since he would not deserve eternal punishment.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage continues the critique of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ. The author poses a dilemma: God gives the disbeliever the power (qudra) to perform disbelief, knowing he will use it. What is more beneficial: to withhold that power or to give it? If the opponent says withholding is better, then by giving it, God has abandoned what is most beneficial. If the opponent says giving is better despite certain disbelief, then he is being stubborn. The author also addresses the objection that withholding power would nullify moral obligation (taklīf). He responds that being released from obligation is actually beneficial, as it saves the person from eternal punishment. This argument undermines the Mu'tazilite claim that God always does what is most beneficial for His servants.

تكليف
كافر

Misconception: One might think that moral obligation (taklīf) is always beneficial, so removing it is harmful.
Correction: The passage shows that removal from obligation can be a greater benefit because it saves the person from eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the benefit of removing a person from moral obligation (taklīf)?
A) He can then disbelieve without punishment.
B) He would not deserve eternal punishment.
C) He would become a believer.
D) He would have more freedom.

Key Takeaway
God's granting power to a known disbeliever cannot be reconciled with the claim that He always does what is most beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Honestly search your heart: does knowing His foreknowledge make you more grateful for His mercy, or more careless in your actions? Commit today to use His gift of power only for obedience.

The Dilemma of Universal Belief and al-Aslah
Dialectical refutation of Mu'tazilite al-aslah via a dilemma about God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma used by Maturidi theologians to refute the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah regarding God's power to cause universal belief.

Core Idea
If God can cause universal belief, His not doing so challenges the Mu'tazilite notion of al-aslah.

This passage presents a sharp dialectical argument that turns the Mu'tazilite position on its head. It begins by asking whether God has the power to make all people believe, then forces the opponent into a choice between limiting God's power or admitting that He abandoned what is most beneficial. The argument culminates in a stark comparison between a small reward and eternal punishment, exposing an absurdity in the Mu'tazilite view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice how the author uses a series of conditional questions and responses.
2
Identify the two initial possibilities
The argument first asks whether God can cause universal belief. Identify the two possible answers and their immediate consequences.
The first answer (no) leads to accusing the opponent of kufr; the second (yes) leads to the main dilemma.
3
Analyze the objection and final dilemma
The opponent objects that causing belief would reduce reward. The author then presents a stark choice: is a small reward better than eternal punishment? Track both branches.
Both branches either concede the abandonment of al-aslah or lead to an absurd statement.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider why this argument is considered a successful refutation of the Mu'tazilite position. How does it force the opponent into an inescapable contradiction?
The principle of al-aslah is shown to be self-defeating if God could have made everyone believe but did not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : إذا كان قادرًا على ما لو فعل لكفر به الخلق فهل تصفون بأنه قادر على ما لو فعل لآمن به الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد قصروا قدرته على أحد الضدين وهو عندهم اضطرار والقول به كفر وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا : فإذا لم يفعل فقد ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم؟ فإن قالوا: لم يفعل ذلك لأنه لو فعل ثم آمنوا لكان ثواب إيمانهم أقل أو لم يكن لإيمانهم ثواب قلنا : ما قولكم أن القليل من الثواب على الإيمان أصلح لهم، (أم الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا : القليل من الثواب كان أصلح لهم. قد أقروا أنه ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم ) (وإن قالوا : الخلود في النار أصلح. فقد تجانفوا وجعلوا نفوسهم ضحكة للخلق.)
It was said: Since He is able to do that which, if He did it, the creatures would disbelieve, do you describe Him as able to do that which, if He did it, the creatures would believe? If they say: No, they have restricted His power to one of the two opposites, and in their view that is compulsion (iḍṭirār), and holding that is unbelief. If they say: Yes. We say: Then if He did not do it, He has abandoned what is most beneficial for them? If they say: He did not do it because if He had done it and then they believed, the reward of their faith would be less, or they would have no reward for their faith. We say: What do you say: is the small reward for faith more beneficial for them, or eternal abiding in the Fire? If they say: The small reward was more beneficial for them, then they have admitted that He abandoned what is most beneficial for them. If they say: Eternal abiding in the Fire is more beneficial, then they have deviated and made themselves a laughingstock for the people.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage continues the debate by pressing the Mu'tazilite on their own grounds. If God has power to cause universal disbelief (as shown by Quranic verses), then does He also have power to cause universal belief? If the opponent denies this, they restrict God's power to one opposite, which they themselves define as compulsion (iḍṭirār) and declare such a view as kufr. If they affirm, then God has not used that power, so He has abandoned what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for humanity—since causing universal belief would save everyone from Hell. The opponent may object that such belief would have less reward. The author responds by asking: is a small reward better than eternal punishment? If they say small reward is better, they concede that God abandoned what is most beneficial. If they say eternal punishment is better, they are absurd. Thus the Mu'tazilite position is refuted.

Key Terms
الأصلح
ثواب
خلود
اضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's not causing universal belief is due to lack of power.
Correction: The passage argues it is due to wisdom, not inability, and uses the dilemma to refute the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the author present to the opponent regarding God's power to cause universal belief?
A) Either God is not able, or He is able but did not act.
B) Either God is unjust, or He is limited.
C) Either reward is less, or punishment is greater.
D) Either God is compelled, or He is free.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah is refuted by the fact that God could cause universal belief but does not, and any objection leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazilites were trying to defend God's justice, yet their logic led to absurdity. Are there areas in your faith where you try too hard to rationalize God's actions, instead of simply submitting? What is one thing you can do today to practice tawakkul in a situation you don't understand?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Claim About Reward
The author questions the nature of reward and its timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite claim that God's reward provides additional assistance.

Core Idea
If reward helps, why withhold it until after deserving?

The author begins by quoting the Mu'tazilite position on reward and punishment. He then launches a dialectical attack, questioning the purpose of reward. This passage examines the contradiction in claiming that reward is a means of additional assistance while also being dependent on prior merit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the opening lines where the Mu'tazilite position is stated. Then the author declares it invalid.
Notice the dialectical style begins immediately.
2
Follow the two branches
The author poses a question about additional assistance. Consider both possible answers (yes and no) and their implications.
Each branch leads to a contradiction.
3
Apply to the disbeliever
The same logic is extended to the disbeliever. See how the argument concludes that the Mu'tazilite position is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثوبة للمؤمنين وبطريق العقوبة للكافرين. فاسد، لأنا نقول ما فعل من ذلك بطريق الثواب هل كان به فضل معونة على الخير والطاعة أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: ولم لم يعطه ذلك قبل استحقاقه بطريق الثواب وهو أنفع له وأصلح فقد منع الأصلح إذا قبل استجابة ذلك بفعله. وإن قالوا: ليس يحصل به فضل معونة. قيل: لا فائدة إذا به ولا منه، ولم يكن هو نعمة فلا معنى لجعله ثوابًا. وكذا يقال: هل هذا المعنى بحيث لو فعل الكافر لآمن؟ إن قالوا: لا. فإذا لا فائدة فيه فلا يصلح ثوابًا. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: فقد منع الكافر ذلك، فأما ما فعل بالكافر فنقول: أبقى الكفار بعد ما فعل بهم من الختم والطبع وما ذكرنا ممتحنين أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد جعلوهم فيما يعتقدونه ويفترون به على الله تعالى ويرتكبون من أنواع المعاصي معذورين، وجعلوا هذه الأفعال لهم غير جائزة المؤاخذة، والقول بذلك كفر وخروج أيضا عن إجماع أهل القبلة. وإن قالوا: هم بعد ما فعل بهم من الختم والطبع وغير ذلك ممتحنون، قلنا: فإذا كانوا ممتحنين فأي الأمرين أصلح لهم في الدين وجود هذه المعاني أم ارتفاعها؟ فإن قالوا: وجودها. جعلوا ما هو من الله تعالى عقوبة لهم من ختم القلوب وطبعها وجعل الغشاوة على أبصارهم أصلح لهم في الدين من تركها، وهذا جهل عظيم. وإن قالوا: عدمها. فقد أقروا أنه [فيا؟] تعالى يفعل بالممتحنين ما ليس بأصلح لهم في الدين وقد هدموا جميع قواعدهم. ثم يقال: وأي ضرر عليهم في الختم والطبع ولم يتألموا بهما في الدنيا بل انتقموا بهما حيث كانا أصلح لهم في أمر دينهم حتى ليعاقبهم الله بذلك على ارتكابهم معصية وكفرهم بآياته ولا ضرر عليهم في الآخرة بهذا الختم الموجود في الدنيا إذ صار أصلح في الدين، وما هو أصلح في الدين سبب نفع الآخرة، ولا جواب لهم عنه بأفصح من السكوت، والله الموفق.
Reward for the believers and in the way of punishment for the disbelievers. Invalid, for we say: what He did from that by way of reward, was there through it an additional assistance towards good and obedience or not? If they say: Yes. It is said: Why did He not give him that before his deserving it by way of reward, while it is more beneficial and more suitable for him, so He prevented the most suitable when [he] accepted the response by his action? And if they say: There is not obtained by it an additional assistance. It is said: There is no benefit in it then, and it is not a blessing, so there is no meaning to making it a reward. And likewise it is said: Is this meaning such that if He did it to the disbeliever, he would believe? If they say: No. Then there is no benefit in it, so it is not suitable as a reward. And if they say: Yes. It is said: So He prevented the disbeliever that. As for what He did to the disbeliever, we say: Did He leave the disbelievers, after what He did to them of sealing and impressing and what we mentioned, tested or not? If they say: No. Then they made them, in what they believe and fabricate against Allah and commit of types of sins, excused, and they made these actions not permissible for accountability, and the statement of that is disbelief and also a departure from the consensus of the people of the qibla. And if they say: They are, after what He did to them of sealing and impressing and other than that, tested. We say: So if they are tested, then which of the two matters is more suitable for them in religion: the existence of these meanings or their absence? If they say: Their existence. They made what is from Allah as a punishment for them, of sealing of hearts and impressing them and placing a covering on their eyes, more suitable for them in religion than leaving them, and this is great ignorance. And if they say: Their absence. Then they acknowledged that He [فيا؟] does with the tested what is not more suitable for them in religion, and they have demolished all their principles. Then it is said: And what harm is there upon them in the sealing and impressing, and they did not suffer pain from them in the world, but rather they benefited by them where they were more suitable for them in the matter of their religion, so that Allah may punish them thereby for their committing sin and their disbelief in His signs, and there is no harm upon them in the Hereafter from this seal found in the world, since it became more suitable in religion, and what is more suitable in religion is a cause of benefit in the Hereafter, and there is no answer for them about it more eloquent than silence. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position regarding reward and punishment and the concept of 'aslah' (most beneficial). The author begins by quoting the Mu'tazilite statement that reward is for believers and punishment is for disbelievers, and then declares it invalid. He then poses a series of dialectical questions. First, concerning the reward: if God's action of rewarding provides additional assistance towards good, then why did He not give it before the person earned it, as it would be more beneficial? If they say it does not provide assistance, then it has no benefit and cannot be a reward. Similarly, if the disbeliever would have believed if given that assistance, then God prevented him; if not, then it is useless. He then turns to the actions done to the disbeliever, such as sealing the heart. If they claim that after such actions the disbeliever is no longer tested, then they would be excused, which contradicts the consensus. If they claim they are still tested, then the author asks whether the presence or absence of these seals is more beneficial for their religion. Either answer leads to a contradiction: either they consider a punishment as beneficial, or they admit God does what is not beneficial. The author concludes that there is no harm in this seal, as it does not cause pain in this world and is a means to benefit in the Hereafter, and the Mu'tazilites have no answer but silence.

Key Terms
مثوبة
فضل معونة
أصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward as assistance is consistent with God's wisdom.
Correction: If reward assists, it should be given before deserving; if not, it is useless.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the problem if the Mu'tazilites claim that reward provides additional assistance?
A) Then the reward would be a punishment.
B) Then God should have given it before the person earned it, which He did not.
C) Then the disbeliever would not believe.
D) Then the reward is not a blessing.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite concept of reward as assistance is logically flawed because it either implies God prevented a greater benefit or the reward has no value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly trust that God's delay in rewarding you—or withholding what you think is best—is actually for your own good? What area of your life reveals impatience with His timing?
?
When you feel you have earned a good outcome, do you demand it immediately, or are you willing to wait? What does your reaction reveal about your belief in God's perfect wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted God must give the most beneficial at every moment. Have you ever been angry at God for not giving you something you thought was best? How does this passage challenge you to submit to His decree even when it seems less beneficial to you?

The Mu'tazilite Dilemma on Sealing Hearts and Accountability
The author presents a dilemma regarding the seal on hearts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position on whether the seal on hearts removes accountability.

Core Idea
If the seal excuses, it negates accountability; if tested, the seal cannot be most beneficial.

The author now turns to the Mu'tazilite claim about God's actions on disbelievers, such as sealing hearts. He presents a dilemma: either the disbelievers remain tested or they are excused. Both options lead to conclusions that contradict Mu'tazilite principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
The author asks whether disbelievers remain tested after sealing. Read carefully the two options.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
2
Analyze the 'not tested' option
If they say not tested, then disbelievers are excused, which is disbelief. Understand why.
This contradicts Islamic consensus.
3
Analyze the 'tested' option
If tested, the author asks which is more beneficial. Both answers undermine the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah.
Either they call punishment beneficial or deny God does what is best.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثوبة للمؤمنين وبطريق العقوبة للكافرين. فاسد، لأنا نقول ما فعل من ذلك بطريق الثواب هل كان به فضل معونة على الخير والطاعة أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: ولم لم يعطه ذلك قبل استحقاقه بطريق الثواب وهو أنفع له وأصلح فقد منع الأصلح إذا قبل استجابة ذلك بفعله. وإن قالوا: ليس يحصل به فضل معونة. قيل: لا فائدة إذا به ولا منه، ولم يكن هو نعمة فلا معنى لجعله ثوابًا. وكذا يقال: هل هذا المعنى بحيث لو فعل الكافر لآمن؟ إن قالوا: لا. فإذا لا فائدة فيه فلا يصلح ثوابًا. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: فقد منع الكافر ذلك، فأما ما فعل بالكافر فنقول: أبقى الكفار بعد ما فعل بهم من الختم والطبع وما ذكرنا ممتحنين أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد جعلوهم فيما يعتقدونه ويفترون به على الله تعالى ويرتكبون من أنواع المعاصي معذورين، وجعلوا هذه الأفعال لهم غير جائزة المؤاخذة، والقول بذلك كفر وخروج أيضا عن إجماع أهل القبلة. وإن قالوا: هم بعد ما فعل بهم من الختم والطبع وغير ذلك ممتحنون، قلنا: فإذا كانوا ممتحنين فأي الأمرين أصلح لهم في الدين وجود هذه المعاني أم ارتفاعها؟ فإن قالوا: وجودها. جعلوا ما هو من الله تعالى عقوبة لهم من ختم القلوب وطبعها وجعل الغشاوة على أبصارهم أصلح لهم في الدين من تركها، وهذا جهل عظيم. وإن قالوا: عدمها. فقد أقروا أنه [فيا؟] تعالى يفعل بالممتحنين ما ليس بأصلح لهم في الدين وقد هدموا جميع قواعدهم. ثم يقال: وأي ضرر عليهم في الختم والطبع ولم يتألموا بهما في الدنيا بل انتقموا بهما حيث كانا أصلح لهم في أمر دينهم حتى ليعاقبهم الله بذلك على ارتكابهم معصية وكفرهم بآياته ولا ضرر عليهم في الآخرة بهذا الختم الموجود في الدنيا إذ صار أصلح في الدين، وما هو أصلح في الدين سبب نفع الآخرة، ولا جواب لهم عنه بأفصح من السكوت، والله الموفق.

?

Will you take one concrete step today to soften your heart toward guidance?
?

?
The Mu'tazilites wanted to defend God's justice by saying He always does what is best. But here, the seal appears as a punishment. If you were to blame Allah for your spiritual struggles, what would this argument say to you? What would it mean for your trust in His wisdom if you believed He does not always do what is best for you? Are you willing to submit to a wisdom that sometimes withholds what seems beneficial?

The Harmlessness of Sealing and the Mu'tazilite Silence
The author argues the seal is harmless and beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author concludes that the seal on hearts is not harmful and that the Mu'tazilites have no response.

Core Idea
Sealing causes no pain in this world and leads to benefit in the Hereafter.

After demolishing the Mu'tazilite position, the author argues that the seal on hearts is actually beneficial for the disbelievers' religion and does not cause any harm. He concludes with a rhetorical flourish that the Mu'tazilites have no answer but silence.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding argument
Read the passage where the author argues that sealing causes no harm in this world and is beneficial for religion.
2
Reflect on the benefit
Consider how the seal is said to be 'more suitable' for religion and a cause of benefit in the Hereafter.
This turns the Mu'tazilite objection on its head.
3
Note the silence
The author claims the Mu'tazilites have no answer but silence. Think about why this is a strong rhetorical conclusion.

Source Text

Refutation of Equal Intellects and Divine Favor
Refutation of two Mu'tazilite doctrines via empirical and logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses empirical observation and logical consistency to refute Mu'tazilite doctrines of equal intellects and obligatory equal treatment.

Core Idea
God's favor is proven by observable differences in intellect; fairness does not mean uniformity.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God must treat all people equally in intellect and favor, otherwise He would be unjust. The author counters by pointing to the evident variation in human mental abilities, showing that equality is not a divine requirement. He then extends the argument to show that the Mu'tazilite position leads to absurdities and contradicts common sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full argument's flow.
Notice the author's rhetorical style: he uses empirical examples and reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the two claims being refuted
The passage refutes: (1) that all intellects are equal and (2) that Allah cannot single out with mercy unless deserved. See how the author transitions from one to the other.
The second claim is introduced with 'ثم لما كان'.
3
Examine the author's evidence
The author uses observable differences in intelligence and learning speed to prove variation. He then argues that denying this would also allow denying obvious physical differences, leading to absurdity.
This is a classic use of empirical evidence and logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أفضى بهم تماديهم في الضلال إلى أن زعموا أن الله تعالى لم يفضل أحد على أحد بإعطاء زيادة العقل وعقول الناس كلها في الخلقة مستوية، مع ما يرى ويعاين من وفور عقول بعض الناس وجودة قرائحهم وقوة أذهانهم، وقلة عقول البعض وبلادة أفهامهم وكلال خواطرهم، بحيث يحرز واحد من أنواع العلوم وفنون الآداب وودائع العقول وخزائن الأفهام في مدة لا يقدر من وقع منه في الطرف الأقصى من البلادة والغباوة على إحراز جزء من أضعاف ألف جزء وفي تلك المدة، ولو لم يكن هذا تجاهلاً فلا تجاهل في العالم، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز لآخر أن يقول: إن الله تعالى سوى بين الناس في الحسن والدمامة والطول وقصر القامة وقوة البطش ورباطة الجأش والضعف والجبن وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى كثرة، وكل ذلك تجاهل، ومذهب يفضي بصاحبه إلى ارتكاب مثل هذه المحالات ويبدو عواره للبداهة كل هذا الظهور، فكان حقيقا على العاقل أن يستعيذ بالله تعالى منه ويسأل العصمة عن الوقوع فيه وفي أمثاله. ثم لما كان من مذهبهم أن ليس الله تعالى أن يختص برحمته ونعمته وتوفيقه وإصلاحه من يشاء بل الواجب عليه أن يسوي بين كل من عباده في ذلك إلا ما استحقه أحد بطاعته، فكذلك ليس له أن يخص واحدًا منهم بما لم يفعل بغيره مثله إلا إذا كان ذلك أصلح له في الدين أو استحقه بمعصيته فأما لا على ذلك الوجه فهو
Then their persistence in misguidance led them to claim that Allah did not favor anyone over another by giving an increase of intellect, and that the intellects of all people in creation are equal, despite what is seen and observed of the abundance of intellects of some people, the excellence of their minds, the strength of their understandings, and the scarcity of intellects of others, the dullness of their comprehensions, and the weakness of their thoughts, to the extent that one person acquires many types of sciences, arts of literature, deposits of intellects, and treasuries of understandings in a duration in which another, who is at the extreme end of dullness and stupidity, is unable to acquire even a part of a thousandth part in that same duration. And if this were not ignorance, then there is no ignorance in the world. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible for another to say: Indeed Allah made equal among people in beauty and ugliness, tallness and shortness of stature, strength of assault and firmness of composure, weakness and cowardice, and other things which cannot be counted. And all of that is ignorance, and a school of thought that leads its adherent to committing such impossibilities, and its flaw appears to common sense with all this clarity. So it is befitting for the rational person to seek refuge with Allah from it and ask for protection from falling into it and its likes. Then since it is from their doctrine that Allah is not to single out with His mercy, His grace, His success-giving, and His reform whom He wills, but rather it is obligatory upon Him to make equal among all His servants in that, except what someone deserves by his obedience, likewise He is not to single out anyone of them with what He has not done to another like him, unless that is more suitable for him in religion or he deserves it by his disobedience. But if not in that way, then it is
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage refutes two related Mu'tazilite doctrines: the equality of all intellects and the denial of divine privilege. The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazilites went so far as to claim that God does not favor anyone with increased intellect and that all human intellects are equal in creation. He then presents empirical evidence to the contrary: people obviously vary in mental capacity, intelligence, quickness of understanding, etc. He uses a hyperbolic example: one person can learn many sciences in a certain time while another cannot even learn a fraction of that. He argues that to deny this difference is sheer ignorance, and if one denies it, one could also deny obvious physical differences like beauty, height, strength, etc. This school of thought leads to absurdities. The author then extends the argument to divine favour generally: Mu'tazilites believe God must treat all equally unless someone deserves by obedience, but then they also must deny that God can single out anyone with something not given to others unless it is for the benefit of religion or as punishment. The passage ends abruptly, but the implication is that the Mu'tazilite position is inconsistent with observable reality and leads to unacceptable conclusions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that God's justice requires distributing equal intellect to all people.
Correction: The author shows that observable variation in intellect is a clear fact, and denying it leads to absurd conclusions. God's favor is not bound by human notions of equality.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author use to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that all intellects are equal?
A) The consensus of scholars.
B) The observable differences in people's mental abilities.
C) A verse from the Quran.
D) The fact that all people are the same in physical appearance.

Key Takeaway
Observable variation in intellect and other traits proves that God's favor is not uniform; the Mu'tazilite position is refuted by both observation and logical consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt resentment towards Allah because someone else was given more intelligence, beauty, or wealth than you? How does this passage confront that feeling, and what will you do the next time such envy arises?
?
The Mu'tazilites demanded that God treat everyone equally according to their own standard of fairness. Where in your life have you insisted on your own idea of fairness rather than submitting to Allah's wisdom? What step can you take today to surrender that demand and accept His decree with contentment?

Divine Decree

The Absurd Consequence of Obligatory Compensation for Children's Pain
First part: reductio ad absurdum for children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili doctrine of obligatory compensation for children's pain leads to an absurd conclusion that contradicts observed reality.

Core Idea
If pain always brings compensation, children would be in constant agony.

The author of the Tabṣira presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilī claim that God must compensate every pain. If pain were always beneficial and compensated, then the most 'beneficial' thing would be to inflict continual pain on children for eternal reward, but reality shows otherwise. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if ... then ... but we see ... therefore false.'
Notice the footnote markers (3) and (4); they indicate additional commentary in the original text.
2
Understand the reductio
The author assumes the Mu'tazili premise (pain leads to compensation with no harm) and draws a necessary conclusion: it would be most beneficial for God to inflict constant pain on children. Then he checks reality and finds the opposite, disproving the premise.
This is a classic logical fallacy refutation.
3
Reflect on the empirical check
The author relies on observable evidence: children are not in constant pain. Ask yourself: why is this a strong argument?
It ties theological reasoning to empirical reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لو كان الإيلام (۳) سببًا لنيل العوض ولا مفسدة فيه للصغير لما أنه لا يعقل ليصير عاصيًا بالجزع وترك الصبر لكان الأصلح لكل صغير أن لا يخليه الله تعالى في ساعة من الساعات عن أنواع الآلام والأوجاع لينال بذلك الأعواض الخطيرة والأبدال العظيمة النفيسة في دار الآخرة، وحيث رأينا أن ذلك غير ثابت، ونرى الأطفال بضد هذه الحالة علمنا أن ما قالوا من استيجاب العوض أو (٤) من وجوب الأصلح باطل. ثم من زعمهم أنه لو فعل ذلك ببالغ لم توجد منه جناية يصير ذلك عقوبة عليها استوجب هذا () البالغ أيضا العوض. ومن مذهبهم أن هذا هو الأصلح لهذا البالغ، وإذا كان كذلك وكان الله بذلك فاعلاً لمصلحة العبد فصار عاملاً له فكيف يلزمه العوض دون أن يستوجب به العوض على عرف العقول وصنيع الأطباء من (٢) الشاهد، والله الموفق.
Then we say: If pain were a cause for obtaining compensation and there is no harm in it for the child—since he does not become a sinner by panic and lack of patience—then it would be most beneficial for every child that God, the Exalted, would not leave him for a single moment from types of pains and agonies so that he might attain thereby the great compensations and the precious, valuable substitutes in the abode of the Hereafter. But since we see that this is not established, and we see children in the opposite of this state, we know that what they said about the necessity of compensation or the obligation of the most beneficial is false. Then among their claims is that if He did that to an adult with no crime committed by him, it would become a punishment, and that adult would also deserve compensation. And according to their doctrine, this is the most beneficial for this adult. And if it is so, and God acts thereby for the benefit of the servant, He becomes acting for him—so how can compensation be incumbent upon Him without the adult deserving compensation thereby according to the custom of intellects and the practice of physicians in the witnessed world? And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the Mu'tazili doctrines of obligatory compensation (iwad) and the most beneficial (al-aslah). The author presents a reductio ad absurdum: if pain were always compensated and carried no harm for a child (since he does not sin by impatience), then it would be most beneficial for God to inflict continuous pain on every child to earn immense eternal rewards. However, empirical observation shows children are not in such a state; they are often healthy and free from constant pain. Therefore, the doctrines are false. Next, the author addresses a similar claim regarding adults: if God inflicted pain on an adult without any crime, the opponent says it would still be compensation and beneficial. The author counters by noting that if God acts purely for the servant's benefit, then the servant becomes the one doing the action (so God is acting as his agent), and in worldly custom, one who acts for another does not owe compensation; rather, the beneficiary deserves compensation. Thus, the opponent's position is inconsistent. The author ends with 'And God is the Giver of success' as a pious conclusion.

Key Terms
إيلام
عوض
أصلح
مفسدة
استيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to always provide the most beneficial for His creatures, including compensating every pain.
Correction: If true, God would inflict constant pain on children for maximum reward, but they are not in pain, so the doctrine is false.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the doctrines of obligatory compensation and the most beneficial are false?
A) Because children do not feel pain.
B) Because if true, God would constantly inflict pain on children for more reward, but we see children healthy.
C) Because adults always deserve punishment.
D) Because God never benefits His servants.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili doctrine of obligatory compensation for children's pain is refuted by the empirical fact that children are not in constant pain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you believe that every pain you have ever endured must be compensated by God? If not, what does that imply about your trust in His wisdom when you experience suffering that seems pointless or unjust?
?
When you see a child in pain, do you instinctively think that God is obliged to make it up to them? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and how should it reshape your attitude toward the suffering of the innocent?
?
If you face intense suffering and are tempted to panic or lose patience, what does this lesson teach you about the nature of divine justice? Are you willing to accept that some suffering may have no immediate compensation, yet still hold firmly to God's perfect justice?

The Adult Case: Contradiction in the Doctrine of Compensation
Second part: counterargument for adults

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazili claim that inflicting pain on an adult without crime still requires compensation from God.

Core Idea
If God acts for the servant's benefit, the servant deserves compensation, not God.

The author now addresses a related claim: if God inflicts pain on an adult without any crime, the Mu'tazila say it is still the most beneficial and deserving of compensation. The author counters with a logical inconsistency drawn from worldly custom: if God is acting as the servant's agent for his benefit, then the servant should be the one compensated, not God. This lesson unpacks that counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the adult case. Notice the shift from children to adults.
The passage uses a rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The Mu'tazili claim is: if God inflicts pain on an adult without any crime, it is still beneficial and the adult deserves compensation.
3
Understand the author's counter
The author argues that if God acts for the servant's benefit, He becomes the servant's agent. In worldly custom, an agent does not owe compensation; rather, the beneficiary (the servant) deserves compensation. This contradicts the opponent's position.
The analogy with physicians highlights that a doctor who benefits a patient does not owe the patient compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to compensate adults for pain even if they committed no sin, because it is the most beneficial.
Correction: If God acts for the servant's benefit, He is like an agent; in custom, the agent does not owe compensation to the beneficiary.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the Mu'tazili claim about adult compensation contradictory?
A) Because adults always sin.
B) Because if God acts for the servant's benefit, He becomes the servant's agent, and an agent does not owe compensation.
C) Because pain is never beneficial.
D) Because the adult would become an agent.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of obligatory compensation for adult pain is inconsistent because if God acts for the servant's benefit, He is the servant's agent and does not owe compensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God owes you something after a difficult trial? This lesson shows that if God is acting for your benefit, He is your agent, not your debtor. In what specific area of your life do you need to replace a sense of entitlement with gratitude?
?
Do you truly believe that every painful experience from God is for your ultimate good, even when you can't see how? What would it look like to actively trust that in your current struggles?
?
If God is acting as your agent for your benefit, what does that mean for how you approach Him in prayer? Are you treating Him as a Master who owes you nothing but gives everything, or as a servant you expect to compensate you? What needs to change in your duʿā' and your heart?
